Refutation of the Denial of Color's Reality
Refutes the denial of color's reality with counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument for the objective reality of color and how it refutes the reductionist view that color is merely an effect of light.

Core Idea
Color has its own reality beyond physical causes like light mixing.

In this passage, the author challenges the claim that color is an illusion arising from light interactions. He presents counterexamples showing that whiteness can occur independently, and he critiques the selective denial of whiteness versus blackness. This lesson unpacks the argument and its implications for the objective reality of sensible qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing view
Read the passage and note the claim that color has no reality; whiteness is light mixing, blackness is lack of light penetration.
The opponents give examples like snow, foam, crystal for whiteness, and wet cloth for blackness.
2
Note the author's initial response
The author says this is 'some of the causes', not the whole truth.
This opens the door for alternative explanations.
3
Examine the counterexamples
The author provides examples of whiteness without light mixing: boiled eggs, virgin milk, and plaster.
These are meant to refute the claim that whiteness is solely due to light.
4
Understand the critique of selective denial
Some opponents deny only whiteness because it can shed and accept other colors, but the author finds this weak because blackness is also stable.
This shows inconsistency in the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
لا حقيقة للون عند بعض الناس
قال: المبحث الثاني: من الناس من زعم أنه لا حقيقة للون وإنما يتخيل أن البياض من مخالطة الضوء للأجسام الشفافة كما في الثلج، والزبد ومسحوق البللور والزجاج والسواد من عدم غور الضوء في الجسم ولهذا ينسب إلى الماء حيث يخرج الهواء فلا يكمل نفوذ الضوء على ما يشاهد في الثوب المبلول والبواقي من اختلاف في الأمرين.
والحق أن هذا بعض أسباب الحصول على ما قال ابن سينا شك في حدوث البياض بما ذكر، لكنا ندعي حدوثه بغيره كما في البيض المسلوق ولبن العذراء والجص، واقتصصر بعضهم على نفي البياض لما أنه ينسلخ ويقبل محله الألوان، بخلاف السواد وضعفه ظاهر.
The second investigation: There is no reality for color according to some people. He said: The second investigation: Some people claim that color has no reality, rather it is imagined that whiteness arises from the mixing of light with transparent bodies, as in snow, foam, powdered crystal and glass, and blackness from the lack of penetration of light into the body, and for this it is attributed to water when air escapes so light does not fully penetrate, as seen in wet cloth and the like, with variation in the two matters. But the truth is that this is some of the causes. Regarding what Ibn Sina said: doubt about the occurrence of whiteness by what was mentioned, but we claim its occurrence by other than that, as in boiled eggs, virgin milk, and plaster. Some restricted themselves to denying whiteness because it sheds and its substrate accepts colors, unlike blackness and its weakness is apparent.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the reality of color, reducing it to physical phenomena. The author first states the position: some claim color has no reality; whiteness is merely light mixing with transparent bodies (snow, foam, crystal, glass), and blackness is the absence of light penetration (as in wet cloth). Then the author counters: this is only some causes. He mentions Ibn Sina's doubt about whiteness arising solely from light mixing, but the author asserts that whiteness can occur independently, e.g., in boiled eggs, virgin milk (likely a type of lime?), and gypsum. Furthermore, some critics restrict their denial to whiteness alone, arguing that whiteness can shed and the substrate can accept other colors, whereas blackness is more stable and its weakness is obvious. The implication is that whiteness has its own reality beyond mere optical effects.

The Reality of Color: Refuting Denial
Refutation of two views denying color's reality and affirmation of colors as real qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) affirm the reality of color, refuting those who claim it is merely an illusion from light and air, and why physical causes do not negate reality.

Core Idea
Colors are real qualities, not illusions; their occurrence through physical causes does not negate their reality.

This passage confronts a philosophical challenge: if color can be explained by light and air, is it real? The author presents and refutes two arguments denying the reality of color, culminating in the investigators' affirmation that colors are real qualities. By examining examples like a boiled egg and a chemical preparation, we see that whiteness can arise from causes other than light mixing with air.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two false claims
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Note the two different claims that deny the reality of color. The first attributes whiteness to light mixing with transparent bodies; the second attributes it to air in small particles.
The author presents these as arguments to be refuted.
2
Observe the refutation pattern
The author refutes each claim by providing counterexamples where whiteness occurs without the claimed cause. For the first claim, examples are boiled egg, virgin's milk, and plaster. For the second, the same examples are used alongside a quote from Ibn Sina.
Notice that the refutation does not deny the cause's effectiveness, but its exclusivity.
3
Understand the investigators' positive position
The passage concludes that colors are real qualities (kayfiyyat mutahaqqiqa), not imagined. Their appearance via specific causes does not contradict their reality, and they can arise from other causes.
This is the core theological-philosophical stance.
4
Reflect on the principle: causation vs. illusion
Consider why the author emphasizes that a quality can be real even if it has a physical explanation. This guards against the misconception that physical causes make something unreal.
This connects to broader kalam discussions about the reality of sensible qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: من الناس من زعم أنه لا حقيقة للون وإنما يتخيل البياض من مخالطة الضوء للأجسام الشفافة كما في الثلج والزبد ومسحوق البللور والزجاج، والسواد من عدم غور الضوء في الجسم ولهذا ينسب إلى الماء حيث يخرج الهواء فلا يكمل نفوذ الضوء على ما يشاهد في الثوب المبلول، والبواقي من اختلاف في الأمرين. والحق أن هذا بعض أسباب الحصول على ما قال ابن سينا لا شك في حدوث البياض بما ذكر، لكنا ندعي حدوثه بغيره كما في البيض المسلوق ولبن العذراء والجص، واقتصار بعضهم على نفي البياض لما أنه ينسلخ ويقبل محله الألوان، بخلاف السواد وضعفه ظاهر. سبب: زعم بعضهم أنه لا حقيقة للون أصلا، والبياض إنما يتخيل من الهواء للأجسام الشفافة المتصغرة جداً، كما في الثلج فإنه لا مخالطة الهواء ونفوذ الضوء في أجزاء صغار جهدية شفافة، وكذا في زبد الماء والمسحوق من البلور والزجاج الصافي، والسواد يتخيل من عدم غور الضوء في الجسم لكثافته واندماج أجزائه، وباقي الألوان تتخيل اختلاف الشفيف وتفاوت مخالطة الهواء. وقد يسند السواد إلى الماء بحسب النظر إلى أنه يخرج الهواء فلا يكمل نفوذ الضوء إلى السطوح، ولهذا يميل الثوب المبلول إلى السواد. والمحققون على أنها كيفيات متحققة لا متخيلة، وظهورها في الصور المذكورة بالأسباب المذكورة لا ينافي تحققها، ولا حدوثها بأسباب أخر. على ما قال ابن سينا: إنه لا شك في أن اختلاط الهواء بالمشف سبب لظهور اللون الأبيض، ولكنا ندعي أن البياض قد يحدث من غير هذا الوجه كما في البيض المسلوق فإنه يصير أشد بياضاً مع أن النار لم تحدث فيه تخلخلاً وهوائية، بل أخرجت الهوائية عنه، ولهذا صار أثقل، وكما في الدواء المسمى بلبن العذراء فإنه يكون من خل طبخ فيه المرداسنج حتى انحل فيه ثم يصفى حتى يبقى الخل في غاية الإشفاف ثم يطبخ المرداسنج في ماء طبخ فيه القلى، ويبالغ في تصفيته ثم يخلط الماءان فينعقد فيه المنحل الشفاف من المرداسنج، ويصير في غاية البياض ثم يجف.
The second research topic: Some people claimed that there is no reality for color, and that whiteness is only imagined from the mixture of light with transparent bodies, as in snow, froth, and powdered crystal and glass; and blackness from the lack of penetration of light into the body, and for this reason it is attributed to water when air escapes so the penetration of light is not complete, as is observed in a wet garment; and the remaining [colors] from a difference in the two matters. The truth is that this is one of the causes of occurrence according to what Ibn Sina said—there is no doubt about whiteness occurring by what was mentioned—but we claim its occurrence by other than that, as in a boiled egg, virgin's milk, and plaster. And some restricted themselves to denying whiteness because it peels off and its place accepts colors, unlike blackness, and its weakness is apparent. A cause: Some claimed that there is no reality for color at all, and that whiteness is only imagined from air for very small transparent bodies, as in snow, for it has mixture of air and penetration of light in small, [جهدية] transparent parts, and likewise in water froth and the powder of crystal and clear glass; and blackness is imagined from the lack of penetration of light into the body due to its density and the fusion of its parts; and the remaining colors are imagined from the difference of transparency and the variation of mixture of air. And blackness may be attributed to water according to the view that it expels air so the penetration of light to the surfaces is not complete, and for this reason a wet garment inclines to blackness. The investigators [hold] that they are real qualities, not imagined, and their appearance in the mentioned forms by the mentioned causes does not contradict their reality, nor their occurrence by other causes. According to what Ibn Sina said: There is no doubt that the mixture of air with the transparent is a cause for the appearance of white color, but we claim that whiteness may occur from other than this aspect, as in a boiled egg—it becomes more white despite the fact that fire did not cause rarefaction and airiness in it, rather it expelled the airiness from it, for this reason it became heavier—and as in the medicine called virgin's milk: it is made from vinegar in which [silver slag] (murdasanj) is cooked until it dissolves, then it is filtered until the vinegar is extremely clear, then the [silver slag] is cooked in water in which alkali has been cooked, and it is thoroughly filtered, then the two waters are mixed, and the transparent dissolved [silver slag] congeals in it and becomes extremely white, then it dries.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the reality of color. The author presents two false claims and refutes each. First, some claim that whiteness is merely an illusion from the mixture of light with transparent bodies, and blackness from lack of light penetration. The author responds that while these are indeed causes, they are not the only causes; whiteness can occur from other processes, as exemplified by a boiled egg (which becomes whiter without such mixture) and virgin's milk (a chemical preparation that produces whiteness). Second, another claim attributes whiteness to mixture with air in very small transparent particles. The author uses the same refutation, citing Ibn Sina's admission that air mixture is a cause but not necessary, and providing the same examples. The main point of the investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) is that colors are real qualities (kayfiyyat mutahaqqiqa), not mere illusions. Their appearance via certain causes does not negate their reality, nor does it preclude their occurrence from other causes. The passage follows an argumentative order: statement of false view, then refutation, then another false view, then refutation again, closing with the affirmative position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Colors are mere illusions caused by light and air; they have no independent reality.
Correction: Colors are real qualities (kayfiyyat mutahaqqiqa) that can be produced by various causes, including light, air, or other processes like boiling or chemical reactions. Their dependence on causes does not make them illusory.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Colors are real qualities; their occurrence through physical causes does not make them illusory, as demonstrated by counterexamples of whiteness arising from non-light/air causes.

Primary Colors Debate and Epistemic Limitations
Stating the debate on whether black and white are the only primary colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on primary colors in kalam and why empirical observation is insufficient for universal judgments.

Core Idea
Observation of particulars cannot yield universal truths; rational demonstration is essential in kalam.

In this lesson, we examine a classic debate among kalam scholars about which colors are primary. Some argue that only black and white are origins, while others add red, yellow, and green. The passage highlights a crucial methodological point: empirical observation of a few cases does not provide a universal judgment. This reflects the Ash‘arī insistence on rational demonstration for certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
2
Identify the Two Views
Find the two opinions on primary colors: (1) black and white only, (2) also red, yellow, and green. Note that the rest are claimed to be composites.
The second view relies on observation of some instances.
3
Understand the Author's Critique
The author states that such observation does not yield a universal judgment. Ask: why is observation insufficient?
This highlights the need for rational demonstration, not just empirical evidence.
4
Reflect on the Methodological Principle
Reflect on the epistemological principle: universal judgments in kalam require rational proof, not just particular observations.
This is a key distinction from purely empirical approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السواد والبياض أصل الألوان قال: وقيل : الأصل هو البياض والسواد ، وقيل : والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة أيضاً، والبواقي بالتركيب تعويلاً (۳) على مشاهدة ذلك في بعض الصور، ولا يخفى أنها لا تفيد الحكم الكلي).
Blackness and whiteness are the origin of colors. He said: It is said: the origin is whiteness and blackness. And it is said: also redness, yellowness, and greenness are origins, and the rest are by composition, relying on observation of that in some cases. It is not hidden that they do not provide a universal judgment.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage summarizes a debate about the classification of primary colors. Some hold that black and white are the only origins; others add red, yellow, and green, with remaining colors being composites. The argument relies on observation of particular cases. The author then notes a key epistemological limitation: such empirical observation cannot yield a universal judgment (ḥukm kullī). This reflects the Ash‘arī emphasis on the insufficiency of empirical generalization without rational demonstration, a crucial point in kalam methodology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing that some colors are primary in a few examples proves they are primary universally.
Correction: Observation only provides particular cases; a universal judgment requires rational demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Empirical observation alone cannot establish universal judgments; rational demonstration is required in kalam.

Types of Light: Essential and Accidental
Defines light and distinguishes essential/accidental, clarifies darkness as privation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and accidental light, and the nature of darkness as privation rather than a positive quality.

Core Idea
Light is either self-subsistent or derived; darkness is simply the absence of light.

In the third section of his work, Sanusi lays down a precise definition of light, dividing it into essential and accidental types. He also clarifies that darkness is not a positive quality but a privation, using a rational argument and a Quranic reference. This foundational distinction is essential for understanding later discussions on vision and the nature of divine light.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the terms ضياء and نور are distinguished.
2
Identify the two categories of light
Find the division into essential (ذاتي) and accidental (عرضي), and note the terms used for each (ضياء and نور).
Essential light comes from itself, like the sun.
3
Understand the nature of accidental light
See how accidental light is further divided into first, second, third, etc. depending on how many reflections it goes through.
Examples given: light directly from the sun vs. reflected.
4
Analyze the definition of darkness
Read the argument that darkness is a privation (عدم ملكة), not a positive quality. Consider the cave example.
Also note the Quranic verse and the final definition of light as a primary perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث الضوء قال : المبحث الثالث: الضوء ذاتي إن كان من ذات المحل كما للشمس. ويسمى ضياء، وإلا فعرضي كما للقمر يسمى نوراً، وهو إن حصل من الضوء لذاته فأول كضوء ما يقابل الشمس، وإلا فشان وثالث وهلم جراً كضوء وجه الأرض قبل الطلوع ، وضوء داخل البيت من الدار وهكذا إلى أن ينعدم وهو الظملة فهي عدم ملكة له لا كيفية وجودية، وإلا لكان مانعاً للجالس في الغار من إبصار الخارج، كالعكس للقطع بعدم الفرق في الحائل ما يحيط بالرائي أو بالمرئي وليست عدماً صرف التنافي المجعولية من قوله تعالى : وجعل الظلمات والنور غني عن التعريف كسائر المحسوسات وتعريفه بأنه كيفية هي كمال أول
The third section: Light. He said: The third section: Light is essential if it is from the substance itself, as for the sun, and is called diyā'. Otherwise it is accidental, as for the moon, and is called nūr. And it, if obtained from light for itself, then first, like light opposite the sun; otherwise, second, third, and so on, like light on the earth's surface before sunrise, and light inside a room of a house, and so on until it disappears, and that is darkness. So it (darkness) is a privation of it (light), not a positive quality; otherwise it would prevent one sitting in a cave from seeing the outside, and vice versa, because there is no difference between the barrier surrounding the seer or the seen. And it is not pure non-existence contradicting the creation (ja'liyyah) from His saying: 'And He made darkness and light.' It is needless of definition like other sensibles, and its definition is that it is a quality that is the primary perfection.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of light. It begins by stating that light (ḍaw') is either essential (dhātī) if it originates from the substance itself, like the sun, and is called ḍiyā'; or accidental (ʿaraḍī) if it comes from another source, like the moon, and is called nūr. The accidental light can be primary, secondary, etc., depending on the sequence of reflection. Then it discusses darkness (ẓulmah): it is a privation (ʿadam milkah) of light, not a positive quality (kayfiyyah wujūdiyyah). The author provides a proof: if darkness were a positive quality, it would act as a barrier to vision, but a person in a cave can see outside because darkness does not prevent vision. He also references the Quranic verse 'and He made darkness and light' (al-Anʿam 6:1) to show that darkness is created, but not as a positive entity. Finally, he notes that light is known by sensation and does not need definition, but if defined, it is a quality that is a primary perfection (kamāl awwal).

Key Terms
الضوء
الذاتي
العرضي
الضياء
النور
الظلمة
عدم الملكة
كيفية
كمال أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that darkness is a positive quality or a substance.
Correction: The passage argues darkness is a privation of light, not a positive quality. It provides a rational proof and a Quranic reference.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is darkness?
A) A positive quality
B) A privation of light
C) A substance
D) A type of light

Key Takeaway
Light is either essential (from the substance) or accidental (acquired), while darkness is simply the absence of light, not a positive quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel darkness in your heart—despair, confusion, or distance from Allah—do you treat it like a solid wall that blocks you, or do you recognize it as the absence of His light that you must seek to fill? What will you do today to let His light in?
?
You call yourself a believer—but is your faith essential, like the sun’s light that shines from its own substance, or is it accidental, flickering depending on circumstances? Since only Allah’s light is essential, what does it mean for your faith to be a reflected light, and how does that humble you?
?
If darkness is merely the absence of light, then what areas of your life have you allowed to remain dark by not seeking the light of divine guidance? Name one specific habit or thought you have accepted as 'just the way you are' that is actually a privation of truth; what step will you take to remove that darkness?

Defining Shadow and Critiquing a Misinterpretation
Sanusi defines shadow and critiques a misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of shadow as given by Sanusi and why a common interpretation is rejected.

Core Idea
Shadow is a positive result from air opposite a luminous body.

Sanusi begins with a careful definition of shadow, distinguishing between luminous bodies. He then critiques an alternative interpretation that would incorrectly include moonlight, showing the importance of precision in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the precise wording: shadow is 'what results from air opposite a luminous body.'
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents two interpretations of shadow. Find both and see how they differ.
3
Understand the critique
The author rejects the second interpretation because it is too broad and would include moonlight, which is not shadow by agreement.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما الظل فهو ما يحصل من الهواء المقابل للمضيء، بالذات كالشمس والنار، أو بالغير كالقمر، وتفسيره بالمستفاد من المضيء بالغير ليس بمطرد لتناوله ما هو من مقابلة القمر . جرم أي الضوء الحاصل من الهواء المضيء بالذات كالشمس والنار، أو بالغير كالقمر، وقد يفسر بالضوء المستفاد والمضيء بالغير، ولا خفاء في صدقه على الضوء الحاصل من مقابلة القمر، مع أنه ليس بظل وفاقاً، وما ذكر في المواقف من أن مراتب الظل تختلف قوة وضعفاً بحسب اختلاف الأسباب والمعدات كما يشاهد في اختلاف ضوء البيت بحسب كبر الكوة وصغرها، حتى إنه ينقسم إلى ما لا نهاية له انقسام الكوة ، فمبنى على ما يراه الحكماء من عدم تناهي انقسامات الأجسام والمقادير وما يتبعها، وإن كانت محصورة بين حاصرين حتى إن الذارع الواحد يقبل الانقسام إلى ما لا نهاية له، ولو بالفرض والوهم وما تقرر من أن المحصور بين حاصرين لا يكون إلا متناهياً، فمعناه بحسب الكمية الاتصالية، أو الانفصالية، لا بحسب قبول الانقسام.
He said: As for shadow, it is what results from the air opposite a luminous body—whether luminous in itself like the sun and fire, or by another like the moon. Interpreting it as 'what is derived from a luminant by another' is not universal, because it includes what is from the moon's opposition. [Jarm] i.e., the light resulting from air luminous in itself like sun and fire, or by another like the moon. And it may be interpreted as 'acquired light' and 'luminant by another,' and there is no doubt it applies to the light resulting from the moon's opposition, even though it is not shadow by agreement. And what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif that the degrees of shadow vary in strength and weakness according to the difference of causes and instruments, as seen in the variation of light in a room according to the size of the window, such that it divides into endless divisions like the division of the window, is based on what the philosophers hold about the non-finiteness of the divisions of bodies and quantities and what follows them, even if they are bounded between two boundaries, such that a single cubit can be divided endlessly, even by supposition and imagination. And what is established that what is bounded between two boundaries cannot but be finite, its meaning is in terms of continuous or discrete quantity, not in terms of capacity for division.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines shadow as what arises from air opposite a luminous body—either self-luminous (like sun) or luminant by another (like moon). It critiques a definition of shadow as 'acquired light from a luminant by another' because that definition would incorrectly include light from the moon's opposition (which is not considered shadow). The author then explains that al-Mawaqif's discussion about shadow's infinite gradations (e.g., varying window sizes) relies on the philosophers' view that bodies and quantities can be divided infinitely in potential, even if they are bounded actually. The point is that the infinite divisibility is in terms of capacity for division, not in actual finite measurement.

المضيء بالذات
المضيء بالغير

A) It is too broad and includes cases considered not shadow.
B) It is too narrow and excludes the sun.
C) It only applies to the moon.
D) It contradicts the definition of light.

Key Takeaway
Shadow is defined as the air opposite a luminous body; interpreting it as acquired light from a luminant by another would wrongly include moonlight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The moon's light is not shadow even though it superficially matches a flawed criterion. What in your life—a belief, a habit, a relationship—do you mislabel because you use a flawed measure? What step will you take today to align that label with the truth of revelation?

Infinite Gradations of Shadow and Continuous Division
Sanusi explains infinite gradations of shadow based on infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical basis for the infinite gradations of shadow and the concept of potential infinite division.

Core Idea
Shadow can be infinitely divided in potential, like continuous quantities.

Sanusi references al-Mawaqif's observation that shadow intensities vary infinitely with the size of the obstacle. This leads into a discussion of the philosophers' view on the infinite divisibility of bodies, and a clarification of the principle that bounded objects are finite only in actual measure, not in capacity for division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on gradations
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the example of the window size affecting light intensity.
2
Connect to infinite divisibility
The author says this infinite variation is based on the philosophers' view about infinite division of bodies. Think about how a physical object can be divided endlessly in potential.
3
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The passage clarifies that an object bounded by two limits can still be infinitely divided in capacity. Understand the difference between actual finite measure and potential division.
This is a key point in Islamic philosophy of physics.

Source Text

Light Is Distinct from Color and a Condition for Its Visibility
Al-Taftazani argues that light is distinct from color and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that light is a distinct quality from color and a necessary condition for seeing color, not for its existence, and how the author refutes opposing views.

Core Idea
Light is not color; it is a separate quality needed only for seeing color.

This passage from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a fundamental issue in kalām physics: the relationship between light and color. The author defends the view that light is a distinct quality from color and serves as a condition for vision, not for existence. He presents sensory evidence and logical refutations against philosophers like Ibn Sina and Imam al-Razi, establishing a clear distinction that underpins later theological discussions on perception and causality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thesis
The author states the central claim: light is distinct from color in reality and a condition for correct vision. Note the two aspects he will prove.
The thesis sets up the entire discussion.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof uses sense perception and examples like black objects and crystal at night to show light and color can exist separately.
Observe how empirical evidence is used.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof argues that color is not seen in darkness even when present, showing light is needed for vision, not existence.
This relies on the certainty of color's existence in darkness.
4
Analyze refutations of opposing views
The author refutes the views that light is the appearance of color (philosophers) and that it is a condition for existence (Ibn Sina), and also addresses al-Razi's circularity objection.
Each refutation targets a different aspect of the opposing arguments.
5
Identify the author's conclusion
The author concludes that light is a distinct quality and only a condition for seeing color, dismissing opposing arguments as weak or near denial of self-evident truths.
The conclusion reaffirms the initial thesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس : الضوء مغاير للون في الحقيقة وشرط له في صحة الرؤية . أما الأول : فبشهادة الحس وتضاد الألوان دون الأضواء وافتراقهما في الوجود كما في الأسواد واللامضيء والبللور المرئي في الليل ضوءه دون لونه . وأما الثاني : فلأنه لا يرى في الظلمة عند تحقق الشرائط مع القطع بوجوده وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الضوء ظهور اللون فالظهور المطلق الضوء، والخفاء المطلق هو الظلمة والمتوسط هو الظل، ويختلف مراتبه باختلاف مراتب القرب من الطرف، ولا تمسك لهم يعتد به . وذهب ابن سينا إلى أن الضوء شرط وجود اللون، لأن عدم رؤيته في الظلمة إنما هو عدمه، لا لكون الهواء المظلم عائقاً عن الإبصار، بدليل أن الجالس في الغار يبصر الخارج حول النار . ورد بأنه لانتفاء شرط الرؤية وهو الضوء المحيط بالمرئي. وقال الإمام الرازي قبول الضوء مشروط بوجود اللون فاشتراطه بوجود الضوء دور ، وهو ضعيف لأنه دور معية . الحق أن الضوء كيفية مغايرة للون وليس عبارة عن ظهور اللون على ما يراه بعض الحكماء، وأنه شرط لرؤية اللون لا لوجود على ما يراه ابن سينا ولا تمسك لهما يعتد به فيما ادعيا . كيف وإنه قريب من إنكار الضروريات وما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن قبول الجسم للضوء مشروط بوجود اللون فلو كان وجود اللون مشروطاً بوجود الضوء لزم الدور ضعيف، لأنه إن أراد بالمشروطة توقف السبق فممنوع أو المعية فغير محال، على أنه قد صرح بوجود الضوء بدون اللون كما في البللور المرئي بالليل .
Fifth Section: Light is distinct from color in reality and a condition for it in the correctness of vision. As for the first: by the testimony of the senses and the contrariety of colors but not of lights, and their separation in existence, as in black, non-luminous, and crystal seen at night—its light without its color. As for the second: because it is not seen in darkness when conditions are satisfied despite certainty of its existence. Some went to that light is the appearance of color: absolute appearance is light, absolute concealment is darkness, and the intermediate is shade, and its degrees differ according to degrees of proximity to the extremities, but there is no tenable argument for them. Ibn Sina went to that light is a condition for the existence of color, because its non-vision in darkness is due to its non-existence, not because dark air is an obstacle to sight, evidenced by the fact that the one sitting in a cave sees outside around the fire. It was refuted that this is because the condition of vision—namely light surrounding the visible—is absent. Imam al-Razi said: the acceptance of light is conditioned by the existence of color, so conditioning it by the existence of light is circular (dawr). It is weak because it is a circularity of simultaneity (dawr ma'iyya). The truth is that light is a quality distinct from color and is not the appearance of color as some philosophers hold, and that it is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence, contrary to Ibn Sina. There is no tenable argument for either of them in what they claim. Moreover, it is close to denying necessities. What Imam al-Razi mentioned—that the body's acceptance of light is conditioned by the existence of color, so if the existence of color were conditioned by the existence of light, circularity would follow—is weak, because if by 'conditioned' he means temporal priority, then it is denied; or if simultaneity, then it is not impossible, especially since he has affirmed the existence of light without color, as in crystal seen at night.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a kalām argument about the nature of light and its relation to color. The author (Saʿd al-Dīn al-Taftāzānī) defends the position that light is a distinct quality from color and is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence. He begins by stating the thesis: light is different from color in reality and is a condition (sharṭ) for correctly seeing color. He then provides two proofs. The first proof (أما الأول) relies on sense-perception and the fact that colors can be opposite to each other (e.g., black and white) while lights are not opposite, and that they can be separated in existence—for example, black objects (aswād) and non-luminous objects (lāmuḍīʾ) have color but little light, and crystal seen at night has light but no apparent color. The second proof (أما الثاني) is that color is not seen in complete darkness even when all other conditions of sight are met, yet we are certain the color exists. This shows light is a condition for visibility, not for the color's existence. The author then mentions a contrary view: some (al-Ḥukamāʾ) hold that light is simply the appearance of color (ẓuhūr al-lawn), with absolute appearance being light, absolute concealment being darkness, and intermediate being shade. He dismisses this as having no valid argument (lā tumassik lahum yuʿtaddu bihī). Next, he presents Ibn Sīnā's view: light is a condition for the existence of color (not just vision), arguing that non-vision in darkness is due to the color's non-existence, not an obstacle. Ibn Sīnā cites the person in a cave who sees outside around a fire—suggesting that darkness is not an obstacle per se. The author refutes this: the person in the cave sees because the firelight surrounds the external objects; thus the condition of vision (surrounding light) is absent in total darkness, not the color itself. Then he mentions Imām al-Rāzī's objection: if light is conditioned by color (i.e., the body's acceptance of light requires color), then conditioning color by light would be circular. The author calls this weak because it would be a circularity of simultaneity (dawr maʿiyya), which is not logically impossible; it only denies temporal priority. Moreover, he points out that both Ibn Sīnā and al-Rāzī have implicitly admitted light can exist without color (as in crystal at night), which undermines their positions. Finally, the author declares the truth (al-ḥaqq): light is a quality (kayfiyya) distinct from color, not its appearance, and it is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence. He concludes that the arguments of both opponents are untenable and even approach denial of self-evident truths (ḍarūriyyāt). Thus the passage systematically defends a realist distinction between light and color, a key point in kalām physics.

Key Terms

رؤية
ظلمة
ظل
شرط

معية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light is merely the appearance of color or a condition for the existence of color.
Correction: Light is a distinct quality from color and only a condition for seeing color, not for its existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the relationship between light and color?
A) Light is the appearance of color.
B) Light is a quality identical to color.
C) Light is distinct from color and a condition for seeing it.
D) Light is a condition for the existence of color.

Key Takeaway
Light is a distinct quality from color and serves only as a condition for vision, not for the existence of color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something does not exist simply because you cannot perceive it? How does this lesson's distinction between light as a condition for vision versus existence challenge you to separate your perception from reality in your own life?
?
When you find yourself in a disagreement about a matter of faith, do you tend to conflate the means of knowing (like reason or scripture) with the thing itself? Reflect on the cave analogy—how might hidden assumptions skew your reasoning about God's attributes?
?
Al-Taftazani insists on precise distinctions (light vs. color, condition vs. existence). In what area of your spiritual practice are you tempted to blur similar distinctions—perhaps between intention and action, or between means and end—and what would it cost you to sharpen them today?

The Ontology of Sound: External Existence and Perception
Al-Taftazani defines sound and argues for its external existence through perception of direction and distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's philosophical argument that sound exists externally, not merely in the sense of hearing, based on the perception of direction and distance.

Core Idea
Our ability to locate sounds proves they exist outside the mind.

Al-Taftazani begins by defining sound as a quality in air arising from waves caused by striking and rending. He then tackles a key question: does sound exist only in the perceiver's sense, or externally? Through a careful analysis of how we perceive direction and distance, he argues that sound must have an external reality beyond subjective sensation. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of sound
Read the definition of sound as a quality in air arising from wave, striking, and rending. Note the conditions of resistance.
Sound is not the wave itself, but a separate quality.
2
Identify the subjective view
The author presents the view that sound exists only in the sense of hearing, not externally. See why this is initially plausible.
3
Follow the refutation using direction and distance
The key argument: if sound were only in the sense, we could not perceive its direction or distance. Since we do, sound must exist externally. Examine the reasoning.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Consider objections and answers
An objector suggests that direction is inferred from air movement, not sound's location. The author rebuts by showing that plugging one ear and other experiments disprove this.
The refutation uses concrete examples.
5
Explore the subtlety of external vs. arriving air
The author then asks whether sound is heard only in the air that reaches the ear or also in the external air. The answer is both, again supported by direction/distance perception.
This further solidifies the external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البطلان إلا فيما يحتاج إلى زيادة، بيان والصوت عندهم كيفية تحدث في الهواء بسبب تموجه المعلول للقرع الذي هو أساس عنيف، والقلع الذي هو تفريق عنيف بشرط مقاومة المقروع للقارع والمقلوع للقالع، كما في قرع الماء وقلع الكرباس بخلاف القطن لعدم المقاومة، والمراد بالتموج حالة شبيهة بتموج الماء تحدث بصدم بعد صدم مع سكون بعد سكون، وليس الصوت نفس التموج، أو نفس القرع والقلع على ما توهمه بعضهم بناء على اشتباه الشيء بسببه القريب أو البعيد لأن التموج والقرع والقلع ليست من المسموعات قطعاً، بل ربما يدرك الأول باللمس والآخران بالبصر، وقد يتوهم وجود للصوت في الخارج وإنما يحدث في الحس عند وصول الهواء المتموج إلى الصماخ واستدل على بطلان ذلك بأنه لو لم يوجد إلا في الحس لما أدرك عند سماعه جهته وحده من القرب والبعد لأن التقدير أنه لا وجود له في مكان وجهة خارج الحس واللازم باطل قطعاً، لأنـا إذا سمعنا الصوت تعرف أنه وصل إلينا من جهة اليمين أو اليسار ومن مكان قريب أو بعيد.
لا يقال : يجوز أن يكون إدراك الجهة لأجل أن الهواء المتموج يجيء منها ويميز القريب والبعيد، لأجل أن أثر القارع القريب أقوى من البعيد، وإن لم يكن الصوت موجوداً في الجهة والمسافة، لأنا نقول : لو صح الأول لما أدركت الجهة التي على خلاف الأذن السامعة وليس كذلك لأن السامع قد يسد أذنه اليمنى ويجيء الصوت من يمينه فيسمعه بأذنه اليسرى، ويعرف أنه جاء من يمينه مع القطع بأن الهواء المتموج لا يصل إلى اليسرى إلا بعد الانعطاف عن اليمين ولو صح الثاني لزم أن تشتبه القوة والضعف بالقرب والبعد، فلم يميز بين البعيد القوى والقريب الضعيف، وظن في الصوتين المتساويين في القرب والبعد المختلفين بالقوة والضعف أنهما مختلفان في القرب والبعد وليس كذلك ولهم تردد في مقام آخر وهو أنه إذا وصل الهواء المتموج إلى الصماخ فالمسموع هو الصوت القائم بالهواء الواصل فقط، أو بالهواء الخارج أيضاً، والحق هو الأخير بدليل إدراك جهة الصوت وحده من القرب والبعد فإنه لو لم يقع الإحساس به إلا من حيث إنه في الهواء الواصل إلى الصماخ دون الخارج الذي هو مبدأ حدوث الصوت أو واسطة لم يكن عند الحس فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا لم يوجد خارج الصماخ أصلاً فلم يعرف جهته ولا مربه أو بعده، كما أن اللمس لم يدرك الملموس إلا من حيث انتهى إليه، لا من حيث إنه في أول المسافة لم يميز بين وروده من اليمين أو اليسار ومن القريب أو البعيد فظهر أن في معرفة جهة الصوت وحده من القرب والبعد دلالة على مطلوبين من جهة أنها تدل على أن القائم بالهواء الخارج من الصماخ أيضاً مسموع، وذلك يدل على أنها هناك موجودة، وهذا ما قال الإمام إن التميز بين الجهات والقرب والبعد من الأصوات لما كان حاصلاً علمنا أنا ندرك الأصوات الخارجية حيث هي، ولا يمكننا أن ندركها حيث إلا وهي موجودة خارج الصماخ وما أورد من الإشكال، وهو أن المدرك بالسمع لما لم يكن إلا الصوت دون الجهة لم يكن كون الصوت حاصلاً في تلك الجهة مدركاً له بل مدركة الصوت الذي في تلك الجهة لا من حيث هو في تلك الجهة بل من حيث إنه صوت فقط، وهذا لا يختلف باختلاف الجهات فكيف يوجب إدراك الجهة؟ ليس بشيء لأنهم لا يجعلون كون الصوت في تلك الجهة مدركاً بالسمع إلا بمعنى أنا نعرف بسماع الصوت في تلك الجهة أنه هناك، كما نعرف بذوق الحلاوة، أو شم الرائحة هذا الجسم أنهما منه، وإن لم يكن الجسم من المذوقات أو المشمومات، وأما السبب في ذلك فحاصل ما ذكروا فيه أنا بعد ما أدركنا الصوت عند الصماخ نتبعه بتأملنا فيتأدى إدراكنا من الذي يصل إلينا إلى ما قبله فما قبله من هي من
The refutation except where addition is needed. Statement: Sound according to them is a quality that occurs in the air due to its wave, which is caused by striking, which is a violent collision, and rending, which is a violent separation, on condition of resistance of the struck to the striker and the rent to the render, as in striking water and rending canvas, unlike cotton due to lack of resistance. And by wave is meant a state similar to the wave of water, occurring by impact after impact with stillness after stillness. And sound is not the wave itself, nor the striking and rending themselves, as some have imagined based on confusing a thing with its proximate or remote cause, because wave, striking, and rending are certainly not audible; rather, the first may be perceived by touch and the other two by sight. And it may be imagined that sound exists externally, but it only occurs in the sense when the waved air reaches the ear canal. And it was argued for the falsity of this by saying: if it existed only in the sense, then when hearing it, one would not perceive its direction alone and its distance, because it is assumed that it has no existence in a place or direction external to the sense, and the consequent is necessarily false, because when we hear a sound, we know that it reached us from the right or left side and from a near or far place. It cannot be said: it is possible that the perception of direction is due to the fact that the waved air comes from it and distinguishes near and far, because the effect of the near striker is stronger than the far, even if the sound does not exist in the direction and distance. For we say: if the first were correct, one would not perceive the direction opposite the hearing ear, but it is not so because the hearer may block his right ear and the sound comes from his right, so he hears it with his left ear and knows that it came from his right, with certainty that the waved air does not reach the left ear except after turning from the right. And if the second were correct, then strength and weakness would be confused with nearness and distance, so one would not distinguish between the far strong and the near weak, and one would mistakenly think of two sounds equal in nearness and distance but differing in strength and weakness that they differ in nearness and distance, but it is not so. And they have hesitation in another station: when the waved air reaches the ear canal, is the heard sound only the sound subsisting in the arriving air, or also in the external air? The truth is the latter, evidenced by perceiving the direction of the sound alone and its distance from nearness and farness. For if sensation of it occurred only insofar as it is in the air reaching the ear canal, not the external air which is the beginning of sound’s occurrence or intermediary, then in sense there would be no difference between this and if it did not exist outside the ear canal at all, so one would not know its direction nor its passage or distance. Just as touch does not perceive the tangible except insofar as it reaches it, not insofar as it is at the beginning of the distance, and does not distinguish between its arrival from right or left and from near or far. So it appears that in knowing the direction of sound alone and its distance from nearness and farness there is an indication of two sought things: it indicates that what subsists in the external air from the ear canal is also heard, and that indicates that it exists there. And this is what the Imam said: since distinction between directions and nearness/farness of sounds occurs, we know that we perceive external sounds where they are, and we cannot perceive them where they are not existing outside the ear canal. And the objection raised — that since the perceived by hearing is only sound without direction, the existence of sound in that direction is not perceived, rather the perceived is the sound in that direction not insofar as it is in that direction but only insofar as it is sound, and this does not vary with varying directions, so how does it necessitate perception of direction? — is nothing, because they do not make the sound's being in that direction perceived by hearing except in the sense that we know through hearing the sound in that direction that it is there, just as we know through tasting sweetness or smelling a scent that this body has them, even if the body is not among the tasted or smelled things. As for the reason for that, the sum of what they mentioned is that after we perceive the sound at the ear canal, we follow it with our reflection, so our perception extends from what reaches us to what is before it, then what is before it from it from
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a sustained philosophical argument about the nature of sound and its existence. The author begins by defining sound as a quality (كيفية) arising in air due to a wave (تموج) caused by a striking (قرع) which is a violent collision, and a rending (قلع) which is a violent separation, both requiring resistance. He clarifies that the wave is a state of successive impacts and pauses, and sound is not identical to the wave, strike, or rending; these are not audible but perceived by touch or sight. The core argument then refutes the view that sound exists only in the sense (الحس), i.e., subjectively. The refutation is based on the perception of direction and distance: if sound were only in the sense, we could not know whether it came from the right or left, or from near or far. But we do perceive these, so sound must exist externally. An objection is then considered: perhaps we perceive direction from the air's movement, not from sound's external location. The author answers by showing that if direction were inferred from air movement, plugging one ear would prevent correct direction perception, but it does not; also, distinguishing near/far would be confused with strong/weak. After this, the author raises a further subtlety: once air reaches the ear, is the heard sound only that in the arriving air or also in the external air? He affirms the latter, using the same evidence of direction/distance perception. He argues that if we only sensed sound at the ear, we could not know its external origin, just as touch only perceives at the point of contact. Thus, the perception of direction/distance proves both that external air carries sound and that sound exists externally. The author then quotes 'the Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) to summarize this point. Finally, he rebuts an objection: direction is not directly sensed, but inferred from hearing the sound in a certain location, analogous to inferring a body from a taste or smell. The passage concludes by explaining that our reflection extends perception from the sound at the ear to its source.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound is purely subjective and has no external existence.
Correction: The passage argues that because we perceive direction and distance of sounds, they must exist externally; otherwise such spatial perception would be impossible.

Checkpoint

A) The physical definition of sound as a quality in air
B) The perception of direction and distance of sound
C) The fact that sound can be blocked by the ear
D) The comparison of sound to taste and smell

Key Takeaway
Sound exists externally because we can locate its direction and distance, which would be impossible if it were only subjective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that sound has an external reality that you can locate. When you hear the call to prayer, do you consciously recognize it as a real call from a specific place demanding a response, or do you let it fade into background noise? How can you train yourself to respond to the adhan with the awareness that this sound comes from a real source calling you to your Lord?
?
If sound exists objectively because your senses can pinpoint its direction, what does that imply about other unseen realities—like the words of Allah, the Angel’s voice, or the questioning in the grave? Are you treating these as merely internal feelings or as external facts that demand your immediate attention?
?
Taftazani proves you can know where a sound is coming from even when you hear it with the ‘wrong’ ear. Where is the voice of your conscience—or the prompting of faith—coming from? Are you dismissing it as a subjective thought, or do you acknowledge it as a real call from outside yourself needing action?

The Short and Long Syllable: Core Definitions
Al-Taftazani defines short and long syllables and gives alternative definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of the short and long syllables in Arabic phonetics, including the alternative definition that equates a short syllable followed by a silent consonant with a long syllable.

Core Idea
A short syllable is a consonant + short vowel; a long syllable is a consonant + long vowel.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing the basic phonological units used in Arabic metrical theory. These syllables form the building blocks of poetic meter and are essential for understanding longer discussions in logic and rhetoric. The passage first gives a concise definition, then elaborates with the authorities of al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and al-Imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of short and long syllables
Identify the two types: short syllable = consonant + short vowel; long syllable = consonant + long vowel. Note the examples: 'li' (with short vowel) and 'lā' (with long vowel).
The Arabic letters are used as examples.
2
Understand the alternative definition
Notice that sometimes a short syllable with a silent consonant (maqṣūr + sākin) is considered a long syllable due to metrical weight. Examples: 'hal', 'qul'.
This is because their weight equals that of a long syllable.
3
Recognize the scholarly authority
Al-Taftazani cites al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and al-Imam as sources for these definitions, showing the reliance on earlier authorities in this field.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والصامت مع المصوت المقصور يسمى مقطعاً مقصوراً مثل ل ومع الممدود مقطعاً ممدوداً مثل لا وقد يقال المقطع مقصور مع ساكن بعده مثل هل، فهو صامتان بينهما مصوت مقصور بخلاف لا فإنه صامت و مصوت ممدود ،فقط ليس بينهما مصوت مقصور على ما هو اعتبار العربية، وذلك لأن المصوت الممدود ليس إلا إشباعاً للمقصور فهو مندرج فيه جزء منه . قد اشتبه على بعض المتأخرين معنى المقطع مع اشتهاره فيما بين القوم، فأوردنا في ذلك ما صرح به الفارابي وابن سينا والإمام وغيرهم، وهو أن الحرف الصامت مع المصوت المقصور يسمى مقطعاً مقصوراً مثل له بالفتح ، أو الضمة، أو الكسر ، ومع المصوت الممدود يسمى مقطعاً ممدوداً، مثل لا ولو ولى، وقد يقال المقطع الممدود بمقطع مقصور مع صامت ساكن بعده مثل هل وقل وبع لمماثلته المقطع الممدود في الوزن. فإن قيل : لا حاجة إلى هذا التفصيل فإن المقطع الممدود ليس إلا مقطعاً مقصوراً مع ساكن بعـده سواء كان مصوتاً مثل لا أو صامتاً مثل هل، ولهذا يقال : إن المقطع حرف مع حركة أو حرف متحرك مع ساكن بعده ، والأول المقصور الثاني الممدود. قلنا : المقطع الممدود بالاعتبار الثاني صامتان هما الهاء واللام في هل بينهما مصوت مقصور هو فتحة الهاء . وبالاعتبار الأول مجرد صامت و مصوت ممدود ليس بينهما مصوت مقصور على ما يراه أهل العربية من أن لا لام وألف بينهما فتحة، وذلك لأن المصوت الممدود ليس إلا إشباعاً للمصوت المقصور فيكون المقصور مندرجاً في الممدود جزءاً منه وهذا ما يقال إن الحركات أبعاض حروف المد فلا يكون إلا صامتاً مع مصوت ممدود
He said: The consonant with the short vowel is called a short syllable, like 'la' (ل) with [the vowel], and with the long vowel it is called a long syllable, like 'la' (لا). And sometimes the short syllable with a following silent [consonant] is said [to be like a long syllable], like 'hal' (هل), for it is two consonants between which there is a short vowel, unlike 'la' (لا) for it is a consonant and a long vowel only, no short vowel between them, according to the consideration of Arabic, because the long vowel is not but an extension of the short vowel, so the short is contained in it as a part of it. Some later scholars have been confused about the meaning of 'syllable' despite its fame among the people, so we presented in that regard what al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, al-Imam, and others clarified, namely that a consonant with a short vowel is called a short syllable, like 'la' (ل) with fatha, or damma, or kasra, and with a long vowel it is called a long syllable, like 'la' (لا) and 'lu' (لو) and 'li' (لى). And sometimes the long syllable is said [to be] a short syllable together with a following silent consonant, like 'hal' (هل), 'qul' (قل), and 'bi' (بع) due to its similarity to the long syllable in weight. If it is said: There is no need for this detail, for the long syllable is nothing but a short syllable with a following consonant, whether a vowel like 'la' or a consonant like 'hal'; and for this it is said: The syllable is a letter with a movement or a moving letter with a following silent, the first is the short, the second is the long. We say: The long syllable according to the second consideration is two consonants, namely ha and lam in 'hal', between them a short vowel which is the fatha of the ha. But according to the first consideration it is merely a consonant and a long vowel with no short vowel between them, according to the view of the people of Arabic that 'la' is lam and alif with a fatha between them, because the long vowel is only an extension of the short vowel, so the short is contained in the long as part of it; and this is what is said: The movements are parts of the letters of prolongation, so it is only a consonant with a long vowel.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines two types of syllables in Arabic phonetic theory: the short syllable (maqṭa‘ maqṣūr) consists of a consonant and a short vowel (e.g., 'li' with any short vowel), and the long syllable (maqṭa‘ mamdūd) consists of a consonant and a long vowel (e.g., 'lā', 'lū', 'lī'). The author notes that sometimes a short syllable followed by a silent consonant is considered a long syllable due to metrical similarity (e.g., 'hal', 'qul'). An objection is raised: why distinguish these types, since a long syllable can be seen as a short syllable plus a following consonant? The answer provides two perspectives. From one perspective (the second consideration), 'hal' has two consonants with a short vowel between them, so it is a short syllable plus a consonant. From another perspective (the first consideration), 'lā' has a consonant and a long vowel with no intervening short vowel; the long vowel is an extension of the short vowel, so the short vowel is inherently part of the long vowel. This resolves the objection by showing that the definition depends on whether one views the long vowel as containing the short vowel or as a separate element.

Key Terms
مقطع مقصور
مقطع ممدود
صامت
مصوت مقصور
مصوت ممدود
إشباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a short syllable (maqṭa‘ maqṣūr)?
A) ل (with fatha)
B) لا
C) هل
D) لى (with long vowel)

Key Takeaway
A short syllable consists of a consonant and a short vowel, while a long syllable consists of a consonant and a long vowel; additionally, a short syllable followed by a silent consonant can function as a long syllable in meter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a new definition in your studies, do you accept it superficially or do you strive to understand its essence, like the distinction between short and long vowels? Is there an area of your life where you might be satisfied with vague understanding, and will you commit to seeking precision?
?
The passage notes that even later scholars were confused about the meaning of 'syllable' despite its fame. Have you ever assumed you understood a key concept well enough, only to realize you had it wrong? What fundamental belief or practice should you re-examine to ensure you are not falling into similar confusion?
?
Al-Taftazani relies on the definitions of earlier authorities. In your own journey of learning, are you building on a solid foundation of trusted sources, or are you mixing half-truths without verifying? Will you take time today to confirm the soundness of one core Islamic belief you hold?

Objection and Resolution: The Nature of the Long Vowel
Al-Taftazani responds to an objection about syllable classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against distinguishing short and long syllables and the resolution that the long vowel contains the short vowel as its essence, so the two definitions are compatible.

Core Idea
The long vowel is an extension of the short vowel, so the long syllable inherently includes a short vowel.

After defining the syllables, al-Taftazani addresses an objection: why not simply consider a long syllable as a short syllable plus a following consonant? The answer reveals two perspectives on analysis, showing that the long vowel itself includes the short vowel as its part. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that there is no need for a separate category; a long syllable is just a short syllable followed by a consonant (vowel or consonant). For example, 'lā' can be seen as short + alif.
This objection simplifies the classification.
2
Understand the two considerations
The answer offers two perspectives: in 'hal', we have two consonants with a short vowel between them. In 'lā', according to Arabic grammar, we have a consonant and a long vowel with no short vowel between them—the long vowel is an extension of the short vowel.
This shows the two views are not contradictory.
3
Grasp the key insight
The long vowel is not a separate element; it is merely the extension (ishbā') of a short vowel. Therefore, in 'lā', the short vowel is already part of the long vowel, so it cannot be analyzed as short plus consonant.
This preserves the distinct definition of the long syllable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والصامت مع المصوت المقصور يسمى مقطعاً مقصوراً مثل ل ومع الممدود مقطعاً ممدوداً مثل لا وقد يقال المقطع مقصور مع ساكن بعده مثل هل، فهو صامتان بينهما مصوت مقصور بخلاف لا فإنه صامت و مصوت ممدود ،فقط ليس بينهما مصوت مقصور على ما هو اعتبار العربية، وذلك لأن المصوت الممدود ليس إلا إشباعاً للمقصور فهو مندرج فيه جزء منه . قد اشتبه على بعض المتأخرين معنى المقطع مع اشتهاره فيما بين القوم، فأوردنا في ذلك ما صرح به الفارابي وابن سينا والإمام وغيرهم، وهو أن الحرف الصامت مع المصوت المقصور يسمى مقطعاً مقصوراً مثل له بالفتح ، أو الضمة، أو الكسر ، ومع المصوت الممدود يسمى مقطعاً ممدوداً، مثل لا ولو ولى، وقد يقال المقطع الممدود بمقطع مقصور مع صامت ساكن بعده مثل هل وقل وبع لمماثلته المقطع الممدود في الوزن. فإن قيل : لا حاجة إلى هذا التفصيل فإن المقطع الممدود ليس إلا مقطعاً مقصوراً مع ساكن بعـده سواء كان مصوتاً مثل لا أو صامتاً مثل هل، ولهذا يقال : إن المقطع حرف مع حركة أو حرف متحرك مع ساكن بعده ، والأول المقصور الثاني الممدود. قلنا : المقطع الممدود بالاعتبار الثاني صامتان هما الهاء واللام في هل بينهما مصوت مقصور هو فتحة الهاء . وبالاعتبار الأول مجرد صامت و مصوت ممدود ليس بينهما مصوت مقصور على ما يراه أهل العربية من أن لا لام وألف بينهما فتحة، وذلك لأن المصوت الممدود ليس إلا إشباعاً للمصوت المقصور فيكون المقصور مندرجاً في الممدود جزءاً منه وهذا ما يقال إن الحركات أبعاض حروف المد فلا يكون إلا صامتاً مع مصوت ممدود
He said: The consonant with the short vowel is called a short syllable, like 'la' (ل) with [the vowel], and with the long vowel it is called a long syllable, like 'la' (لا). And sometimes the short syllable with a following silent [consonant] is said [to be like a long syllable], like 'hal' (هل), for it is two consonants between which there is a short vowel, unlike 'la' (لا) for it is a consonant and a long vowel only, no short vowel between them, according to the consideration of Arabic, because the long vowel is not but an extension of the short vowel, so the short is contained in it as a part of it. Some later scholars have been confused about the meaning of 'syllable' despite its fame among the people, so we presented in that regard what al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, al-Imam, and others clarified, namely that a consonant with a short vowel is called a short syllable, like 'la' (ل) with fatha, or damma, or kasra, and with a long vowel it is called a long syllable, like 'la' (لا) and 'lu' (لو) and 'li' (لى). Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a long syllable is exactly the same as a short syllable plus a consonant, so there is no real distinction.
Correction: The passage shows that the long vowel is an extension of the short vowel, so the short vowel is already present in the long vowel; thus they are not compositionally identical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does 'lā' not contain a short vowel between the consonant and the long vowel?
A) Because the long vowel is an extension of the short vowel, so the short vowel is included within the long vowel.
B) Because Arabic does not allow short vowels before long vowels.
C) Because 'lā' is a single letter.
D) Because short vowels only appear in short syllables.

Key Takeaway
The long vowel is an extension of the short vowel, so the long syllable inherently contains the short vowel, making it a distinct category from a short syllable followed by a consonant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a matter in your faith, do you often accept the first analysis (like the objector) without considering deeper perspectives? This lesson shows that even a simple syllable can be misunderstood if we ignore hidden components. What is one core belief you hold about yourself or your practice that might benefit from a second i‘tibār? Will you take time today to re-examine it by identifying the 'short vowel' that gives it meaning?
?
The long vowel contains the short vowel as its extension; the outer is sustained by the inner. In your spiritual life, is your outward practice—prayer, charity, speech—merely a long vowel without its inner short vowel? Or does your action flow from an inner reality of faith and sincerity? Let this lesson push you to check one action this week and reconnect it to its inner source.
?
The word 'hal' is analyzed as two consonants with a short vowel between. Your daily routine is like a short syllable: it has small, often hidden acts (like a fatḥa) that give it life. Have you neglected those quiet moments—a sincere du‘ā’, a moment of reflection—that connect your outer duties? Identify one such 'hidden vowel' you can revive today.

The Definition of Speech (Kalām) and Its Components
Al-Taftazani defines speech as ordered heard letters, excluding non-sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of kalām as an ordered collection of heard distinct letters, and how it relates to the terms lafẓ, ḥarf, and maqṭa‘.

Core Idea
Speech is ordered heard letters; not all sounds qualify.

This lesson introduces the fundamental definition of kalām (speech) according to the theologians. Al-Taftazani begins by stating that speech is composed of letters and divides into simple and compound. He then specifies that speech is an ordered collection of heard distinct letters, thereby excluding written letters, imagined sounds, and animal noises. Understanding this definition is key to grasping the technical usage of 'speech' in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that kalām is defined as an ordered collection of heard distinct letters.
2
Identify the exclusions
Pay attention to the exclusions: 'heard' excludes written or imagined; 'distinct' excludes bird sounds. Why are these exclusions important?
They show that kalām is a human, articulated sound.
3
Distinguish kalām from lafẓ
The passage mentions that kalām is also called lafẓ, but there are subtle differences. Observe how the terms are used interchangeably and yet sometimes distinguished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحروف ومكونات اللفظ والكلام والمفرد والمركب. قال: ويتألف من الحروف الكلام المنقسم إلى المفرد والمركب بأقسامهما، ويسمى اللفظ أيضاً، وقد يخص الكلام بما يفيد ولو كان مقطعاً مثل قى، واللفظ بما يتألف من المقاطع، وبهذا يقع في مقابلة الحرف والمقطع حيث يقال أجزاء المركب ألفاظ أو حروف أو مقاطع. ق، ويفسر بالمنتظم من الحروف المسموعة المتميزة، ويحترز بالمسموعة عن المكتوبة أو المتخيلة وبالمتميزة عن أصوات الطيور، والكلام ينقسم إلى المهمل ،والموضوع والموضوع إلى المفرد والمركب، والمفرد إلى الاسم والفعل والحرف والمركب إلى التام الذي يصح السكوت عليه، وإلى غير التام، واللفظ أعم من الحروف والكلام . وقد يخص الكلام باللفظ المفيد بمعنى دلالته على نسبة يصح السكوت عليها سواء كانت إنشائية مثل : قم وهل زيد قائم؟، ولعل زيداً قائم؟؟ ونحو ذلك أو إخبارية مثل: زيد قائم، وسواء كان اللفظ مقطعاً مقصوراً مثل ق أو ممدوداً مثل فى وقو أو مركباً من المقاطع كما ذكر وقد يخص اللفظ بما يتألف من المقاطع فيقابله الحرف والمقطع ولذا يقال : أجزاء المركب ألفاظ أو حروف أو مقاطع فزيد قائم من لفظين وماذا من مقطعين ويا زيد من مقطع، ولفظ درى في أمر المخاطبة
The letters and components of utterance, speech, the simple and the compound. He said: Speech is composed of letters, dividing into the simple and the compound with their subdivisions, and it is also called utterance (lafẓ). Sometimes speech is specified as that which conveys meaning even if it is a syllable, like 'qi' (قى), and utterance is specified as that which is composed of syllables, and by this it stands in contrast to the letter and the syllable, for it is said: The parts of the compound are utterances, or letters, or syllables. (And it is interpreted as the ordered [collection] of heard distinct letters. By 'heard' he excludes written or imagined [letters], and by 'distinct' he excludes the sounds of birds.) Speech divides into the meaningless (muhmal) and the meaningful (mawḍū‘). The meaningful divides into the simple (mufrad) and the compound (murakkab). The simple divides into noun, verb, and particle. The compound divides into the complete (tāmm) on which it is proper to pause, and the incomplete. And utterance is more general than letter and speech. Sometimes speech is specified as the meaningful utterance in the sense of its indication of a relation on which it is proper to pause, whether it is imperative like 'Stand!' (Qum) and 'Is Zayd standing?' (Hal Zaydun qā’im?) and 'Perhaps Zayd is standing?' (La‘alla Zaydan qā’im?) and the like, or declarative like 'Zayd is standing' (Zaydun qā’im). And whether the utterance is a short syllable like 'q' (ق) or a long syllable like 'fī' (فى) and 'qu' (قو) or composed of syllables as mentioned. And sometimes utterance is specified as that which is composed of syllables, so it is contrasted with the letter and the syllable, and for this it is said: The parts of the compound are utterances or letters or syllables. So 'Zaydun qā’imun' consists of two utterances, and 'Mādhā' consists of two syllables, and 'Yā Zaydu' consists of one syllable, and the word 'darā' (درى) in the matter of address.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines the components of speech (kalām). It first states that speech is composed of letters and divides into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab). The term 'lafẓ' (utterance) is synonymous but sometimes used differently. Then it defines 'kalām' as an ordered collection of heard distinct letters, excluding written or imagined letters and bird sounds. Kalām divides into meaningless (muhmal) and meaningful (mawḍū‘). Meaningful divides into simple (a single word) and compound (a phrase). Simple divides into noun, verb, and particle. Compound divides into complete (tāmm, a sentence that can be paused upon) and incomplete. Lafẓ is more general than both 'ḥarf' (letter) and 'kalām'. Sometimes 'kalām' is specifically meaningful utterance that conveys a complete relation, whether imperative, interrogative, or declarative, regardless of whether the utterance is a short syllable, long syllable, or composed of multiple syllables. Additionally, 'lafẓ' can be restricted to what is composed of syllables, contrasting with a single letter or syllable. Examples illustrate: 'Zaydun qā’imun' has two utterances (words), 'Mādhā' has two syllables, 'Yā Zaydu' has one syllable, and 'darā' is used in address.

Key Terms
كلام
لفظ
حرف
مقطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sound produced by the mouth, including grunts and animal noises, is considered speech (kalām).
Correction: Kalām is specifically defined as ordered heard distinct letters, which excludes non-articulate sounds.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'heard' in the definition of kalām exclude?
A) Written letters.
B) Imagined letters.
C) Silent internal speech.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Kalām is defined as an ordered collection of heard distinct letters, excluding written, imagined, and non-human sounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your speech is defined by ordered heard letters, but your heart is known to Allah even without words. Have you ever considered that your silent thoughts are also accountable? How does this definition challenge you to align your inner thoughts with the truth you profess?

Divisions of Speech: Simple and Compound, and the Distinction between Kalām and Lafẓ
Al-Taftazani divides speech into meaningless/meaningful, simple/compound, and clarifies lafẓ usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical divisions of kalām into meaningless and meaningful, and within meaningful into simple (noun, verb, particle) and compound (complete and incomplete), and the different technical uses of the terms kalām and lafẓ.

Core Idea
Meaningful speech divides into simple words and compound sentences, with further subtypes.

Having defined speech as ordered heard letters, al-Taftazani now presents its classification. He divides speech into meaningless (muhmal) and meaningful (mawḍū‘). Meaningful speech then splits into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab). Simple divides into noun, verb, and particle; compound divides into complete (tāmm) and incomplete. He also clarifies the relationship between the terms kalām and lafẓ, showing that lafẓ is more general and that kalām can be narrowly defined as a meaningful utterance conveying a proposition.

Guided Path
1
Examine the classification tree
Read the Arabic passage and note the hierarchy: kalām → muhmal / mawḍū‘; mawḍū‘ → mufrad / murakkab; mufrad → ism, fi‘l, ḥarf; murakkab → tāmm / ghayr tāmm.
Draw a tree diagram.
2
Understand the subdivisions
Murakkab tāmm is a complete sentence that can be paused upon, like 'Zaydun qā’imun'. Murakkab ghayr tāmm is incomplete, like a phrase.
3
Differentiate kalām and lafẓ
See that lafẓ is more general than kalām. Also note the alternative specifications: sometimes kalām is meaningful utterance indicating a relation, and lafẓ is composed of syllables.
This shows terminological variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
