Answering Objections on Mental Forms: The Principle of Different Considerations
Al-Taftazani responds to three objections and provides a systematic defense using iʿtibār and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends the correspondence between mental forms and external substances by distinguishing between different aspects of consideration and by drawing an analogy between mental acquisition and external existence.

Core Idea
A mental form can be both an accident and a substance — under different considerations, there is no contradiction.

Al-Taftazani here confronts several objections against the theory of knowledge as mental representation. Critics argue that an accident (the mental form) cannot correspond to a substance, nor be universal, and that al-Taftazani is inconsistent in defining knowledge. His answer masterfully employs the logical tool of iʿtibār (consideration) and an illuminating analogy. Let us follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
The passage begins with three objections. Identify each: (1) accident vs. substance, (2) particular vs. universal, (3) definitional inconsistency. Notice how they target apparent contradictions.
The objections are phrased as 'among them [the objections]' and 'also'.
2
Grasp the principle of different considerations
Al-Taftazani's core answer is that a single thing can have multiple descriptions under different aspects (iʿtibārāt). Focus on how he applies this to the accident/substance and particular/universal objections.
He distinguishes between existence in the mind and existence in the external world.
3
Understand the analogy with existence and quiddity
He compares the relation of acquisition (ḥuṣūl) to the mental form with the relation of existence (wujūd) to quiddity (māhīyah). This clarifies the ontological status of mental forms.
The form in the intellect is the same quiddity that exists externally, but with an accidental attribute of 'being acquired'.
4
Reflect on the coherence of al-Taftazani's epistemology
Consider how this answer preserves both the intentionality of knowledge (correspondence) and its psychological reality (accident in the soul). How does this strengthen his critique of the skeptics?
This is a sophisticated solution that avoids both naive realism and skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الصورة العلمية عرض قائم بالنفس وقد جعلتموها مطابقة للموجود العيني الذي ربما يكون جوهراً، بل نفس ماهيته ، وامتناع كون العرض مطابقاً للجوهر، ونفس ماهيته معلوم بالضرورة وأيضاً : جعلتموها كلية مع أن كون العرض القائم بالنفس الجزئية جزئياً ضروري وأيضاً تجعلون العلم تارة حصول الصورة، وتارة نفس الصورة مع ظهور الفرق بينهما . والجواب: أن الممتنع هو كون الشيء الواحد باعتبار واحد جوهراً ،وعرضاً، أو كلياً وجزئياً، وأما عند اختلاف الاعتبار فلا، فإن كون الصورة العقلية عرضاً من حيث كونها في الحال قائمة بالموضوع الذي هو النفس لا ينافي كونها جوهراً من حيث إنها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، وإنما المستحيل كون الشيء جوهراً وعرضاً في الخارج بمعنى كونها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع ولا في موضوع، وكذا كونها جزئية من حيث قيامها بالنفس الجزئية لا ينافي كليتها من حيث مطابقتها للأفراد الكثيرة، بمعنى أن الحاصل في العقل من كل منها عند التجريد عن العوارض تكون تلك الصورة بعينها، ثم نسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهية في الخارج، فكماله[؟] ليس للماهية تحقق في الخارج، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود تحقق آخر حتى يجتمعا اجتماع القابل والمقبول، كذلك ليس للصورة تحقق في العقل، ولعارضها المسمى بالحصول تحقق آخر، بل الصورة في العقل هي نفس الماهية التي لها تحقق في الخارج، ولها عارض هو الحصول، ولو تقارنا في الصدق لما كان بينهما تقدم وتأخر.
And among them [the objections]: The scientific form is an accident subsisting in the soul, and you have made it corresponding to the external existent which may be a substance, indeed the very quiddity of it, and the impossibility of an accident corresponding to a substance, and to its very quiddity, is known necessarily. Also: You have made it universal while that an accident subsisting in a particular soul is necessarily particular. Also: You make knowledge sometimes the acquisition of the form, and sometimes the form itself, despite the apparent difference between them. The answer: What is impossible is that one thing under one consideration be both substance and accident, or universal and particular. But under different considerations it is not. For the mental form being an accident inasmuch as it is at present subsisting in the subject which is the soul does not contradict its being a substance inasmuch as it is a quiddity which, if it existed externally, would not be in a subject. Rather the impossible is that a thing be substance and accident in the external world, meaning that its quiddity, if it existed externally, would be in a subject and not in a subject. Likewise, its being particular in terms of its subsistence in a particular soul does not contradict its universality in terms of its correspondence to many individuals, meaning that what is obtained in the intellect from each of them, upon abstraction from accidents, is that same form. Then the relation of acquisition to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence to the quiddity in the external world. So likewise[?], the quiddity has no actuality in the external world, and its accident called existence has another actuality such that they combine as the combination of recipient and received. Similarly, the form has no actuality in the intellect, and its accident called acquisition has another actuality. Rather the form in the intellect is the very quiddity which has actuality in the external world, and it has an accident which is acquisition. And if they coincide in truth, there is no priority or posteriority between them.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections concerning the nature of the mental form (al-ṣūrah al-ʿilmiyyah) and al-Taftazani's comprehensive answer. The objections are: (1) The mental form is an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in the soul, yet it is said to correspond to an external substance; but an accident cannot correspond to a substance, much less to its very quiddity. (2) The mental form is said to be universal, but as an accident inhering in a particular soul it must be particular. (3) Al-Taftazani sometimes defines knowledge as the acquisition of the form and sometimes as the form itself, an apparent inconsistency. Al-Taftazani answers by appealing to the principle of different considerations (iʿtibārāt). The same thing can be considered under different aspects without contradiction. For instance, the mental form is an accident when considered as subsisting in the soul (its mode of existence in the mind), but it corresponds to a substance when considered as a quiddity (māhīyah) that, if existing externally, would exist not in a subject. The impossibility only arises if the same thing, under the same consideration, is both substance and accident externally. Similarly, the form is particular in its existence (as an accident in a particular soul) but universal in its meaning (its correspondence to many individuals) after abstraction from accidents. Thus, the same form (intentionally) is obtained from each individual. Then al-Taftazani draws an analogy: the relation of mental acquisition (ḥuṣūl) to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence (wujūd) to the quiddity in the external world. Just as the quiddity has no actuality in external existence apart from its accident of existence—they combine as recipient and received—so too the form has no actuality in the intellect apart from its accident of acquisition. But crucially, the form in the intellect is the very quiddity that has actuality externally, with acquisition as an accident. And if they coincide in truth, there is no priority or posteriority between them; the form and its acquisition are simultaneous.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental forms cannot correspond to external substances because they are accidents in the soul.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that a mental form is an accident in its mode of existence in the mind but represents a quiddity that would be a substance externally; different considerations remove the contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani resolve the objection that a mental form as an accident cannot correspond to an external substance?
A) He denies that mental forms are accidents
B) He argues that the form is considered an accident only in the mind and a substance in its external quiddity
C) He claims that substances can be accidents in the mind
D) He says the objection is based on a misunderstanding of correspondence

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends the correspondence of mental forms to external substances by distinguishing between the form's mental mode of existence and its quidditative nature, employing the logical principle of different considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction in your faith or understanding, do you honestly search for a different consideration that might resolve it, or do you simply reject the truth? Examine your heart: is there an area where you have dismissed a truth because you refused to change your perspective?
?
Al-Taftazani teaches that the mental form in your intellect is the very quiddity of the thing known. When you claim to know God through His attributes, is your knowledge truly a real correspondence to His essence, or are you content with a vague mental image that you have never rigorously tested against revelation?
?
The acquisition of knowledge (ḥuṣūl) is distinct from the form itself. In your own learning, do you often mistake the process (reading, memorizing) for actual understanding? How might this confusion account for a lack of certainty and transformation in your life?

The Three Objections Against Mental Forms
Taftazani enumerates three objections against mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key objections raised against the theory that knowledge is a mental form.

Core Idea
Mental forms cannot be both accidents in the soul and universal representations of substances.

Before Taftazani presents his defense, he first lays out the strongest objections against the theory of mental forms. These objections challenge the coherence of claiming that an accident in the soul can correspond to an external substance, be universal, and be both the form and its acquisition. Understanding these objections is essential to appreciating the subtlety of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three distinct objections.
Pay attention to the phrasing of each objection.
2
Identify the three objections
List the three objections: substance-accident mismatch, universality of accident, and duality of knowledge.
Consider why each objection seems powerful.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Notice that all objections assume mental forms must have the same properties as external objects.
This assumption is what the answer will challenge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاضر الذي لا تلتفت إليه النفس، مدركاً، وليس كذلك، ومنها أن الصورة العلمية عرض قائم بالنفس وقد جعلتموها مطابقة للموجود العيني الذي ربما يكون جوهراً، بل نفس ماهيته، وامتناع كون العرض مطابقاً للجوهر، ونفس ماهيته معلوم بالضرورة وأيضاً: جعلتموها كلية مع أن كون العرض القائم بالنفس الجزئية جزئياً ضروري وأيضاً تجعلون العلم تارة حصول الصورة، وتارة نفس الصورة مع ظهور الفرق بينهما.
والجواب: أن الممتنع هو كون الشيء الواحد باعتبار واحد جوهراً وعرضاً، أو كلياً وجزئياً، وأما عند اختلاف الاعتبار فلا، فإن كون الصورة العقلية عرضاً من حيث كونها في الحال قائمة بالموضوع الذي هو النفس لا ينافي كونها جوهراً من حيث إنها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، وإنما المستحيل كون الشيء جوهراً وعرضاً في الخارج بمعنى كونها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع ولا في موضوع، وكذا كونها جزئية من حيث قيامها بالنفس الجزئية لا ينافي كليتها من حيث مطابقتها للأفراد الكثيرة، بمعنى أن الحاصل في العقل من كل منها عند التجريد عن العوارض تكون تلك الصورة بعينها، ثم نسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهية في الخارج، فكماله ليس للماهية تحقق في الخارج، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود تحقق آخر حتى يجتمعا اجتماع القابل والمقبول، كذلك ليس للصورة تحقق في العقل، ولعارضها المسمى بالحصول تحقق آخر، وإنما الزيادة بمعنى أن المفهوم من هذا غير المفهوم من ذاك، فبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل العلم تارة نفس الصورة، وتارة حصولها.
And among them is that the mental form is an accident subsisting in the soul, and you have made it corresponding to the external existent which may be a substance, indeed the very quiddity of it, and the impossibility of an accident corresponding to a substance, and the very quiddity of it is known necessarily. Also: you have made it universal while that an accident subsisting in the particular soul is necessarily particular. Also you sometimes make knowledge the acquisition of the form, and sometimes the form itself, despite the apparent difference between them. The answer: That what is impossible is for one single thing in one respect to be substance and accident, or universal and particular; but when the respect differs, it is not. For the mental form being an accident in respect of its subsistence in the subject which is the soul does not preclude it from being a substance in respect of its being a quiddity which, if it existed externally, would not be in a subject. The impossible is only for a thing to be substance and accident in the external world in the sense that its quiddity, if it existed externally, would both be in a subject and not in a subject. Similarly, its being particular in respect of its subsistence in the particular soul does not preclude its universality in respect of its correspondence to many individuals, meaning that what is obtained in the intellect from each of them upon abstraction from accidents is that same form. Then the relation of acquisition to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence to quiddity in the external world. For just as quiddity has no realization in the external world, and its accident called existence has another realization, such that the former does not combine the way the recipient and the received combine, so too the form has no realization in the intellect, and its accident called acquisition has another realization; the addition is only that the concept of this is other than the concept of that. In this respect it is valid to make knowledge sometimes the form itself and sometimes its acquisition.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the theory that knowledge is a mental form, followed by a detailed answer. The objections are: (1) How can a mental accident correspond to an external substance? (2) How can an accident in the particular soul be universal? (3) How can knowledge be both the form and its acquisition? The answer explains that impossibility only arises when the same thing is considered in the same respect; different respects remove the contradiction. The mental form is an accident in its subsistence in the soul, but a substance in terms of its quiddity if it existed externally. Similarly, it is particular as subsisting in a particular soul, but universal in that the same abstracted form corresponds to many individuals. The relation of acquisition to the form is like existence to quiddity: just as quiddity and existence are distinct concepts but not separate entities, acquisition and the form are distinct concepts but not separate realities. Thus it is permissible to call knowledge the form or its acquisition depending on the respect.

The three objections challenge the consistency of mental forms but rely on confusing the respects in which properties are attributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you 'know' something, do you treat your mental image of it as the thing itself? How does the first objection—that an accident cannot correspond to a substance—expose a potential deception in your everyday confidence about what you know?
?
The third objection points to a sloppy conflation: knowledge is both the acquisition of a form and the form itself. In your study of ‘aqeedah, are there concepts you have blurred together to avoid the hard work of precise distinction? Will you commit to clarity even when it is uncomfortable?
?
You claim to know universal truths—like the oneness of Allah. But the second objection says every form in your particular soul must be particular. How can you be certain that your knowledge of tawḥīd is not merely a particular mental accident? What makes it truly universal?

The Answer: Distinction of Respects
Taftazani provides a comprehensive answer to the three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani resolves the objections by distinguishing the respects in which mental forms are described.

Core Idea
A mental form can be an accident in the soul yet a substance in quiddity when considered in different respects.

Taftazani answers the objections by invoking a critical principle: impossibility only arises when the same thing is considered in the same respect. By carefully distinguishing how the mental form relates to the soul versus how it corresponds to external reality, he dissolves each apparent contradiction. This answer not only defends the theory but deepens our understanding of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read the first part of the answer where Taftazani states the general rule about impossibility requiring the same respect.
The principle is that contradiction only occurs when the same thing is considered in the same respect.
2
Apply to substance/accident
See how the mental form is an accident in the soul but a substance in terms of its quiddity.
Different respects remove the contradiction.
3
Apply to particular/universal
Notice that the form is particular in its subsistence but universal in its correspondence to many individuals.
The same form is abstracted from multiple individuals.
4
Examine the analogy with existence and quiddity
Understand the comparison that acquisition is to form as existence is to quiddity, explaining why knowledge can refer to either.
Just as existence and quiddity are conceptually distinct but not separate realities, so too acquisition and form.

And among them is that the mental form is an accident subsisting in the soul, and you have made it corresponding to the external existent which may be a substance, indeed the very quiddity of it, and the impossibility of an accident corresponding to a substance, and the very quiddity of it is known necessarily. Also: you have made it universal while that an accident subsisting in the particular soul is necessarily particular. Also you sometimes make knowledge the acquisition of the form, and sometimes the form itself, despite the apparent difference between them. The answer: That what is impossible is for one single thing in one respect to be substance and accident, or universal and particular; but when the respect differs, it is not. For the mental form being an accident in respect of its subsistence in the subject which is the soul does not preclude it from being a substance in respect of its being a quiddity which, if it existed externally, would not be in a subject. The impossible is only for a thing to be substance and accident in the external world in the sense that its quiddity, if it existed externally, would both be in a subject and not in a subject. Similarly, its being particular in respect of its subsistence in the particular soul does not preclude its universality in respect of its correspondence to many individuals, meaning that what is obtained in the intellect from each of them upon abstraction from accidents is that same form. Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the theory that knowledge is a mental form, followed by a detailed answer. The objections are: (1) How can a mental accident correspond to an external substance? (2) How can an accident in the particular soul be universal? (3) How can knowledge be both the form and its acquisition? The answer explains that impossibility only arises when the same thing is considered in the same respect; different respects remove the contradiction. The mental form is an accident in its subsistence in the soul, but a substance in terms of its quiddity if it existed externally. Similarly, it is particular as subsisting in a particular soul, but universal in that the same abstracted form corresponds to many individuals. The relation of acquisition to the form is like existence to quiddity: just as quiddity and existence are distinct concepts but not separate entities, acquisition and the form are distinct concepts but not separate realities. Thus it is permissible to call knowledge the form or its acquisition depending on the respect.

?

The Objection: Knowledge as an External Accident
The objection that knowledge is an existing accident in the soul, unlike a form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge is an accident existing in the soul and how it challenges the view that perception is a form.

Core Idea
The objection claims knowledge is an external accident, not a mere form.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a strong objection against the theory that perception is the acquisition of a form. The objection argues that knowledge is undoubtedly an accident existing externally, because it involves an essential acquisition in the soul, unlike a form which only exists in the soul without such attribution. Let's examine the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice its concise but packed structure.
The objection is only a few lines but contains a clear argument.
2
Identify the key point
Identify the distinction between knowledge as an accident (with essential acquisition) and form as lacking such acquisition.
The basis is the nature of 'husul' (acquisition).
3
Understand the implication
Realize that this objection, if valid, would refute the sufficiency of form for perception.
Taftazani acknowledges its strength in the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل لا ارتياب في أن العلم عرض موجود في الخارج، لمعنى: حصوله في النفس، حصولاً متأصلاً موجباً للاتصاف كسائر صفات النفس. والصورة ليست كذلك إذ لا حصول لها إلا في النفس، وحصولها فيها ليس حصولاً اتصافياً مثل حصول العرض في المحل، على ما سبق.
قلنا: لا كلام في قوة هذا الإشكال بل أكثر الإشكالات الموردة على كون الإدراك صورة وغاية ما يمكن أن يقال: إن الصورة قد تؤخذ من حيث إن الحصول نفسها، فتكون عرضاً قائماً بالنفس حاصلاً لها حصولاً متأصلاً اتصافياً، فيكون موجوداً عينياً كسائر صفاتها، وقد تؤخذ من حيث إن الحصول غيرها فيكون صورة وماهية للموجود العيني الذي ربما يكون من الجواهر فلا تتصف النفس بها، ولا هي تحصل للنفس حصولاً متأصلاً، وهي بهذا الاعتبار مفهوم لا تحقق له إلا في الذهن وإطلاق المعلوم عليها تجوز لأن المعلوم ماله صورة في العقل، لا نفس الصورة، نعم: قد يستأنف لها تعقل، وتلحقها أحكام وعوارض لا يحاذي بها أمر في الخارج هي بالمعقولات الثانية وبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل الكلية من عوارض المعلوم كما تجعل من عوارض المفهوم.
وأما المعلوم الذي هو ماله الصورة أعني الموجود العيني فلا يتصف بالكلية، إلا بمعنى أن الحاصل منه في العقل كلي.
وذكر في المواقف عن الحكماء أن لموجود في الذهن هو العلم والمعلوم، وأن معنى كون الإنسان كلياً هو أن الصورة الحصلة منه في العقل
For if it is said: There is no doubt that knowledge is an accident existing in the external world, meaning its acquisition in the soul, an essential acquisition that entails attribution like other attributes of the soul. But the form is not like that, for it has no acquisition except in the soul, and its acquisition in it is not an attributive acquisition like the acquisition of an accident in its locus, as previously mentioned. We say: There is no contesting the strength of this objection; indeed most of the objections raised against [the theory that] perception is a form [are strong]. The utmost that can be said is: The form may be taken in the sense that the acquisition is itself the form, in which case it is an accident subsisting in the soul, acquired by it with an essential attributive acquisition, so it is an external existent like other attributes of the soul. Or it may be taken in the sense that the acquisition is other than it, in which case it is a form and quiddity of the external existent which may be among substances, so the soul is not described by it, nor is it acquired by the soul with an essential acquisition. In this respect it is a concept that has no realization except in the mind, and applying the term 'known' to it is metaphorical, for the known is that which has a form in the intellect, not the form itself. Indeed, intellection may be initiated for it, and it may be subject to judgments and accidents that do not correspond to anything external; these are called secondary intelligibles. In this respect it is valid to make universality an accident of the known just as it is made an accident of the concept. As for the known which is that which has the form, i.e., the external existent, it is not described by universality except in the sense that what is obtained from it in the intellect is universal. And it is mentioned in al-Mawaqif, on the authority of the philosophers, that the existent in the mind is knowledge and the known, and that the meaning of 'man' being universal is that the form obtained from him in the intellect [is universal].
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objection claims that knowledge is undoubtedly an accident existing externally because it is an essential acquisition in the soul, like other attributes; the form (sura) is not such because its acquisition is not attributive. The answer concedes the strength of the objection but clarifies that the form can be considered in two ways. First, when acquisition (husul) is identical to the form, then the form itself is an accident in the soul, an essential attribute. Second, when acquisition is distinct from the form, the form is a quiddity of an external substance, not an attribute of the soul; it is a mental concept, and calling it 'known' is metaphorical. Such forms can be subject to secondary intelligibles like universality. Thus universality applies to the mental concept, not the external existent. The passage concludes by citing the philosophers from al-Mawaqif: the mental existent is both knowledge and the known, and the universality of man means the mental form obtained is universal.

Key Terms
عرض
حصول
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that knowledge is a form residing in the soul without any external reality.
Correction: The objection argues that knowledge is an accident existing in the external world (fi al-kharij) because it involves essential attribution, unlike a form which is merely mental.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for claiming that knowledge is an accident existing externally?
A) Knowledge has no acquisition in the soul.
B) Knowledge has essential acquisition in the soul.
C) Knowledge is a form.
D) Knowledge is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the form theory by asserting that knowledge, unlike form, is an accident with essential acquisition in the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new truth about Allah, do you treat it like a form—a picture in your mind you can set aside—or like an accident that truly describes your soul, changing how you live? How can you tell the difference tonight?
?
If knowledge is an essential acquisition that entails attribution, what does your current state of knowledge (or lack of it) reveal about your soul right now? Pause and pick one piece of Islamic knowledge you have merely 'stored' as a form; what would it take for it to become a real attribute of your soul?
?
The objector distinguishes attributive acquisition from mere presence. Before you sleep, examine your knowledge of tawhid: is it merely present in your mind, or does it truly describe your soul? What one action today can transform that knowledge from a form into an accident that defines you?

Taftazani's Twofold Answer: Form as Accident or Mental Concept
Taftazani's twofold answer: form as accident or mental concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani answers the objection by distinguishing two ways of considering the form: as identical to its acquisition or as distinct from it.

Core Idea
The form can be an accident or a mental concept depending on how its acquisition is viewed.

Taftazani begins his answer by acknowledging the strength of the objection. He then offers a nuanced response: the form can be considered in two ways. When the acquisition (husul) is identical to the form, it is an accident in the soul. When the acquisition is distinct, the form is a quiddity of an external entity and only a mental concept. This distinction resolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire answer
Read the Arabic and translation of the answer. Notice it has several parts: acknowledgment, two ways, secondary intelligibles, and a concluding citation.
The answer is denser than the objection.
2
Identify the two ways
Find the two cases: (a) acquisition = form → accident; (b) acquisition ≠ form → mental concept.
These are the core of the answer.
3
Understand secondary intelligibles
Note how the form as a mental concept can have attributes like universality, which are secondary intelligibles.
This links to broader issues in epistemology.
4
Appreciate the resolution
See how this answer preserves the form theory while addressing the objection.
The conclusion from al-Mawaqif reinforces the mental existent as both knowledge and known.

For if it is said: There is no doubt that knowledge is an accident existing in the external world, meaning its acquisition in the soul, an essential acquisition that entails attribution like other attributes of the soul. But the form is not like that, for it has no acquisition except in the soul, and its acquisition in it is not an attributive acquisition like the acquisition of an accident in its locus, as previously mentioned. The utmost that can be said is: The form may be taken in the sense that the acquisition is itself the form, in which case it is an accident subsisting in the soul, acquired by it with an essential attributive acquisition, so it is an external existent like other attributes of the soul. Or it may be taken in the sense that the acquisition is other than it, in which case it is a form and quiddity of the external existent which may be among substances, so the soul is not described by it, nor is it acquired by the soul with an essential acquisition. In this respect it is a concept that has no realization except in the mind, and applying the term 'known' to it is metaphorical, for the known is that which has a form in the intellect, not the form itself. Indeed, intellection may be initiated for it, and it may be subject to judgments and accidents that do not correspond to anything external; these are called secondary intelligibles. And it is mentioned in al-Mawaqif, on the authority of the philosophers, that the existent in the mind is knowledge and the known, and that the meaning of 'man' being universal is that the form obtained from him in the intellect [is universal].

The passage concludes by citing the philosophers from al-Mawaqif: the mental existent is both knowledge and the known, and the universality of man means the mental form obtained is universal.

The Dual Consideration of Knowledge
Sanusi presents the dual consideration of knowledge and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how knowledge can be considered both as the form itself and as its acquisition, and how this resolves an objection about the nature of form.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not a single entity—it can be viewed as form or acquisition.

The author begins by stating that the concept of knowledge can be understood in two distinct ways: as the form itself or as the acquisition of that form. He then anticipates a strong objection: if knowledge is an accident existing in the soul, how can form, which exists only in the soul, be an accident? His answer shows that form can be taken from two perspectives, each yielding a different ontological status. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the corresponding translation. Pay attention to the flow from statement to objection to answer.
The Arabic is dense—allow yourself to get a general sense first.
2
Identify the two considerations of knowledge
Find where the author says knowledge can be made 'the form itself' or 'its acquisition'. Understand what he means by 'increase' (al-ziyāda).
3
Trace the objection
Note the objection: knowledge is an accident in the soul, but form is not an accident because its acquisition in the soul is not attributive. Understand why this is a problem.
4
Study the answer
See how the author resolves the problem by distinguishing two perspectives on the form itself: when acquisition is identical to the form, it is an accident; when acquisition is other than the form, it is a quiddity for an external substance.
This is the key move—memorize the two perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالحصول تحقق آخر، وإنما الزيادة بمعنى أن المفهوم من هذا غير المفهوم من ذاك، فبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل العلم تارة نفس الصورة، وتارة حصولها . فإن قيل لا ارتياب في أن العلم عرض موجود في الخارج، لمعنى : حصوله في النفس، حصولاً متأصلاً موجباً للاتصاف كسائر صفات النفس . والصورة ليست كذلك إذ لا حصول لها إلا في النفس، وحصولها فيها ليس حصولاً اتصافياً مثل حصول العرض في المحل، على ما سبق . قلنا : لا كلام في قوة هذا الإشكال بل أكثر الإشكالات الموردة على كون الإدراك صورة وغاية ما يمكن أن يقال: إن الصورة قد تؤخذ من حيث إن الحصول نفسها، فتكون عرضاً قائماً بالنفس حاصلاً لها حصولاً متأصلاً اتصافياً، فيكون موجوداً عينياً كسائر صفاتها، وقد تؤخذ من حيث إن الحصول غيرها فيكون صورة وماهية للموجود العيني الذي ربما يكون من الجواهر فلا تتصف النفس بها، ولا هي تحصل للنفس حصولاً متأصلاً، وهي بهذا الاعتبار مفهوم لا تحقق له إلا في الذهن وإطلاق المعلوم عليها تجوز لأن المعلوم ماله صورة في العقل، لا نفس الصورة، نعم : قد يستأنف لها تعقل، وتلحقها أحكام وعوارض لا يحاذي بها أمر في الخارج هي بالمعقولات الثانية وبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل الكلية من عوارض المعلوم كما تجعل من عوارض المفهوم . وأما المعلوم الذي هو ماله الصورة أعني الموجود العيني فلا يتصف بالكلية، إلا بمعنى أن الحاصل منه في العقل كلي .
By acquisition another verification [occurs]; rather the increase means that the concept from this is other than the concept from that. So in this regard it is valid to make knowledge sometimes the form itself, and sometimes its acquisition. If it is said: there is no doubt that knowledge is an accident existing externally, by the meaning: its acquisition in the soul, an rooted acquisition causing attribution like other attributes of the soul. And the form is not like that, since it has no acquisition except in the soul, and its acquisition in it is not an attributive acquisition like the acquisition of the accident in the subject, as previously. We say: there is no talk about the strength of this difficulty; rather most of the difficulties raised against perception being a form [are strong]. And the best that can be said: the form may be taken from the perspective that the acquisition is itself, so it becomes an accident subsisting by the soul, acquired for it in a rooted attributive acquisition, being an external existent like its other attributes; and it may be taken from the perspective that the acquisition is other than it, so it becomes a form and quiddity for the external existent which perhaps is from substances, so the soul is not qualified by it, nor is it acquired for the soul in a rooted acquisition. And in this regard it is a concept with no realization except in the mind, and applying 'known' to it is a metaphor because the known is that which has a form in the intellect, not the form itself. Indeed, intellection may be newly initiated for it, and rulings and accidents attach to it that do not correspond to something external—they are called secondary intelligibles. And in this regard it is valid to make universality an attribute of the known just as it is made an attribute of the concept. As for the known which is that which has the form, i.e., the external existent, it is not qualified by universality, except in the sense that what is obtained from it in the intellect is universal.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced discussion on the nature of knowledge (al-‘ilm) and its relation to the form (al-ṣūrah). The author begins by stating that knowledge can be considered in two ways: as the form itself, or as its acquisition (al-ḥuṣūl). The increase in meaning is that the concept of 'knowledge' differs depending on which aspect is taken. Then an objection is raised: knowledge is an accident (ʻaraḍ) existing externally, because it occurs in the soul as a rooted acquisition bringing about attribution, like other attributes of the soul. The form, however, is not an accident in this sense, since it only exists in the soul and its acquisition there is not attributive. The author acknowledges the strength of this objection but offers a resolution: the form can be taken from two perspectives. First, if the acquisition is considered identical to the form, then the form is an accident subsisting by the soul, an external existent. Second, if the acquisition is considered other than the form, then the form is a quiddity for an external substance, not an attribute of the soul, but a concept in the mind. It is thus called 'known' metaphorically. This leads to a discussion of universals: universality can be an attribute of the known (as a mental concept) but not of the external existent, which is particular. The external existent is only universal in the sense that the mental form derived from it is universal.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
الصورة (form)
الحصول (acquisition)
العرض (accident)
المعلوم (the known)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge must be either exclusively a form or exclusively an accident with no middle ground.
Correction: The author shows that knowledge can be considered in two ways, each valid in its own context, resolving the apparent conflict.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when is the form considered an accident?
A) When it is taken as the quiddity of an external substance
B) When the acquisition is taken to be identical to the form
C) When the acquisition is other than the form
D) When it is a mere concept in the mind

Key Takeaway
Knowledge can be validly considered either as the form itself (an accident) or as its acquisition (a mental concept), depending on the perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know that Allah exists,' do you experience that knowledge as a quality that changes your soul, or as a mere mental picture? How would your dua change if you felt it as an accident qualifying your very self?
?
You have learned that knowledge can be considered as a form that does not attribute to the soul. Is there any knowledge you possess that you treat as just a mental concept—never letting it actually affect your character? Will you let it become a real attribute of your soul today?

The Universal and Secondary Intelligibles
Sanusi explains the metaphorical use of 'known', secondary intelligibles, and the attribution of universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the known as a mental concept and as an external existent, and how universality and secondary intelligibles are attributed.

Core Idea
Universality belongs to mental concepts, not to external objects.

Having established the dual nature of knowledge, the author now addresses a further nuance: the term 'known' (al-ma‘lūm) is applied metaphorically to mental concepts, because the true known is the external thing that has a form in the intellect. He then introduces secondary intelligibles—concepts that have no external counterpart—and explains that universality is an attribute of mental concepts, not of external existents except in a derived sense. This lesson unpacks these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the second half of the source passage, starting from 'وإطلاق المعلوم عليها تجوز'. Read the Arabic and then the translation carefully.
2
Understand the metaphor of 'known'
The author explains that calling a mental concept 'known' is metaphorical—what is truly known is the external thing that has a form in the mind, not the form itself.
3
Recognize secondary intelligibles
These are concepts that have no external counterpart; they are purely mental and can be subject to further intellection and rulings.
Examples include universals like 'humanity'.
4
Apply the distinction to universality
Universality can be attributed to mental concepts (the known in the sense of the form) but not to external existents, except in the sense that the mental form derived from them is universal.
This resolves the puzzle of how something particular can be known universally.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

By acquisition another verification [occurs]; rather the increase means that the concept from this is other than the concept from that. So in this regard it is valid to make knowledge sometimes the form itself, and sometimes its acquisition. If it is said: there is no doubt that knowledge is an accident existing externally, by the meaning: its acquisition in the soul, an rooted acquisition causing attribution like other attributes of the soul. And the form is not like that, since it has no acquisition except in the soul, and its acquisition in it is not an attributive acquisition like the acquisition of the accident in the subject, as previously. We say: there is no talk about the strength of this difficulty; rather most of the difficulties raised against perception being a form [are strong]. And the best that can be said: the form may be taken from the perspective that the acquisition is itself, so it becomes an accident subsisting by the soul, acquired for it in a rooted attributive acquisition, being an external existent like its other attributes; and it may be taken from the perspective that the acquisition is other than it, so it becomes a form and quiddity for the external existent which perhaps is from substances, so the soul is not qualified by it, nor is it acquired for the soul in a rooted acquisition. And in this regard it is a concept with no realization except in the mind, and applying 'known' to it is a metaphor because the known is that which has a form in the intellect, not the form itself. Indeed, intellection may be newly initiated for it, and rulings and accidents attach to it that do not correspond to something external—they are called secondary intelligibles. And in this regard it is valid to make universality an attribute of the known just as it is made an attribute of the concept. As for the known which is that which has the form, i.e., the external existent, it is not qualified by universality, except in the sense that what is obtained from it in the intellect is universal.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced discussion on the nature of knowledge (al-‘ilm) and its relation to the form (al-ṣūrah). The author begins by stating that knowledge can be considered in two ways: as the form itself, or as its acquisition (al-ḥuṣūl). The increase in meaning is that the concept of 'knowledge' differs depending on which aspect is taken. Then an objection is raised: knowledge is an accident (ʻaraḍ) existing externally, because it occurs in the soul as a rooted acquisition bringing about attribution, like other attributes of the soul. The form, however, is not an accident in this sense, since it only exists in the soul and its acquisition there is not attributive. The author acknowledges the strength of this objection but offers a resolution: the form can be taken from two perspectives. First, if the acquisition is considered identical to the form, then the form is an accident subsisting by the soul, an external existent. Second, if the acquisition is considered other than the form, then the form is a quiddity for an external substance, not an attribute of the soul, but a concept in the mind. It is thus called 'known' metaphorically. This leads to a discussion of universals: universality can be an attribute of the known (as a mental concept) but not of the external existent, which is particular. The external existent is only universal in the sense that the mental form derived from it is universal.

Key Terms
المعلوم (the known)
الكلي (universal)
المعقولات الثانية (secondary intelligibles)
الماهية (quiddity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality exists in external objects themselves (e.g., 'humanity' is a universal existing in the world).
Correction: The author explains that external existents are particular; universality is an attribute of the mental concept derived from them, not of the external thing.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it correct to say an external existent is universal?
A) Because it contains universal properties
B) Because the mental form obtained from it is universal
C) Because it is a secondary intelligible
D) Because it is a form in the intellect

Key Takeaway
Universality is a property of mental concepts, not of external objects; the external particular is only called universal because the mind abstracts a universal form from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat categories like 'justice' or 'beauty' as if they exist in the world independently? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
When you think of a concept like 'humanity', do you imagine it as a real thing existing outside of minds? What would it mean for your daily judgments if you truly internalized that universality is only in the mind?
?
Is your understanding of God's attributes (like 'The All-Knowing') based on a secondary intelligible? How does this lesson affect how you relate to those attributes in your worship?

The Philosophers' View on Mental Existence and the Universal
The author cites al-Mawaqif on the philosophers' view that the mental existent is knowledge and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that the mental existent is both knowledge and known, and the condition for universality being a mental attribute.

Core Idea
For the philosophers, universality belongs to mental forms, not external objects.

In this passage from al-Mawaqif, the author presents the philosophers' position on mental existence and universality. They argue that what exists in the mind is identical to knowledge and the known, and that universality is a property of mental forms stripped of particularizers. The passage also clarifies the assumptions required for this view to be coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the author's argument.
Notice the citations from al-Mawaqif.
2
Identify the two aspects of universality
The passage mentions two ways to understand 'man is universal': either the mental form is universal, or the known is universal.
3
Analyze the condition for validity
The author states that this view only works if knowledge is the mental form, or if there is an impression in something else. Otherwise the known would be particular.
Consider why external existence would make it particular.
4
Reflect on the author's critique
The author argues that if 'the known' means the mental form, then the two ways are identical and the distinction is pointless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في المواقف عن الحكماء أن الموجود في الذهن هو العلم والمعلوم، وأن معنى كون الإنسان كلياً هو أن الصورة الحاصلة منه في العقل المجردة عن المشخصات كلية أو أن المعلوم بها كلي ثم قال : وهذا إنما يصح على رأي من يجعل العلم والمعلوم هي الصورة الذهنية، أو يجعل للأمور المتصورة ارتساماً في غير العقل وإلا لكان للمعلوم حصول في الخارج فيكون جزئياً لا كلياً، وأنت خبير بأنه إذا أريد بالمعلوم الصورة الذهنية لم يكن بين الوجهين فرق ولا لقوله بها معنى.
And he mentioned in al-Mawaqif from the philosophers that the existent in the mind is knowledge and the known, and that the meaning of 'man is universal' is that the form obtained from him in the intellect, stripped of particularizers, is universal, or that the known by it is universal. Then he said: This is only valid according to the view of one who makes knowledge and the known the mental form, or who makes for the imagined things an impression in something other than the intellect; otherwise the known would have an acquisition in the external and would be particular, not universal. And you are aware that if the known is intended to be the mental form, there is no difference between the two aspects, and his saying has no meaning.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a citation from al-Mawaqif discussing the philosophers' position on mental existence. The philosophers assert that the existent in the mind (al-mawjūd fī al-dhihn) is both knowledge (al-‘ilm) and the known (al-ma‘lūm). They explain universality: when we say 'man is universal,' it means that the mental form of man, stripped of particularizing features, is universal, or alternatively, the known (the mental form) is universal. The author of al-Mawaqif then comments that this view is only coherent on one of two assumptions: either one holds that knowledge and the known are the mental form itself, or one posits that imagined things have an impression in something other than the intellect (like the external world). If neither holds, then the known would have an acquisition in the external world and would be particular, not universal. The author of our text adds a critical remark: if by 'known' one means the mental form, then the two ways of putting it are essentially identical, and the distinction is meaningless.

Key Terms
الموجود في الذهن (mental existent)
الصورة الذهنية (mental form)
الكلي (universal)
المشخصات (particularizers)
الارتسام (impression)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality is a property of external objects.
Correction: The philosophers locate universality in mental forms, not in external objects.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as cited, what is the condition for the statement that 'man is universal' to be valid?
A) That man exists externally as a universal
B) That the mental form is stripped of particularizers
C) That the known is the external existent
D) That the mind is a blank slate

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' view requires that universality is a property of mental forms, not external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of a universal concept like 'justice,' do you consider it as a mere mental construct or as something that exists externally? How does your view affect your pursuit of justice in daily life?
?
The philosophers argue that universality exists only in the mind stripped of particularizers. Where do you place the reality of your deepest beliefs—are they mental forms or grounded in external truth? How does that change how you hold them?
?
If the known is identical to the mental form, the distinction between two conditions collapses. Are there areas in your faith where you assume two separate truths are needed, when in fact they are one? What would it mean for your worship if you unified them?

The Mutakallimun's View on Perception and the Problem of Non-Existents
The mutakallimun's definition of perception and the problem of non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun's denial of mental existence leads to a difficulty in explaining knowledge of non-existent things.

Core Idea
Denying mental existence forces perception to be a relation, but non-existents have no relation.

The mutakallimun rejected the notion of mental existence as a separate category. To explain perception, they defined it as a relation or a quality with a relation. But this creates a problem: how can we perceive or know things that do not exist? This passage begins by laying out their view and the difficulty that arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note the key terms: mental existence, perception, relation, non-existent, impossible.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the mutakallimun's definition of perception
Find where they say perception is either a relation or a quality with a relation. Understand why they defined it this way after denying mental existence.
This is the first part of the passage.
3
Spot the problem with non-existents
The passage says that knowing non-existents and impossibles becomes problematic because relation to something non-existent is inconceivable. Reflect on this objection.
Relation requires two relata; one missing makes relation impossible.
4
Understand the forced conclusion
Despite denying mental existence, they were forced to say there is a 'form' for every perception because perception is a single meaning. This is a concession to the problem.
This form is not mental existence but a different concept.
5
Summarize the dilemma
Summarize in your own words: the mutakallimun's position leads to a contradiction: they deny mental existence but need something in the mind for knowledge of non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والمتكلمون لما أنكروا الوجود الذهني جعلوا الإدراك إضافة بين المدرك والمدرك، أو صفة لها إضافة إليه فورد عليهم العلم بالمعدومات والممتنعات إذ لا تعقل الإضافة إلى ما تحقق له أصلاً، ولزم القول بالصورة في الكل لما أن الإدراك معنى واحد فإن قيل : كما لا إضافة إلى العدم المحض فكذا لا صورة له وإن أخذت صورة لما في الذهن كان في الذهن من المعدوم أمران الصورة وذو الصورة وهو بين البطلان . قلنا : ليس من المعدوم إلا الصورة ومعناها أن له وجوداً غير متأصل، وهي من حيث قيامها بالذهن علم، ومن حيث ذاتها معلوم بخلاف الموجود فإن العلم ما في الذهن والمعلوم ما في الخارج وفي كلام ابن سينا أنه ليس في العقل من الممتنع صورة وتصوره إما على سبيل التشبيه بإن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر وهو الاجتماع ، ثم يحكم بأن مثله لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض، أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم أن ليس بينهما مفهوم هو الاجتماع، وكأنه مراد أبي هاشم حيث أثبت علماً لا معلوماً له).
He said: And the mutakallimun, when they denied mental existence, made perception either a relation between the perceiver and the perceived, or a quality having that relation, so there arose for them the problem of knowledge of non-existents and impossibles, since relation to something with no realization at all is inconceivable; and they were forced to assert the form in everything because perception is a single meaning. If it is said: just as there is no relation to pure non-existence, so too there is no form for it; and if a form is taken for what is in the mind, then in the mind of the non-existent there would be two things: the form and the possessor of the form, which is obviously false. We say: there is nothing of the non-existent except the form, and its meaning is that it has a non-rooted existence; and it, from the perspective of its subsistence in the mind, is knowledge, and from the perspective of its essence, is known; contrary to the existent, for knowledge is what is in the mind and the known is what is in the external. And in the speech of Ibn Sina: there is no form of the impossible in the intellect; its conception is either by way of analogy, like conceiving between blackness and sweetness a thing which is combination, then judging that a similar thing is not possible between blackness and whiteness; or by way of negation, like judging that there is not between them a concept which is combination. And it is as if this is the intent of Abu Hashim when he affirmed knowledge with no known.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's position on perception (al-idrāk) after they denied mental existence. Since they rejected forms in the mind, they made perception either a relation (iḍāfah) between the perceiver and the perceived, or a quality (ṣifah) having that relation. This leads to a problem: how can we know non-existent things (al-ma‘dūmāt) and impossibles (al-mumtani‘āt)? Relation to something that has no reality is inconceivable. Therefore, they were forced to assert that for every perception there is a form, because perception is a single uniform meaning. An objection is then raised: just as there is no relation to pure non-existence, so there is no form for it; if a form is posited for a non-existent, then in the mind there would be two things—the form and the thing itself—which is false. The author answers: of the non-existent, only the form exists; it has a non-rooted existence (wujūd ghayr muta’aṣṣil). As form subsisting in the mind, it is knowledge; as essence, it is known. This differs from external existents, where knowledge is in the mind and the known is outside. The author then cites Ibn Sina's view on conceiving impossibles: either by analogy (e.g., combining black and sweet, then judging similar combination impossible for black and white) or by negation (judging no concept of combination exists). This is similar to Abu Hashim's concept of 'knowledge with no known'.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الإدراك
الإضافة
المعدوم
الممتنع
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimun completely reject any mental representation of non-existents.
Correction: In fact, they are forced to affirm a 'form' for every perception, which functions as a mental representation even if they deny mental existence as a category.

Checkpoint
What problem arises from the mutakallimun's definition of perception as a relation?
A) It conflicts with the existence of the perceiver.
B) Knowledge of non-existent things becomes inconceivable because relation to nothing is impossible.
C) It implies that perception requires a form in the mind.
D) It leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's denial of mental existence forces them to treat perception as a relation, but this cannot account for knowledge of non-existents, compelling them to admit a form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your past deeds that have vanished, do you question whether that knowledge is real? This lesson proves that knowledge of non-existents requires a mental form that is not physical. How does this truth change the way you hold yourself accountable for actions that no longer exist?
?
Do you ever assume that only physical objects are 'real'? This lesson shows that perception of non-existents and impossibles is still meaningful and requires mental existence. If you deny mental existence, what happens to your ability to know Allah, who is not a physical being? Ponder: Is your knowledge of Him real?
?
This passage teaches that perception is one single meaning across all objects. If that is so, why do you allow some perceptions to affect your heart deeply while others, like the reality of the Hereafter, remain distant? What would it take for you to treat your knowledge of the unseen with the same gravity as what you see with your eyes?

The Solution: Non-Rooted Existence and Conception of Impossibles
The solution to the problem of non-existents: non-rooted existence and Ibn Sina's two methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the solution to the problem of knowing non-existents through the concept of non-rooted existence and Ibn Sina's two methods for conceiving impossibles.

Core Idea
Non-existents have a form with non-rooted existence; impossibles are conceived by analogy or negation.

In response to the objection that non-existents cannot have forms, the author clarifies that the form of a non-existent has a special status: a non-rooted existence. He then discusses Ibn Sina's account of how we can conceive impossible things, either by analogy or by negation. This passage resolves the earlier difficulty and provides a deeper understanding of mental content.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation for the objection and the author's response. Note the key claim: 'there is nothing of the non-existent except the form'.
The objection tries to show that positing a form leads to two things in the mind.
2
Understand non-rooted existence
The author explains that the form of a non-existent has a non-rooted existence (wujūd ghayr muta’aṣṣil). Grasp this distinction: it is existence that is not independent but subsists in the mind.
This avoids the problem of having two things in the mind.
3
Contrast with external existents
Notice the difference: for external existents, knowledge is in the mind and the known is outside; for non-existents, the form itself is both knowledge and known.
This is a key point.
4
Examine Ibn Sina's two methods
Read Ibn Sina's account: impossibles are conceived either by analogy (e.g., combining black and sweet, then judging impossible for black and white) or by negation (judging no combination). Understand how this allows conception without a direct form.
These methods avoid positing a form for the impossible.
5
Connect to Abu Hashim's view
The author notes that this resembles Abu Hashim's idea of 'knowledge with no known'. Reflect on how this fits with the overall solution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Takeaway
Non-existents are known through forms with non-rooted existence, and impossibles are conceived by analogy or negation, avoiding the need for mental forms.

Why Perception Cannot Be a Mere Relation
Refutes relational theory of perception and argues for mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relational account of perception fails to account for knowledge of non-existent things, and why mental forms must be affirmed.

Core Idea
Perception as a relation cannot explain knowledge of non-existents; mental forms must be real.

The mutakallimun who denied mental existence tried to define perception as a relation or a quality with a relation. But this leads to an immediate problem: how can we know things that do not exist at all? This passage opens by identifying that problem and concluding that mental forms are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two theories
The passage begins by stating that perception is either a relation or a quality. Read carefully to see which theory the mutakallimun adopted.
2
Identify the problem with the relational theory
The author points out that knowledge of non-existents and impossibles is problematic for the relational view because a relation requires two existing things.
Think about why a relation cannot hold to something that does not exist.
3
Understand why mental forms solve the problem
The passage concludes that mental forms are necessary for all perception because perception is a single meaning that applies to both existents and non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإدراك إما إضافة أو صفة لها قال : والمتكلمون لما أنكروا الوجود الذهني جعلوا الإدراك إضافة بين المدرك والمدرك، أو صفة لها إضافة إليه فورد عليهم العلم بالمعدومات والممتنعات إذ لا تعقل الإضافة إلى ما تحقق له أصلاً، ولزم القول بالصورة في الكل لما أن الإدراك معنى واحد فإن قيل : كما لا إضافة إلى العدم المحض فكذا لا صورة له وإن أخذت صورة لما في الذهن كان في الذهن من المعدوم أمران الصورة وذو الصورة وهو بين البطلان . قلنا : ليس من المعدوم إلا الصورة ومعناها أن له وجوداً غير متأصل، وهي من حيث قيامها بالذهن علم، ومن حيث ذاتها معلوم بخلاف الموجود فإن العلم ما في الذهن والمعلوم ما في الخارج وفي كلام ابن سينا أنه ليس في العقل من الممتنع صورة وتصوره إما على سبيل التشبيه بإن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر وهو الاجتماع ، ثم يحكم بأن مثله لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض، أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم أن ليس بينهما مفهوم هو الاجتماع، وكأنه مراد أبي هاشم حيث أثبت علماً لا معلوماً له).
Perception is either a relation or a quality having it. He said: When the mutakallimun denied mental existence, they made perception a relation between the perceiver and the perceived, or a quality having a relation to it. Then they are faced with knowledge of non-existents and impossibles, since a relation to what has no actual existence is inconceivable. And it becomes necessary to affirm the form in all cases because perception is a single meaning. If it is said: Just as there is no relation to pure non-existence, so there is no form for it. And if a form is taken for what is in the mind, then of the non-existent there would be two things in the mind: the form and the owner of the form, which is clearly false. We say: Of the non-existent there is only the form, and its meaning is that it has a non-essential existence. The form, as subsisting in the mind, is knowledge, and as an essence is known, unlike the existent, for knowledge is what is in the mind and the known is what is in the external. And in Ibn Sina's words, there is no form of the impossible in the mind, and its conception is either by way of analogy, as when one conceives between blackness and sweetness a thing which is combination, then judges that the like of it is not possible between blackness and whiteness, or by way of negation, as when one judges that there is no concept between them which is combination. And it is as if that is what Abu Hashim meant when he affirmed knowledge without a known object.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the mutakallimun who deny mental existence. It begins by stating the two possible accounts of perception: either a pure relation (idafa) between perceiver and perceived, or a quality (sifa) that has that relation. The mutakallimun chose the relational view, but this leads to a problem: knowledge of non-existent things (ma'dumat) and impossible things (mumtani'at) cannot be explained because a relation requires two existing relata. Therefore, to account for such knowledge, one must affirm that there is a form (sura) in the mind, i.e., mental existence. The author then considers an objection: just as there is no relation to pure non-existence, so there cannot be a form of non-existence, because a form requires a thing that has the form. The answer is that the non-existent has only a form, meaning it has a non-essential existence (wujud ghayr muta'asil). The form, as it subsists in the mind, is the act of knowledge (ilm), and as an essence (dhat) it is the known (ma'lum). In contrast, for an external existent, knowledge is the mental form, while the known is the external thing. The passage concludes with a reference to Ibn Sina's view that impossible things have no form in the mind; instead, they are conceived through analogy (tashbih) or negation (nafy). This might be what Abu Hashim intended by 'knowledge without a known object'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that perception as a relation works for all objects.
Correction: Relation requires existence of both relata, so it fails for non-existents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main problem with defining perception as a relation?
A) It violates the principle of non-contradiction.
B) It cannot account for knowledge of non-existent things.
C) It leads to infinite regress.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The relational theory of perception fails because it cannot explain our knowledge of non-existent things; hence, we must affirm mental forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of something you have never seen, like the Throne of Allah, do you realize that your mind contains a real form of it? How does this truth affect your wonder and awe? What would it mean if that form were not real, but merely a relation? Would your worship be less genuine?
?
You claim to know that there is no god but Allah. But what about knowing the nonexistence of false gods? Is that knowledge real in your heart? If perception were only a relation, that knowledge would be impossible. So what does that say about the state of your heart's conviction? Are you treating it as a real mental presence or just an abstract thought?
?
The mutakallimun denied mental existence to protect God's transcendence, but the author shows that leads to a loss of knowledge. Have you ever made a theological mistake to protect a doctrine, only to find it undermines something more fundamental? What is that one thing you are holding onto that, if you followed it through, would nullify your certainty about what you know? Are you ready to change?

Answering Objections: Mental Forms of Non-Existents and Ibn Sina's Solution
Replies to objections regarding mental forms of non-existents and discusses Ibn Sina's view on impossibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the defender of mental forms responds to the objection that non-existents cannot have forms, and how Ibn Sina explains knowledge of impossibles.

Core Idea
Non-existent things have mental forms with non-essential existence; impossibles are known through analogy or negation.

The previous lesson established that mental forms are necessary. But an objection arises: if a non-existent thing has no existence, how can it have a form in the mind? And what about impossible things? This passage presents the answer: the non-existent has only a form, which has a non-essential existence in the mind. It also references Ibn Sina's distinction between conception of impossible things through analogy or negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the response
The objection states that just as there is no relation to pure non-existence, there can be no form of it. The response clarifies that the non-existent has only a form with non-essential existence.
Notice the shift from relation to form.
2
Understand the concept of non-essential existence
The non-existent's form has a non-essential existence (wujud ghayr muta'asil), which means it exists mentally but not externally.
This is key to defending mental existence.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's two methods for conceiving impossibles
The passage explains that we conceive impossibles either by analogy (e.g., combining attributes) or by negation (judging that no concept exists).
Think about how this differs from having a direct mental form.
4
Consider the link to Abu Hashim's view
Ibn Sina's approach might be what Abu Hashim meant by 'knowledge without a known object'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإدراك إما إضافة أو صفة لها قال : والمتكلمون لما أنكروا الوجود الذهني جعلوا الإدراك إضافة بين المدرك والمدرك، أو صفة لها إضافة إليه فورد عليهم العلم بالمعدومات والممتنعات إذ لا تعقل الإضافة إلى ما تحقق له أصلاً، ولزم القول بالصورة في الكل لما أن الإدراك معنى واحد فإن قيل : كما لا إضافة إلى العدم المحض فكذا لا صورة له وإن أخذت صورة لما في الذهن كان في الذهن من المعدوم أمران الصورة وذو الصورة وهو بين البطلان . قلنا : ليس من المعدوم إلا الصورة ومعناها أن له وجوداً غير متأصل، وهي من حيث قيامها بالذهن علم، ومن حيث ذاتها معلوم بخلاف الموجود فإن العلم ما في الذهن والمعلوم ما في الخارج وفي كلام ابن سينا أنه ليس في العقل من الممتنع صورة وتصوره إما على سبيل التشبيه بإن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر وهو الاجتماع ، ثم يحكم بأن مثله لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض، أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم أن ليس بينهما مفهوم هو الاجتماع، وكأنه مراد أبي هاشم حيث أثبت علماً لا معلوماً له).
Perception is either a relation or a quality having it. Then they are faced with knowledge of non-existents and impossibles, since a relation to what has no actual existence is inconceivable. And if a form is taken for what is in the mind, then of the non-existent there would be two things in the mind: the form and the owner of the form, which is clearly false. We say: Of the non-existent there is only the form, and its meaning is that it has a non-essential existence. The form, as subsisting in the mind, is knowledge, and as an essence is known, unlike the existent, for knowledge is what is in the mind and the known is what is in the external. ?

?

How can you apply Ibn Sina's method of analogy and negation to test the possibility of your desires before acting on them? Will you commit to asking 'Is this possible in reality?' before pursuing it?
?

Are you sometimes tempted to think that your mental concept of Allah is exactly as He is in reality? How does the distinction between knowledge and known protect you from attributing limitations of mental existence to Allah? What specific attribute will you reflect on differently today?

Knowledge as Relation: The Problem of Self-Knowledge
Explains positions on knowledge as relation, al-Qadi's addition, and self-knowledge objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key positions on knowledge as a relation among those who deny mental existence, and how the problem of self-knowledge is addressed through conceptual distinction.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge challenges the relational theory of knowledge; conceptual distinction saves it.

This passage from al-Maqasid delves into a critical debate over the nature of knowledge. For thinkers who deny mental existence, knowledge must be explained as some kind of relation between knower and known. But this leads to a puzzle: how can something know itself, since a relation requires two distinct things? The text presents the views of those denying mental existence, al-Qadi's extension, and the proposed solution of conceptual distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Pay attention to the structure: positions, addition, objection, answer.
2
Identify the two initial positions
The passage presents two ways those denying mental existence define knowledge: either as a mere relation, or as a quality possessing that relation. Find these in the text.
The relation is called 'worldliness' (al-ālamiyya).
3
Recognize al-Qadi's additional relation
Al-Qadi posited an extra relation beyond knowledge and worldliness, leading to three or four entities. Understand the logic behind this multiplication.
This shows the complexity of the relational view.
4
Grasp the objection of self-knowledge
The objection is that if knowledge is a relation, then self-knowledge is impossible because a relation requires two distinct relata. See how this targets all relational theories.
This is a powerful critique.
5
Understand the answer using conceptual distinction
The response is that a conceptual distinction (taghāyur iʿtibārī) between the knower and known is sufficient for the relation even in self-knowledge. This saves the relational theory.
This concept was previously discussed in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أن من لم يقل بالوجود الذهني وحصول الصورة جعل العلم إما مجرد إضافة وتعلق بين العالم والمعلوم، وإما صفة لها تلك الإضافة فالصفة العلم، والإضافة العالمية . وأثبت القاضي وراء العلم والعالمية إضافة إما لأحدهما فيكون هناك ثلاثة أمور ولكل منهما فتكون أربعة وعلى هذا قياس سائر الإدراكات، فإنه أورد عليهم علم الشيء بنفس ذاته فإن التعلق لا يتصور إلا بين شيئين . أجيب بأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف على ما مر في حصول الشيء للشيء،
He means: Whoever does not affirm mental existence and the occurrence of the form makes knowledge either a mere relation and connection between the knower and the known, or a quality that has that relation, so the quality is knowledge and the relation is worldliness. And al-Qadi affirmed beyond knowledge and worldliness an additional relation, either to one of them, so there are three things, or to each of them, so there are four. And upon this analogy for all perceptions. Then he objected against them: knowledge of a thing by its own self, for relation is inconceivable except between two things. It was answered that conceptual distinctions are sufficient, as has passed regarding the occurrence of a thing for a thing.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage explains the positions of those who deny mental existence. It states that they make knowledge either a mere relation between knower and known, or a quality that has that relation. Al-Qadi (the judge) went further and posited an additional relation beyond knowledge and 'worldliness' (al-'alamiyya), leading to three or four entities. Then an objection is raised against them: knowledge of a thing by itself (self-knowledge) seems impossible because a relation requires two distinct things. The answer given is that a conceptual distinction (taghayur i'tibari) is sufficient for the relation, as previously discussed in the context of a thing occurring for a thing.

Key Terms

صورة
علم
إضافة
عالمية
قاضي
تغاير اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge is often taken as evidence for mental existence, but here it is used to refute the relational theory.
Correction: The passage uses self-knowledge as an objection against the relational theory of knowledge, not as proof of mental existence. The solution of conceptual distinction saves the relational theory without invoking mental existence.

Checkpoint
What was the response to the objection that self-knowledge requires two distinct relata?
A) They denied self-knowledge.
B) They said knowledge is not a relation.
C) They said conceptual distinction is sufficient.
D) They added more relations.

Key Takeaway
The relational theory of knowledge requires conceptual distinction to account for self-knowledge, illustrating the dialectical depth of kalām.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own self-knowledge, do you perceive a distance between the 'knower' and the 'known' within you? How does this lesson's response of conceptual distinction either satisfy or unsettle your demand for a real separation?
?
The kalām scholars resorted to conceptual distinctions to preserve the relational theory. In your own life, are you quick to accept intellectual distinctions that solve a problem, or do you insist on tangible evidence? What does your answer reveal about your trust in divine wisdom?
?
If Allah knows you completely without any gap between His knowledge and your reality, how does this lesson's discussion of relation and distinction affect your sense of being 'known'? Will you allow this truth to humble you today?
