The Objection of Non-Existents and the Defense of Mental Forms
Answers the objection that mental forms also fail for non-existent things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the theory of mental forms against objections concerning knowledge of non-existent things.

Core Idea
Mental forms solve the problem of knowing non-existents without requiring a second entity.

This passage tackles a key objection against mental forms: if knowledge is a mental form, how can we know non-existent things like impossible objects? The author first turns the tables on the critics, then provides a sophisticated answer that preserves the unity of mental form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial counter-objection
Notice how the author points out that those denying mental forms also cannot account for knowledge of non-existent possibles and impossibles. This shifts the burden back to the critics.
The examples (geometric shapes, hypotheticals) show that non-existents have no external reality.
2
Grasp the objection against mental forms
The objection claims that a mental form requires a bearer, leading to a regress: for a non-existent, there would need to be both a form and a possessor of the form in the mind.
This is a specific attack on the mental forms view itself.
3
Learn the author's answer
The author says the mind contains only the form. The form is simultaneously knowledge (as mental subsistence) and the known (in its own essence). No second entity is needed.
This answer hinges on distinguishing two aspects of the same mental form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يرد عليهم العلم بالمعدومات من الممكنات ككثير من الأشكال الهندسية، والممتنعات كالمفروضات التي يبين بها الخلف فإنه لا تحقق لها في الخارج، وإذا لم تتحقق في الذهن أيضاً لم تتصور الإضافة بينهما وبين العالم وما يقال من إمكان تحققها قائمة بأنفسها على ما هو رأي أفلاطون، أو بغيرها من الأجرام الغائبة عنا فضروري البطلان في الممتنعات ، لا يقال غاية ما في الباب إثبات الصورة الذهنية في العلم بالمعدومات. قلنا : الإدراك معنى واحد لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة إلى المدرك والمدرك ، فإن علم أنه غير نفس الإضافة في موضع علم كونه كذلك مطلقاً. فإن قيل : العلم بالمعدومات وارد على القول بالصورة أيضاً لأن الصورة إنما تكون لذي الصورة لا للعدم المحض فإما أن تكون في الخارج فلا يكون معدوماً والكلام فيه أو في الذهن فيكون من المعدوم أمر هو الصورة، وأمر آخر له الصورة وهو باطل لم يقل به أحد . قلنا ليس في الذهن إلا أمر واحد هو الصورة، ومعنى كونها صورة للمعدوم أنها بحيث لو أمكن في الخارج تحققها وتحقق ذلك المعدوم لكانت إياه، ثم إنها من حيث قيامها بالذهن وحصولها فيه علم تتصف به النفس، ومن حيث ذاتها وماهيتها العقلية أعني مع قطع النظر عن قيامها بالذهن معلوم وجود غير متأصل وهذا بخلاف الموجود فإن العلم ما في الذهن له
Yes, they are faced with knowledge of non-existent possibilities, like many geometric shapes, and impossibles, like the hypotheticals by which the reductio ad absurdum is shown. For these have no actuality in the external. And if they are also not actualized in the mind, then relation between them and the world cannot be conceived. And what is said about the possibility of their actuality as subsistent in themselves according to the view of Plato, or through other remote bodies, is necessarily false in the case of impossibles. It is not said that the most that can be done is to affirm mental forms for knowledge of non-existents. We say: Perception is a single meaning, which does not differ except in relation to the perceiver and the perceived. He knows that it is not the relation itself in any case, he knows it to be so absolutely. If it is said: Knowledge of non-existents also applies to the view of mental forms, because the form is only for the possessor of the form, not for pure non-existence. So either it is in the external, then it is not non-existent, and the discussion is about it; or it is in the mind, then of the non-existent there would be a thing which is the form and another thing which has the form, and that is false, and no one has said it. We say: There is nothing in the mind except one thing, which is the form. And the meaning of its being a form of the non-existent is that if it were possible for it to be actualized in the external and for that non-existent to be actualized, it would be it. Then, with regard to its subsistence in the mind and its occurrence in it, it is knowledge with which the soul is qualified; and with regard to its essence and its intellectual quiddity, i.e., disregarding its subsistence in the mind, it is known, having a non-essential existence. And this is unlike the existent, for knowledge of it is what is in the mind.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage addresses a further objection against those who deny mental existence by turning the tables on them. First, it notes that they also face problems with knowledge of non-existent possibles and impossibles (like geometrical shapes and hypotheticals for reductio ad absurdum) because these have no external reality and, if not in the mind, no relation is possible. The author dismisses the idea that such things could exist as Platonic Forms or in distant bodies as obviously false for impossibles. Then, the author presents an objection against the mental forms view itself: if the form is for the possessor of the form, then for a non-existent, either the form is outside (contradicting its non-existence) or in the mind (requiring two things: the form and its possessor). The answer is that the mind contains only one thing, the form. Its being a 'form of the non-existent' means that if both could exist externally, they would be identical. The form as subsisting in the mind is knowledge, and considered in itself (apart from mental subsistence) it is the known, with non-essential existence. For an existent, knowledge is the mental form while the external thing is the known.

Key Terms
صورة
معدوم
ممتنع
وجود غير متأصل
علم
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of non-existents requires two mental entities: the form and the thing it represents.
Correction: The author argues that only the form exists in the mind; it is both knowledge (as mental existence) and the known (in its essence).

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that a mental form of a non-existent would require a second thing (the bearer) in the mind?
A) He says the form exists in the outside.
B) He says the non-existent has no form.
C) He says the mind contains only the form, which is what is known.
D) He says there are two things: the form and the thing.

Key Takeaway
Mental forms are sufficient for knowledge of non-existents because the form itself, as a mental entity, functions as both the knowledge and the known, without needing an extra bearer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you think of a belief you hold that has no external evidence? This lesson says mental forms are sufficient for knowledge — do you feel a real, unshakeable certainty about that belief, or do you secretly doubt it because it lacks physical proof? What would it take for you to trust your intellect as much as your senses?
?
When you imagine something impossible (e.g., a square circle), you still have a form in your mind. Does that disturb you — that your mind can hold 'things' that can never be? How does this lesson change your view of what it means to know? Are you willing to accept that knowledge is not always about copying reality, but about the mind's own structure?
?
If mental forms are the same for knower and known (for non-existents), then what you know is not separate from you. How does that reshape your relationship with your own thoughts? Are you comfortable with the idea that some of your knowledge is self-contained, or do you feel a need to 'ground' it outside yourself? What would change if you truly owned that intellectual knowledge is just as real as physical reality?

The Objection of Self-Knowledge and the Conceptual Distinction
Al-Qadi's addition, the self-knowledge objection, and the answer using conceptual distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Qadi's addition of an extra relation leads to an objection regarding self-knowledge and why the conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i'tibari) suffices to resolve it.

Core Idea
A relation requires two distinct things; a conceptual distinction provides the needed duality.

This lesson begins the debate on the nature of knowledge. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar posited an additional relation beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing, leading to questions about how a thing can know itself. The objection is that a relation requires two distinct entities, so self-knowledge seems impossible. The Ash'ari response invokes the conceptual distinction—the mind can treat one thing as two aspects. Let's examine this foundational exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the structure: al-Qadi's thesis, the objection, and the answer.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that knowledge of a thing by itself is impossible because relation requires two things. Find that in the text.
The objection is introduced by 'فإنه أورد عليهم علم الشيء بنفس ذاته'.
3
Understand the answer
The answer uses 'التغاير الاعتباري' – a distinction made by the mind. How does that solve the problem?
The answer is 'أجيب بأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this is important: it establishes that knowledge does not require real multiplicity in the object.
This sets the stage for more radical objections about non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأثبت القاضي وراء العلم والعالمية إضافة إما لأحدهما فيكون هناك ثلاثة أمور ولكل منهما فتكون أربعة وعلى هذا قياس سائر الإدراكات، فإنه أورد عليهم علم الشيء بنفس ذاته فإن التعلق لا يتصور إلا بين شيئين. أجيب بأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف على ما مر في حصول الشيء للشيء، نعم يرد عليهم العلم بالمعدومات من الممكنات ككثير من الأشكال الهندسية، والممتنعات كالمفروضات التي يبين بها الخلف فإنه لا تحقق لها في الخارج، وإذا لم تتحقق في الذهن أيضاً لم تتصور الإضافة بينهما وبين العالم وما يقال من إمكان تحققها قائمة بأنفسها على ما هو رأي أفلاطون، أو بغيرها من الأجرام الغائبة عنا فضروري البطلان في الممتنعات. لا يقال غاية ما في الباب إثبات الصورة الذهنية في العلم بالمعدومات. قلنا: الإدراك معنى واحد لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة إلى المدرك والمدرك، فإن علم أنه غير نفس الإضافة في موضع علم كونه كذلك مطلقاً. فإن قيل: العلم بالمعدومات وارد على القول بالصورة أيضاً لأن الصورة إنما تكون لذي الصورة لا للعدم المحض فإما أن تكون في الخارج فلا يكون معدوماً والكلام فيه أو في الذهن فيكون من المعدوم أمر هو الصورة، وأمر آخر له الصورة وهو باطل لم يقل به أحد. قلنا ليس في الذهن إلا أمر واحد هو الصورة، ومعنى كونها صورة للمعدوم أنها بحيث لو أمكن في الخارج تحققها وتحقق ذلك المعدوم لكانت إياه، ثم إنها من حيث قيامها بالذهن وحصولها فيه علم تتصف به النفس، ومن حيث ذاتها وماهيتها العقلية أعني مع قطع النظر عن قيامها بالذهن معلوم وجود غير متأصل وهذا بخلاف الموجود فإن العلم ما في الذهن والمعلوم ما في الخارج كما مر، وبهذا يندفع إشكال آخر وهو: أنهم صرحوا بأن الصورة إنما تكون علماً إذا كانت مطابقة للخارج وذلك لأن هذا إنما هو في صور الأعيان الخارجية، وأما المعدومات من الاعتباريات وغيرها فمعنى مطابقتها ما ذكرنا. هذا، وفي بعض المواضع من كلام ابن سينا اعتراف بأن العلم بالممتنعات ليس حصول الصورة لأنه ذكر في الشفاء أن المستحيل لا يحصل له صورة في العقل ولا يمكن أن يتصور شيء هو اجتماع النقيضين بل تصور المستحيل إنما يكون على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع، ثم يقال مثل هذا الأمر لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض، أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض. وعلى هذا حمل صاحب المواقف كلام أبي هاشم حيث جعل العلم بالمستحيل علماً لا معلوم له بناء على أن المعلوم شيء والمستحيل ليس بشيء، وحينئذ لا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأنه تناقض إذ لا معنى للمعلوم سوى ما تعلق به العلم ولا يحتاج إلى ما ذكره الأمدي من أن له أن يصطلح على أن المعلوم ما تعلق به العلم من الأشياء.
And al-Qadi established, beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing, an addition, either to one of them so there are three things, or to each of them so there are four, and upon this is the analogy of all other perceptions. For it was objected to them: knowledge of a thing by its own essence, for relation cannot be conceived except between two things. It was answered that the conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i'tibari) suffices, as previously mentioned in the attainment of a thing for a thing. Yes, there is objected to them knowledge of non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) among possibles, like many geometrical shapes, and impossibilities (al-mumtani'at) like the suppositions by which the absurd is shown, for they have no actuality in the external world. And if they also have no actuality in the mind, the relation between them and the knower is not conceived. And what is said of the possibility of their existence subsisting by themselves, as is the opinion of Plato, or by other than them of bodies absent from us, is necessarily false regarding impossibilities. It cannot be said that the utmost in the matter is the proof of the mental form (al-surah al-dhihniyyah) in knowledge of non-existents. We said: perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that does not differ except by relation to the perceiver and the perceived; for if it is known that it is other than the relation itself in one place, it is known that it is so absolutely. For if it is said: knowledge of non-existents is an objection against the statement of the form as well, because the form is only for the possessor of the form, not for pure non-existence. So either it is in the external world, then it is not non-existent, and the discussion is about it, or it is in the mind, then from the non-existent there is a thing that is the form, and another thing that has the form, and this is false and no one has said it. We said: there is not in the mind except one thing, which is the form. And the meaning of its being a form for the non-existent is that it is such that if its actualization and the actualization of that non-existent were possible in the external world, it would be it. Then, it is, from the perspective of its subsistence in the mind and its occurrence in it, knowledge with which the soul is described; and from the perspective of its essence and its intellectual quiddity, i.e., disregarding its subsistence in the mind, it is a known thing of non-originate existence. And this is unlike the existent, for knowledge is what is in the mind and the known is what is in the external world, as previously mentioned. And by this another problem is repelled, namely: they have stated that the form is only knowledge if it corresponds to the external world. This is only in the forms of external entities; as for non-existents, whether conceptual or others, the meaning of their correspondence is what we have mentioned. This, and in some places of the speech of Ibn Sina there is acknowledgment that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form, because he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the impossible does not acquire a form in the intellect, nor can something that is the conjunction of two opposites be conceived; rather, the conception of the impossible is only by way of analogy, by conceiving between blackness and sweetness something that is the conjunction, then it is said that such a thing is not possible between blackness and whiteness; or by way of negation, by the intellect judging that a concept that is the conjunction of blackness and whiteness cannot exist. And upon this the author of al-Mawaqif interpreted the speech of Abu Hashim, where he made knowledge of the impossible a knowledge with no known object, based on the fact that the known is a thing and the impossible is not a thing. And then the objection of al-Imam is not valid, that it is a contradiction, because there is no meaning of the known other than that to which knowledge is attached, and there is no need for what al-Amidi mentioned, that he may stipulate that the known is what knowledge is attached to of things.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dense theological argument about the nature of knowledge (al-‘ilm) and its objects (al-ma‘lumat), specifically addressing problems raised by knowledge of non-existents (al-ma‘dumat) and impossibilities (al-mumtani‘at). The author follows a dialectical method: each objection is met with a precise response. The passage begins by noting that al-Qadi (likely ‘Abd al-Jabbar) posited an additional relation beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing (‘alimiyyah), leading to a debate about whether knowledge can relate to a single thing by itself. The objection is that a relation requires two distinct things, so knowledge of a thing by its own essence would be impossible. The response is that a conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i‘tibari) suffices—the mind can distinguish aspects of a single entity. Then a greater objection is raised: knowledge of non-existents, such as geometrical shapes that have no external reality, or impossibilities like the supposition used in a proof by contradiction. If these have no external existence and also no mental existence (since they are not actualized in the mind either), then there can be no relation between them and the knower. The response clarifies that perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that only differs by relation to the perceiver and the perceived; it does not require the object to exist externally. The discussion then turns to the theory of mental forms (al-surah al-dhihniyyah). An opponent argues that if knowledge of a non-existent is a mental form, then either the form exists externally (contradicting its non-existence) or it exists in the mind, making the non-existent consist of both a form and a possessor—absurd. The response is that only one thing exists in the mind: the form itself. Its being 'for' a non-existent means that if the non-existent were to exist externally, the form would correspond to it. The form has two aspects: as subsisting in the mind, it is knowledge (al-‘ilm) describing the soul; as an intellectual quiddity, disregarding its mental subsistence, it is a known thing (ma‘lum) of non-originate existence. This distinguishes it from existents, where knowledge is in the mind and the known is in the external world. This theory also resolves another problem: the requirement of correspondence (mutabaqah) between the form and external reality applies only to forms of external entities; for non-existents, correspondence means something else. Finally, the author notes that Ibn Sina acknowledged in al-Shifa‘ that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form. Instead, it occurs by analogy (tashbih) or negation (nafy). For example, conceiving the impossible conjunction of blackness and whiteness is done by first imagining a possible conjunction (like blackness and sweetness) and then denying its applicability. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) interpreted Abu Hashim’s statement that knowledge of the impossible is ‘knowledge with no known object’ in this light, thereby defusing al-Imam’s (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) accusation of contradiction. The passage concludes that the known is simply whatever knowledge attaches to, and one need not adopt al-Amidi’s stipulative definition.

Key Terms
العلم
العالمية
الإضافة
التغاير الاعتباري
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a thing by itself is impossible because it requires two real distinct entities.
Correction: The Ash'ari tradition holds that a conceptual distinction in the mind suffices; the known and knower can be aspects of the same thing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was it objected that a thing cannot know itself?
A) Because knowledge is an accident and cannot subsist in the same subject.
B) Because a relation (ta'alluq) cannot be conceived except between two things.
C) Because self-knowledge leads to an infinite regress.
D) Because the attribute of being knowing is identical with knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i'tibari) is a powerful tool that allows relations to exist between aspects of a single entity, resolving the problem of self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own thoughts and intentions, do you ever feel a split between the 'you' that is aware and the 'you' that is being examined? How does the conceptual distinction help you trust that your self-knowledge is genuine, not just an illusion?
?
If you truly accept that a conceptual distinction is enough for a real relation, how does that change your understanding of your relationship with Allah—especially when you consider that your knowledge of Him is real, even though He is one and you cannot perceive two distinct realities?
?
Think of a time you doubted your own sincerity or self-awareness. Now that you know about al-taghayur al-i'tibari, would you still dismiss that doubt as confusion, or can you now see it as a valid relation between different aspects of your soul? How will you apply this next time you feel internally divided?

Knowledge of Non-Existents and Impossibilities
Objection from knowledge of non-existents and impossibilities, and response that perception is unvarying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge of non-existents and impossibilities poses a problem for the theory of knowledge, and the initial response that perception is a single meaning differentiated only by relation.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not require the object to exist externally; perception is one meaning with varying relations.

After establishing that conceptual distinctions can solve the self-knowledge problem, a more serious objection is raised: how can we know things that do not exist at all, such as geometrical shapes or imaginary impossibilities? These have no external reality and, if they have no mental reality either, there can be no relation between them and the knower. The first response is that perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that only differs by its relation to the perceiver and the perceived. Let's unpack this objection and the initial answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two categories: non-existents among possibles (e.g., geometrical shapes) and impossibilities (e.g., suppositions in proof by contradiction).
2
Identify the core objection
The objection is that these have no actuality in the external world, and if they also have no actuality in the mind, there is no basis for a relation between them and the knower.
The text says: 'فإنه لا تحقق لها في الخارج، وإذا لم تتحقق في الذهن أيضاً لم تتصور الإضافة بينهما وبين العالم'.
3
Examine the response
The response is that perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that does not differ except by relation. It does not require the object to be real; the relation itself suffices.
The response begins: 'قلنا: الإدراك معنى واحد لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة'.
4
Consider the implication
This means that the nature of knowledge does not change whether its object is existent or non-existent; only the relation differs. This is a key move in the debate.

The discussion then turns to the theory of mental forms (al-surah al-dhihniyyah). An opponent argues that if knowledge of a non-existent is a mental form, then either the form exists externally (contradicting its non-existence) or it exists in the mind, making the non-existent consist of both a form and a possessor—absurd. The response is that only one thing exists in the mind: the form itself. Its being 'for' a non-existent means that if the non-existent were to exist externally, the form would correspond to it. The form has two aspects: as subsisting in the mind, it is knowledge (al-‘ilm) describing the soul; as an intellectual quiddity, disregarding its mental subsistence, it is a known thing (ma‘lum) of non-originate existence. This distinguishes it from existents, where knowledge is in the mind and the known is in the external world. This theory also resolves another problem: the requirement of correspondence (mutabaqah) between the form and external reality applies only to forms of external entities; for non-existents, correspondence means something else. Finally, the author notes that Ibn Sina acknowledged in al-Shifa‘ that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form. Instead, it occurs by analogy (tashbih) or negation (nafy). For example, conceiving the impossible conjunction of blackness and whiteness is done by first imagining a possible conjunction (like blackness and sweetness) and then denying its applicability. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) interpreted Abu Hashim’s statement that knowledge of the impossible is ‘knowledge with no known object’ in this light, thereby defusing al-Imam’s (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) accusation of contradiction. The passage concludes that the known is simply whatever knowledge attaches to, and one need not adopt al-Amidi’s stipulative definition.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الممتنع
الصورة الذهنية
الإدراك
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge requires the known object to exist in some way, either externally or in the mind as a form.
Correction: The Ash'ari response argues that perception is a single meaning that only differs by relation; the object need not have any reality outside the relation itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of perception (al-idrak) that allows knowledge of non-existents?
A) It varies depending on the reality of the object.
B) It is a single meaning that only differs by relation.
C) It is the same as the mental form.
D) It requires the object to be present in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Perception is a single meaning that varies only by relation; therefore, knowledge does not require the object to have any independent existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, whose existence is unlike anything else, do you rely on a mental image or on the unvarying perception that the passage describes? If you catch yourself relying on imagination, what will you do today to replace that with pure acknowledgment?
?
The passage says perception does not differ except by relation. When you are tested by doubt about the unseen, like the reality of the afterlife, do you seek proof through imagined scenarios, or rest in the certainty of the unvarying principle? Name one specific doubt you will confront using this principle.
?
You probably assumed knowing something requires it to exist in some way. This passage challenges that. Identify one belief about God or the unseen that you have been hesitating to affirm because you thought it needed external evidence. Will you now affirm it based on the unvarying nature of perception?

Defending the Mental Form Theory for Non-Existents
Objection against mental forms for non-existents and the Ash'ari defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari theologian defends the theory of mental forms against the objection that a form cannot be for a non-existent, and how the dual aspect of the form resolves the problem.

Core Idea
The mental form is the sole entity in the mind; its being 'for' a non-existent means it would correspond if the object existed.

The debate now turns to the theory of mental forms (al-surah al-dhihniyyah). An opponent argues that if knowledge of a non-existent is a mental form, then either the form exists externally (contradicting non-existence) or it exists in the mind, leading to an absurd multiplication of entities. The Ash'ari response is that only one thing exists in the mind: the form itself. Its relationship to the non-existent is potential: it would be identical to the non-existent if the latter were to exist. Furthermore, the form has two aspects: as subsisting in the mind, it is knowledge; as a quiddity, it is a known thing. This also solves the problem of correspondence (mutabaqah) with the external world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The objection is that a form must belong to a possessor, which leads to absurdity for non-existents.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says: either the form is external (but then it's not non-existent) or it is in the mind (but then the non-existent would consist of a form and a possessor, which is absurd).
The text: 'فإما أن تكون في الخارج فلا يكون معدوماً... أو في الذهن فيكون من المعدوم أمر هو الصورة وأمر آخر له الصورة'.
3
Analyze the response
The response: there is only one thing in the mind, the form. Its being 'for the non-existent' means if the non-existent were to exist externally, the form would be it.
'ليس في الذهن إلا أمر واحد هو الصورة، ومعنى كونها صورة للمعدوم أنها بحيث لو أمكن في الخارج تحققها وتحقق ذلك المعدوم لكانت إياه'.
4
Explore the dual aspect
The form is knowledge when considered as subsisting in the mind, and a known thing when considered in itself (quiddity). This distinguishes it from existent objects.
The text explains this two-fold perspective.
5
Understand the resolution of correspondence
The requirement of correspondence (mutabaqah) applies only to forms of external entities; for non-existents, correspondence means something else.
The passage says: 'أما المعدومات من الاعتباريات وغيرها فمعنى مطابقتها ما ذكرنا'.

Source Text

وأثبت القاضي وراء العلم والعالمية إضافة إما لأحدهما فيكون هناك ثلاثة أمور ولكل منهما فتكون أربعة وعلى هذا قياس سائر الإدراكات، فإنه أورد عليهم علم الشيء بنفس ذاته فإن التعلق لا يتصور إلا بين شيئين. أجيب بأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف على ما مر في حصول الشيء للشيء، نعم يرد عليهم العلم بالمعدومات من الممكنات ككثير من الأشكال الهندسية، والممتنعات كالمفروضات التي يبين بها الخلف فإنه لا تحقق لها في الخارج، وإذا لم تتحقق في الذهن أيضاً لم تتصور الإضافة بينهما وبين العالم وما يقال من إمكان تحققها قائمة بأنفسها على ما هو رأي أفلاطون، أو بغيرها من الأجرام الغائبة عنا فضروري البطلان في الممتنعات. لا يقال غاية ما في الباب إثبات الصورة الذهنية في العلم بالمعدومات. قلنا: الإدراك معنى واحد لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة إلى المدرك والمدرك، فإن علم أنه غير نفس الإضافة في موضع علم كونه كذلك مطلقاً. فإن قيل: العلم بالمعدومات وارد على القول بالصورة أيضاً لأن الصورة إنما تكون لذي الصورة لا للعدم المحض فإما أن تكون في الخارج فلا يكون معدوماً والكلام فيه أو في الذهن فيكون من المعدوم أمر هو الصورة، وأمر آخر له الصورة وهو باطل لم يقل به أحد. قلنا ليس في الذهن إلا أمر واحد هو الصورة، ومعنى كونها صورة للمعدوم أنها بحيث لو أمكن في الخارج تحققها وتحقق ذلك المعدوم لكانت إياه، ثم إنها من حيث قيامها بالذهن وحصولها فيه علم تتصف به النفس، ومن حيث ذاتها وماهيتها العقلية أعني مع قطع النظر عن قيامها بالذهن معلوم وجود غير متأصل وهذا بخلاف الموجود فإن العلم ما في الذهن والمعلوم ما في الخارج كما مر، وبهذا يندفع إشكال آخر وهو: أنهم صرحوا بأن الصورة إنما تكون علماً إذا كانت مطابقة للخارج وذلك لأن هذا إنما هو في صور الأعيان الخارجية، وأما المعدومات من الاعتباريات وغيرها فمعنى مطابقتها ما ذكرنا. هذا، وفي بعض المواضع من كلام ابن سينا اعتراف بأن العلم بالممتنعات ليس حصول الصورة لأنه ذكر في الشفاء أن المستحيل لا يحصل له صورة في العقل ولا يمكن أن يتصور شيء هو اجتماع النقيضين بل تصور المستحيل إنما يكون على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع، ثم يقال مثل هذا الأمر لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض، أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض. وعلى هذا حمل صاحب المواقف كلام أبي هاشم حيث جعل العلم بالمستحيل علماً لا معلوم له بناء على أن المعلوم شيء والمستحيل ليس بشيء، وحينئذ لا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأنه تناقض إذ لا معنى للمعلوم سوى ما تعلق به العلم ولا يحتاج إلى ما ذكره الأمدي من أن له أن يصطلح على أن المعلوم ما تعلق به العلم من الأشياء.
And al-Qadi established, beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing, an addition, either to one of them so there are three things, or to each of them so there are four, and upon this is the analogy of all other perceptions. For it was objected to them: knowledge of a thing by its own essence, for relation cannot be conceived except between two things. It was answered that the conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i'tibari) suffices, as previously mentioned in the attainment of a thing for a thing. Yes, there is objected to them knowledge of non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) among possibles, like many geometrical shapes, and impossibilities (al-mumtani'at) like the suppositions by which the absurd is shown, for they have no actuality in the external world. And if they also have no actuality in the mind, the relation between them and the knower is not conceived. And what is said of the possibility of their existence subsisting by themselves, as is the opinion of Plato, or by other than them of bodies absent from us, is necessarily false regarding impossibilities. It cannot be said that the utmost in the matter is the proof of the mental form (al-surah al-dhihniyyah) in knowledge of non-existents. We said: perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that does not differ except by relation to the perceiver and the perceived; for if it is known that it is other than the relation itself in one place, it is known that it is so absolutely. For if it is said: knowledge of non-existents is an objection against the statement of the form as well, because the form is only for the possessor of the form, not for pure non-existence. So either it is in the external world, then it is not non-existent, and the discussion is about it, or it is in the mind, then from the non-existent there is a thing that is the form, and another thing that has the form, and this is false and no one has said it. We said: there is not in the mind except one thing, which is the form. And the meaning of its being a form for the non-existent is that it is such that if its actualization and the actualization of that non-existent were possible in the external world, it would be it. Then, it is, from the perspective of its subsistence in the mind and its occurrence in it, knowledge with which the soul is described; and from the perspective of its essence and its intellectual quiddity, i.e., disregarding its subsistence in the mind, it is a known thing of non-originate existence. And this is unlike the existent, for knowledge is what is in the mind and the known is what is in the external world, as previously mentioned. And by this another problem is repelled, namely: they have stated that the form is only knowledge if it corresponds to the external world. This is only in the forms of external entities; as for non-existents, whether conceptual or others, the meaning of their correspondence is what we have mentioned. This, and in some places of the speech of Ibn Sina there is acknowledgment that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form, because he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the impossible does not acquire a form in the intellect, nor can something that is the conjunction of two opposites be conceived; rather, the conception of the impossible is only by way of analogy, by conceiving between blackness and sweetness something that is the conjunction, then it is said that such a thing is not possible between blackness and whiteness; or by way of negation, by the intellect judging that a concept that is the conjunction of blackness and whiteness cannot exist. And upon this the author of al-Mawaqif interpreted the speech of Abu Hashim, where he made knowledge of the impossible a knowledge with no known object, based on the fact that the known is a thing and the impossible is not a thing. And then the objection of al-Imam is not valid, that it is a contradiction, because there is no meaning of the known other than that to which knowledge is attached, and there is no need for what al-Amidi mentioned, that he may stipulate that the known is what knowledge is attached to of things.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dense theological argument about the nature of knowledge (al-‘ilm) and its objects (al-ma‘lumat), specifically addressing problems raised by knowledge of non-existents (al-ma‘dumat) and impossibilities (al-mumtani‘at). The author follows a dialectical method: each objection is met with a precise response. The passage begins by noting that al-Qadi (likely ‘Abd al-Jabbar) posited an additional relation beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing (‘alimiyyah), leading to a debate about whether knowledge can relate to a single thing by itself. The objection is that a relation requires two distinct things, so knowledge of a thing by its own essence would be impossible. The response is that a conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i‘tibari) suffices—the mind can distinguish aspects of a single entity. Then a greater objection is raised: knowledge of non-existents, such as geometrical shapes that have no external reality, or impossibilities like the supposition used in a proof by contradiction. If these have no external existence and also no mental existence (since they are not actualized in the mind either), then there can be no relation between them and the knower. The response clarifies that perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that only differs by relation to the perceiver and the perceived; it does not require the object to exist externally. The discussion then turns to the theory of mental forms (al-surah al-dhihniyyah). An opponent argues that if knowledge of a non-existent is a mental form, then either the form exists externally (contradicting its non-existence) or it exists in the mind, making the non-existent consist of both a form and a possessor—absurd. The response is that only one thing exists in the mind: the form itself. Its being 'for' a non-existent means that if the non-existent were to exist externally, the form would correspond to it. The form has two aspects: as subsisting in the mind, it is knowledge (al-‘ilm) describing the soul; as an intellectual quiddity, disregarding its mental subsistence, it is a known thing (ma‘lum) of non-originate existence. This distinguishes it from existents, where knowledge is in the mind and the known is in the external world. This theory also resolves another problem: the requirement of correspondence (mutabaqah) between the form and external reality applies only to forms of external entities; for non-existents, correspondence means something else. Finally, the author notes that Ibn Sina acknowledged in al-Shifa‘ that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form. Instead, it occurs by analogy (tashbih) or negation (nafy). For example, conceiving the impossible conjunction of blackness and whiteness is done by first imagining a possible conjunction (like blackness and sweetness) and then denying its applicability. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) interpreted Abu Hashim’s statement that knowledge of the impossible is ‘knowledge with no known object’ in this light, thereby defusing al-Imam’s (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) accusation of contradiction. The passage concludes that the known is simply whatever knowledge attaches to, and one need not adopt al-Amidi’s stipulative definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A mental form of a non-existent would require the non-existent to have a possessor, leading to two mental entities.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that only one mental entity (the form) exists; its relation to the non-existent is potential, not actual.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, what does it mean for a mental form to be a 'form for the non-existent'?
A) The form exists externally as the non-existent.
B) The form is identical to the non-existent in the mind.
C) If the non-existent were to exist externally, the form would be it.
D) The form is a copy of a Platonic idea.

Key Takeaway
The mental form of a non-existent is the only entity in the mind, and it represents the non-existent potentially, not actually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your mental form of a non-existent is only true knowledge if it would match the object were it to exist, how do you know that your conception of Allah (who is not a physical object) is not a false mental form? What accountability does this place on you to refine your understanding of His attributes?
?
Consider a non-existent entity you are certain about, such as a future intention or a theoretical concept like justice. After this lesson, can you still be confident that your mental representation is true? What steps must you take to ensure your mental forms correspond to reality?
?

Are you living as if your knowledge of the unseen (e.g., the afterlife) has actual implications for your actions? If your mental forms about the hereafter are true, what must you change today?

Ibn Sina's Alternative: Knowledge of Impossibilities by Analogy and Negation
Ibn Sina's view on knowledge of impossibilities and its reconciliation with Abu Hashim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's view that knowledge of impossibilities does not involve a mental form but occurs through analogy or negation, and how this interpretation resolves earlier objections.

Core Idea
Impossibilities are known not by forms but by analogy with possibles or by pure negation.

The debate reaches a climax with a reference to Ibn Sina's position in al-Shifa'. Ibn Sina argued that the impossible (al-mustahil) cannot have a form in the mind; rather, we conceive it by analogy or negation. For example, we conceive the impossibility of blackness and whiteness coexisting by first imagining a possible conjunction (like blackness and sweetness) and then denying its possibility. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) used this to interpret Abu Hashim's claim that knowledge of the impossible is 'knowledge with no known object'. This refutes al-Imam al-Razi's accusation of contradiction. Let's see how the known is simply whatever knowledge attaches to.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on Ibn Sina's two methods: analogy (tashbih) and negation (nafy).
2
Understand analogy
Ibn Sina says we conceive the impossible by first imagining a possible conjunction (e.g., blackness and sweetness) and then asserting that such a thing is not possible between blackness and whiteness.
'على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع، ثم يقال مثل هذا الأمر لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض'.
3
Understand negation
Alternatively, the intellect judges that no concept of a conjunction of blackness and whiteness can exist.
'على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض'.
4
Connect to Abu Hashim and al-Imam
The author of al-Mawaqif interprets Abu Hashim's 'knowledge with no known object' as consistent with this view, because the 'known' is just what knowledge attaches to, not an actual thing.
The passage concludes that al-Imam al-Razi's objection of contradiction is avoided.

And al-Qadi established, beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing, an addition, either to one of them so there are three things, or to each of them so there are four, and upon this is the analogy of all other perceptions. For it was objected to them: knowledge of a thing by its own essence, for relation cannot be conceived except between two things. It was answered that the conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i'tibari) suffices, as previously mentioned in the attainment of a thing for a thing. Yes, there is objected to them knowledge of non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) among possibles, like many geometrical shapes, and impossibilities (al-mumtani'at) like the suppositions by which the absurd is shown, for they have no actuality in the external world. And if they also have no actuality in the mind, the relation between them and the knower is not conceived. And what is said of the possibility of their existence subsisting by themselves, as is the opinion of Plato, or by other than them of bodies absent from us, is necessarily false regarding impossibilities. It cannot be said that the utmost in the matter is the proof of the mental form (al-surah al-dhihniyyah) in knowledge of non-existents. We said: perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that does not differ except by relation to the perceiver and the perceived; for if it is known that it is other than the relation itself in one place, it is known that it is so absolutely. For if it is said: knowledge of non-existents is an objection against the statement of the form as well, because the form is only for the possessor of the form, not for pure non-existence. So either it is in the external world, then it is not non-existent, and the discussion is about it, or it is in the mind, then from the non-existent there is a thing that is the form, and another thing that has the form, and this is false and no one has said it. We said: there is not in the mind except one thing, which is the form. And the meaning of its being a form for the non-existent is that it is such that if its actualization and the actualization of that non-existent were possible in the external world, it would be it. Then, it is, from the perspective of its subsistence in the mind and its occurrence in it, knowledge with which the soul is described; and from the perspective of its essence and its intellectual quiddity, i.e., disregarding its subsistence in the mind, it is a known thing of non-originate existence. And this is unlike the existent, for knowledge is what is in the mind and the known is what is in the external world, as previously mentioned. And by this another problem is repelled, namely: they have stated that the form is only knowledge if it corresponds to the external world. This is only in the forms of external entities; as for non-existents, whether conceptual or others, the meaning of their correspondence is what we have mentioned. This, and in some places of the speech of Ibn Sina there is acknowledgment that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form, because he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the impossible does not acquire a form in the intellect, nor can something that is the conjunction of two opposites be conceived; rather, the conception of the impossible is only by way of analogy, by conceiving between blackness and sweetness something that is the conjunction, then it is said that such a thing is not possible between blackness and whiteness; or by way of negation, by the intellect judging that a concept that is the conjunction of blackness and whiteness cannot exist. And upon this the author of al-Mawaqif interpreted the speech of Abu Hashim, where he made knowledge of the impossible a knowledge with no known object, based on the fact that the known is a thing and the impossible is not a thing. And then the objection of al-Imam is not valid, that it is a contradiction, because there is no meaning of the known other than that to which knowledge is attached, and there is no need for what al-Amidi mentioned, that he may stipulate that the known is what knowledge is attached to of things.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dense theological argument about the nature of knowledge (al-‘ilm) and its objects (al-ma‘lumat), specifically addressing problems raised by knowledge of non-existents (al-ma‘dumat) and impossibilities (al-mumtani‘at). The author follows a dialectical method: each objection is met with a precise response. The passage begins by noting that al-Qadi (likely ‘Abd al-Jabbar) posited an additional relation beyond knowledge and the attribute of being knowing (‘alimiyyah), leading to a debate about whether knowledge can relate to a single thing by itself. The objection is that a relation requires two distinct things, so knowledge of a thing by its own essence would be impossible. The response is that a conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i‘tibari) suffices—the mind can distinguish aspects of a single entity. Then a greater objection is raised: knowledge of non-existents, such as geometrical shapes that have no external reality, or impossibilities like the supposition used in a proof by contradiction. If these have no external existence and also no mental existence (since they are not actualized in the mind either), then there can be no relation between them and the knower. The response clarifies that perception (al-idrak) is a single meaning that only differs by relation to the perceiver and the perceived; it does not require the object to exist externally. The discussion then turns to the theory of mental forms (al-surah al-dhihniyyah). An opponent argues that if knowledge of a non-existent is a mental form, then either the form exists externally (contradicting its non-existence) or it exists in the mind, making the non-existent consist of both a form and a possessor—absurd. The response is that only one thing exists in the mind: the form itself. Its being 'for' a non-existent means that if the non-existent were to exist externally, the form would correspond to it. The form has two aspects: as subsisting in the mind, it is knowledge (al-‘ilm) describing the soul; as an intellectual quiddity, disregarding its mental subsistence, it is a known thing (ma‘lum) of non-originate existence. This distinguishes it from existents, where knowledge is in the mind and the known is in the external world. This theory also resolves another problem: the requirement of correspondence (mutabaqah) between the form and external reality applies only to forms of external entities; for non-existents, correspondence means something else. Finally, the author notes that Ibn Sina acknowledged in al-Shifa‘ that knowledge of impossibilities is not the acquisition of a form. Instead, it occurs by analogy (tashbih) or negation (nafy). For example, conceiving the impossible conjunction of blackness and whiteness is done by first imagining a possible conjunction (like blackness and sweetness) and then denying its applicability. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Jurjani) interpreted Abu Hashim’s statement that knowledge of the impossible is ‘knowledge with no known object’ in this light, thereby defusing al-Imam’s (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) accusation of contradiction. The passage concludes that the known is simply whatever knowledge attaches to, and one need not adopt al-Amidi’s stipulative definition.

Key Terms
الممتنع
المستحيل
التشبيه
النفي
المعلوم
صاحب المواقف
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge requires a mental form (surah) that directly represents the object.
Correction: Ibn Sina argued that knowledge of impossibilities proceeds by analogy or negation, not by acquiring a form.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina as cited in the passage, how does one conceive of an impossibility like the conjunction of blackness and whiteness?
A) By acquiring a mental form of the conjunction.
B) By analogy with a possible conjunction and then negating its possibility.
C) By direct intellectual intuition of the impossible.
D) By reading the works of al-Imam al-Razi.

Key Takeaway
Impossibilities are known through analogy or negation, not through mental forms, resolving the paradox of 'knowledge with no known object'.

When you reflect on Allah’s attributes—such as His being 'not a body'—do you rely on direct mental images or on negation? How comfortable are you with not having a clear mental picture?

?

Knowledge of Impossible Things Does Not Require a Mental Image
The author refutes an objection about knowledge of impossibles and explains its nature via analogy or negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how knowledge of impossible things is possible without a mental image, through analogy or negation.

Core Idea
Knowledge of impossibles relies on analogy and negation, not mental images.

The author continues to clarify the nature of knowledge, specifically addressing an objection: if knowledge requires a mental image corresponding to external reality, how can we know impossible things? The answer lies in distinguishing between images of existents and conceptualization of impossibles through analogy and negation. This passage draws on Ibn Sina and later scholars to resolve the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument: from principle to objection to resolution.
2
Identify the distinction
Notice how the author distinguishes between images of external entities and non-existents (ma'dumat). This is key to understanding the objection.
The objection assumes all knowledge requires a mental image corresponding to external reality.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's methods
Ibn Sina says conceptualizing impossibles is not by an image but by analogy (tashbih) or negation (nafy). Find these two methods in the passage.
Analogy: understanding conjunction between blackness and sweetness, then denying it for blackness and whiteness. Negation: the intellect judges impossibility.
4
Follow the resolution
See how the author of al-Mawāqif interprets Abū Hāshim's view and dismisses al-Rāzī's objection. This shows consistency in the definition of 'known'.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Summarize why knowledge of impossibles does not require a mental image. How does this affect the broader understanding of knowledge in kalam?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعلوم ما في الخارج كما مر، وبهذا يندفع إشكال آخر وهو: أنهم صرحوا بأن الصورة إنما تكون علماً إذا كانت مطابقة للخارج وذلك لأن هذا إنما هو في صور الأعيان الخارجية، وأما المعدومات من الاعتباريات وغيرها فمعنى مطابقتها ما ذكرنا هذا، وفي بعض المواضع من كلام ابن سينا اعتراف بأن العلم بالممتنعات ليس حصول الصورة لأنه ذكر في الشفاء أن المستحيل لا يحصل له صورة في العقل ولا يمكن أن يتصور شيء هو اجتماع النقيضين بل تصور المستحيل إنما يكون على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع، ثم يقال مثل هذا الأمر لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض، أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض وعلى هذا حمل صاحب المواقف كلام أبي هاشم حيث جعل العلم بالمستحيل علماً لا معلوم له بناء على أن المعلوم شيء والمستحيل ليس بشيء وحينئذ لا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأنه تناقض إذ لا معنى للمعلوم سوى ما تعلق به العلم ولا يحتاج إلى ما ذكره الأمدي من أن له أن يصطلح على أن المعلوم ما تعلق به العلم من الأشياء.
The known is what is in the external [world] as previously stated, and by this another objection is repelled, namely: they declared that the mental image (al-ṣūrah) is only knowledge when it corresponds to the external, because that is only regarding images of external entities. As for non-existents (al-maʿdūmāt) among constructs (al-iʿtibāriyyāt) and others, the meaning of their correspondence is what we have mentioned. And in some places of Ibn Sīnā's speech is an acknowledgment that knowledge of impossible things (al-mumtaniʿāt) is not the acquisition of a mental image, because he mentioned in al-Shifāʾ that the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) does not have an image in the intellect, and it is not possible to imagine something that is the conjunction of two contradictories. Rather, the conceptualization of the impossible is only by way of analogy (tashbīh), such that one understands between blackness and sweetness an entity that is conjunction, then it is said that such an entity is not possible between blackness and whiteness; or by way of negation (nafy), such that the intellect judges that it is not possible for a concept that is the conjunction of blackness and whiteness to exist. And upon this the author of al-Mawāqif interpreted the speech of Abū Hāshim where he made knowledge of the impossible a knowledge without an object (maʿlūm), based on the fact that the known (al-maʿlūm) is a thing and the impossible is not a thing; and then the objection of the Imām [al-Rāzī] that it is contradictory is not valid, since there is no meaning of 'known' except that which knowledge attaches to, and there is no need for what al-Āmidī mentioned that he could stipulate that the known is what knowledge attaches to among things.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is an argument and refutation. It begins by reiterating that the known (al-maʿlūm) is what exists externally, as established earlier. This principle refutes another objection: the claim that mental images are knowledge only when they correspond to external reality is true only for images of actually existing entities. For non-existent mental constructs (al-maʿdūmāt al-iʿtibāriyyah), correspondence means something different, as previously explained. The author then cites Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna), who acknowledged that knowledge of impossible things (al-mumtaniʿāt) is not the acquisition of a mental image. In al-Shifāʾ, Ibn Sīnā stated that the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) cannot have an image in the mind, nor can one imagine a conjunction of two contradictories (ijtimāʿ al-naqīḍayn). Instead, conceptualizing the impossible occurs either by analogy (tashbīh) — e.g., understanding a conjunction between blackness and sweetness and then recognizing it cannot exist between blackness and whiteness — or by negation (nafy) — the intellect judges that a concept of a conjunction of blackness and whiteness cannot exist. The author then relates that the author of al-Mawāqif (al-Ījī) interpreted Abū Hāshim’s statement that knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object (ʿilm lā maʿlūm lahu) as consistent, because 'known' means a thing, and the impossible is not a thing. Thus, Imām al-Rāzī’s objection that this is contradictory is invalid, since the term 'known' only means that which knowledge attaches to. Consequently, al-Āmidī’s suggestion that one could simply stipulate a definition is unnecessary.

The Four Types of Perception: Sensation, Imagination, Estimation, and Intellect
Classification of perception into four types with conditions and definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of perception in Islamic epistemology and how they differ in terms of abstraction from matter and particularity.

Core Idea
The four types of perception form a hierarchy of abstraction from matter.

In Islamic epistemology, perception (idrāk) is not limited to sensory experience. Scholars distinguish four modes of knowing, each with its own conditions and objects. This passage from al-Maqasid lays out the foundational classification and defines each type with its degree of abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Notice that the author begins by stating there are four types of perception. This sets the stage for the definitions that follow.
The four types are sensation, imagination, estimation, and intellect.
2
Identify the conditions
Sensation has three conditions: presence of matter, surrounding states, and particularity. Each subsequent type is free from one or more conditions.
This shows a progression of abstraction.
3
Understand each definition
Sensation perceives particulars in matter with sensible qualities; imagination retains these qualities without matter; estimation perceives non-sensible meanings tied to particulars; intellect perceives essences abstractly.
Focus on what each type perceives and its object.
4
Compare abstraction levels
See how each type removes conditions, culminating in intellect which is completely abstract. Create a mental table to compare.
The hierarchy: sensation (matter+states+particular), imagination (states+particular), estimation (particular), intellect (none).
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint to ensure you can differentiate the types based on their conditions and objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني قال : أنواع الإدراك أربعة : إحساس، وتخيل، وتوهم وتعقل والاحساس مشروط لحضور المادة واكتناف الهيئات، وكون المدرك جزئياً، والتخيل مجرد عن الأول، والتوهم عن الأولين والتعقل عن الكل. الإحساس إدراك للشيء الموجود في المادة الحاضرة عند المدرك على هيئات مخصوصة به محسوسة من الأين والوضع، ونحو ذلك. والتخيل : إدراك لذلك الشيء مع الهيئات المذكورة، ولكن في حالتي حضوره وغيبته. والتوهم : إدراك لمعان غير محسوسة من الكيفيات والإضافات مخصوصة بالشيء الجزئي الموجود في المادة. والتعقل : إدراك للشيء من حيث هو هو فقط، لا من حيث شيء آخر سواء أخذ وحده أو مع غيره من الصفات المدركة هذا النوع من الإدراك، فالإحساس مشروط بثلاثة أشياء حضور المادة واكتناف الهيئات، وكون المدرك جزئياً. والتخيل : مجرد عن الشرط الأول، والتوهم : مجرد عن
The second section. He said: The types of perception are four: sensation (iḥsās), imagination (takhayyul), estimation (wahm), and intellect (taʿaql). Sensation is conditioned by the presence of matter (mādda), the surrounding of states (hayʾāt), and that the perceived is particular (juzʾī). Imagination is free from the first [condition], estimation is free from the first two, and intellect is free from all. Sensation is perception of a thing existing in matter present to the perceiver, with specific sensible states (hayʾāt) such as location (ayn) and position (waḍʿ), and the like. Imagination is perception of that same thing with the mentioned states, but in both its presence and absence. Estimation is perception of non-sensible meanings (maʿānī) such as qualities (kayfiyyāt) and relations (iḍāfāt) specific to the particular thing existing in matter. Intellect is perception of a thing just as it is only, not in terms of anything else, whether taken alone or with other attributes that perceive this type of perception. So sensation is conditioned by three things: presence of matter, surrounding states, and that the perceived is particular. Imagination is free from the first condition, estimation is free from
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition section. It begins by stating that the types of perception (al-idrāk) are four: sensation (iḥsās), imagination (takhayyul), estimation (wahm), and intellect (taʿaql). It then outlines the conditions associated with each. Sensation (iḥsās) is conditioned by three things: (1) presence of matter (ḥuḍūr al-mādda), (2) surrounding states (iktināf al-hayʾāt) – i.e., the sensible accidents like location and posture, and (3) that the perceived object is particular (juzʾī), i.e., an individual object. Imagination (takhayyul) is free from the first condition only, meaning it can occur without the matter being present, but it retains the states and particularity. Estimation (wahm) is free from the first two conditions, meaning it can occur without matter and without the sensible states, but it still pertains to particular meanings. Intellect (taʿaql) is free from all three – it abstracts from matter, states, and particularity. The passage then provides precise definitions: Sensation perceives an external object present in matter with sensible accidents like location and position. Imagination perceives the same object with its sensible accidents whether the object is present or absent. Estimation perceives non-sensible meanings (maʿānī) like qualities and relations that are specific to the particular material thing. Intellect perceives a thing in its essence (min ḥaythu huwa huwa), without considering any other aspect, either alone or with attributes that perceive this type of perception. Finally, it repeats the conditions for sensation (three) and notes that imagination is free from the first, estimation from the first two. The text is cut off at 'estimation is free from' but the pattern implies estimation is free from the first two and intellect from all three.

Key Takeaway
The four types of perception differ by how many conditions (matter, states, particularity) they require; intellect is fully abstract, while sensation requires all three.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Make a firm intention to cleanse your concept of God from any traces of anthropomorphism.

Are you aware of how your estimation often judges people based on their outward appearance or circumstances, leading to prejudice? Will you commit to using your intellect to judge by inner reality rather than your estimation?

Defining Forgetfulness, Oversight, and Two Types of Ignorance
Defines four epistemological terms in relation to perceptual images and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theology distinguishes between forgetfulness (nisyān) and oversight (sahw), and between simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab).

Core Idea
Forgetfulness is loss requiring re-acquisition; simple ignorance is absence of knowledge.

In this lesson, we delve into precise definitions of key epistemological terms used in kalam. The author clarifies how oblivion (dhuhūl) relates to perceptual images, and distinguishes between two types of ignorance. Understanding these terms is foundational for later discussions on divine attributes and human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Note the technical terms.
2
Examine the distinction between nisyān and sahw
Observe that both come from dhuhūl (oblivion), but differ in whether the image is permanently lost or temporarily absent.
The condition is whether re-acquisition is needed.
3
Distinguish jahl basīṭ and jahl murakkab
Simple ignorance is absence of knowledge; compound ignorance is the opposite because it involves a false belief.
The author says compound ignorance is opposed (muḍādd) to simple ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والذهول عن الصورة الإدراكية إن انتهى إلى زوالها بحيث يفتقر إلى اکتساب فنسيان وإلا ،فسهو والجهل البسيط عـدم ملكة العلم، والمركب مضاد له لصدق الحد .
He said: And oblivion (dhuhūl) concerning the perceptual image: if it leads to its removal such that it requires acquisition, then it is forgetfulness (nisyān); otherwise, it is oversight (sahw). And simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) is the absence of the faculty of knowledge, and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) is opposed to it according to the truthfulness of the definition.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a concise definition of four terms. It begins with dhuhūl (oblivion) regarding a perceptual image (al-ṣūra al-idrākiyya). If the oblivion results in the image being removed from the mind to the extent that one needs to reacquire it (by studying again, etc.), that is nisyān (forgetfulness). If the image is merely absent from consciousness temporarily but not lost, that is sahw (oversight). Then it defines jahl basīṭ (simple ignorance) as the mere absence of the faculty of knowledge — that is, not knowing something without being aware that one does not know. Jahl murakkab (compound ignorance) is the opposite of jahl basīṭ, meaning one mistakenly believes oneself to know when one does not. The phrase 'according to the truthfulness of the definition' indicates that compound ignorance contradicts the definition of knowledge, so it is its opposite.

Key Terms
dhuhūl
ṣūra idrākiyya
nisyān
sahw
jahl basīṭ
jahl murakkab

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple ignorance and compound ignorance are essentially the same or similar.
Correction: According to the passage, compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) is opposed (muḍādd) to simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ). Simple ignorance is absence of the faculty of knowledge, while compound ignorance involves a false belief that contradicts the definition of knowledge.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes nisyān from sahw according to the passage?
A) Nisyān is permanent, sahw is temporary
B) Nisyān requires reacquisition of the image, sahw does not
C) Nisyān involves the opposite of knowledge, sahw does not
D) There is no distinction; they are synonyms

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness is the loss of a perceptual image requiring re-acquisition, while simple ignorance is the absence of the knowledge faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If simple ignorance is merely absence of knowledge, why do you think people often fear admitting it? How can embracing simple ignorance (acknowledging you don't know) become a path to true knowledge rather than a weakness?

Distinction between Sahw and Nisyān as Forms of Privation
Explains the difference between sahw and nisyān by analogy to death and life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sahw (oversight) and nisyān (forgetfulness) as two distinct forms of privation, and how they relate to non-existence.

Core Idea
Sahw and nisyān are distinct privations, not identical absences of knowledge.

This passage clarifies a subtle but important distinction between two terms often confused: sahw (oversight) and nisyān (forgetfulness). The author uses a vivid analogy — comparing their relation to non-existence to the relation of death to life — to show that both are absences, but with specific differences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's wording.
Notice the compressed style: the passage ends with 'with a specific condition' — that is key.
2
Understand the analogy
The author says that sahw and nisyān relate to non-existence (ʻadam) as death relates to life. This means both are absences, just as death is the absence of life.
But death is not the same as being asleep — both are absences of life but with different conditions.
3
Distinguish the two via their conditions
Each term has a specific condition (qayd) that makes them distinct. Refer to earlier definitions: nisyān is loss requiring reacquisition, sahw is temporary non-presence.
This explains why they are not identical even though both are privations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشير إلى الفرق بين السهو والنسيان، وقد لا يفرق بينهما، ونسبتهما إلى العدم نسبة الموت إلى الحياة، بمعنى أنهما عدم ملكة للعلم مع خصوصية قيد
He points to the difference between oversight (sahw) and forgetfulness (nisyān); and sometimes one does not distinguish between them. And their relation to non-existence is like the relation of death to life, meaning that both are the absence of the faculty of knowledge with a specific condition.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note clarifying the distinction between sahw (oversight) and nisyān (forgetfulness). The author notes that some people do not differentiate between them. He then provides an analogy: their relation to non-existence (ʻadam) is like the relation of death to life. Just as death is the absence of life, sahw and nisyān are both absences of the faculty of knowledge (ʻadam mulkat al-ʻilm). However, they are not identical absences; each has a specific condition (khuṣūṣiyya qayd). The specific conditions were previously defined: nisyān is loss requiring reacquisition, sahw is mere temporary non-presence. The analogy emphasizes that both are forms of privation, not positive states, but with distinct characteristics.

Key Terms
sahw
nisyān
ʻadam
hayāh
mawt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sahw and nisyān are identical, with no difference between them.
Correction: The passage shows they are both absences of knowledge, but each has a specific condition that distinguishes them, just as death and being asleep are both absences of life but differ.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between sahw/nisyān and non-existence?
A) They are positive states of knowledge
B) They are both forms of non-existence, analogous to death relative to life
C) Only nisyān is a form of non-existence
D) They are completely unrelated to non-existence

Key Takeaway
Sahw and nisyān are both absences of the faculty of knowledge, but each has a specific condition that distinguishes them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you forgot (nisyān) or overlooked (sahw) a religious obligation or piece of sacred knowledge. What specific 'condition' (qayd) in your life caused that absence—was it laziness, distraction, or something deeper? How does recognizing that it is a privation, not just a simple mistake, change how you will address it?
?
The analogy compares sahw and nisyān to death in relation to life. In what areas of your faith have you allowed a 'death' of awareness or vigilance to creep in? What is one specific step you will take today to revive that knowledge in your heart, knowing that it requires intentional recovery, not just passive waiting?
?
You now understand that sahw and nisyān are distinct absences with different remedies. When you catch yourself being neglectful in worship, are you quick to attribute it to mere forgetfulness, or do you examine whether it is a deeper loss? What would change if you treated each instance with the seriousness of a diagnosis?

The Nature of Shakk (Doubt) as Tasdiq
Defines shakk as a form of tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that shakk (doubt) is not merely a cognitive state but a form of tasdiq (assent) — a judgment about the equality of two sides.

Core Idea
Shakk is a judgment, not just indecision.

The author begins by addressing a potential misconception: that shakk (doubt) is simply an equal weighing of two possibilities. He argues that this very assessment is itself an act of tasdiq (assent) — a judgment about the state of one's knowledge. This sets the stage for understanding refined distinctions among epistemic states.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once to see how the author presents an objection and then his reply.
The objection is introduced with 'فإن قيل'.
2
Identify the core point
The author insists that the very judgment of equal probability is a form of assent (tasdiq). Reflect on why this matters for defining doubt.
3
Connect to the broader context
This clarification about shakk leads directly into the definitions of sahw and nisyan in the next lesson.
The phrase 'أحد الأقسام الأربعة' hints at a classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المراد بالشك الحكم بتساوى الطرفين عند العقل .
قلنا : هذا تصديق بكون أحد الأقسام الأربعة بمنزلة قولك : أنا شاك في الفرق بين السهو والنسيان
قال : والذهول عن الصورة الإدراكية إن انتهى إلى زوالها بحيث يفتقر إلى اكتساب فنسيان وإلا فسهو والجهل البسيط عدم ملكة العلم، والمركب مضاد له لصدق الحد .
وقالت المعتزلة مماثل لأن الاختلاف إنما هو بعارض المطابقة واللامطابقة، ولأن العلم ينقلب جهلا مع بقاء ذاته كما إذا اعتقد قيام زيد طول نهاره، وقد قعد في البعض.
والجواب أن العارض قد يكون لازماً فتختلف الذات باختلافه واتحاد الذات في الحالين نفس المتنازع .
يشير إلى الفرق بين السهو والنسيان، وقد لا يفرق بينهما، ونسبتهما إلى العدم نسبة الموت إلى الحياة، بمعنى أنهما عدم ملكة للعلم مع خصوصية قيد الطريان
If it is said: What is meant by shakk (doubt) is the judgment of the equality of the two sides according to reason. We say: This is a tasdiq (assent) that one of the four divisions is like your saying: I am doubtful about the difference between sahw (inattention) and nisyan (forgetfulness). He said: The inattention from the perceptual image, if it ends in its removal such that it requires acquisition, then it is nisyan, and if not, then it is sahw. And jahl basit (simple ignorance) is the absence of the faculty of knowledge, and compound ignorance is its opposite due to the applicability of the definition. The Mu'tazila said they are similar, because the difference is only by the accident of correspondence and non-correspondence, and because knowledge turns into ignorance while its essence remains, as when one believes that Zayd is standing all day long while he sat for part of it. The answer: The accident may be necessary (lazim), so the essence differs by its difference, and the unity of the essence in the two states is the very point of dispute. [This] indicates the difference between sahw and nisyan, and one may not distinguish between them, and their relationship to non-existence is like the relationship of death to life, meaning they are absence of the faculty of knowledge with the particular condition of occurrence (qayd al-turyan).
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a definition, an objection-and-answer, and further explanatory commentary. It begins with an objection: 'If it is said that shakk (doubt) means the judgment of equality of two sides in reason, we reply that this is itself a tasdiq (assent) — namely, a judgment about one of the four (presumably logical) categories. It is like saying 'I am doubtful about the difference between sahw and nisyan', which is a statement of a cognitive state. Then the author gives definitions: al-dhuhul (inattention or mental distraction) from a perceptual image, if it leads to complete loss requiring re-acquisition, is nisyan (forgetfulness); if not, it is sahw (simple oversight). Jahl basit (simple ignorance) is defined as the absence of the faculty of knowledge. Jahl murakkab (compound ignorance) is said to be opposite (did) of knowledge because the definition of opposites applies. Next, the Mu'tazila object: knowledge and ignorance are similar (mumathil) because the difference between them is only an accident (arad) of correspondence (mutabaqa) or non-correspondence with reality, and because a single belief can change from knowledge to ignorance while its essence remains (e.g., believing Zayd standing all day when he sat part of the time). The author answers that an accident may be a necessary attribute (lazim), such that the essence changes with it; and the claim that the essence is the same in both states is precisely the point at issue. The passage concludes with a further remark clarifying sahw and nisyan: they are like death to life, i.e., they are absence of the faculty of knowledge with the particular condition of occurrence (qayd al-turyan). This means they are privations of a specific type.

Key Terms
شك
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is shakk best understood?
A) As a suspension of judgment.
B) As a form of tasdiq (assent).
C) As pure ignorance.
D) As a type of forgetfulness.

Key Takeaway
Shakk (doubt) is a form of tasdiq (assent) in which one judges that two possibilities are equally probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced shakk (doubt) about your own faith or knowledge? Did you realize that even that doubt was a judgment you were making? How does acknowledging shakk as tasdiq affect your responsibility to seek certainty?
?
You say 'I'm not sure' about a matter of aqeedah. That statement itself is a claim. What does this lesson demand you to do with that claim? Will you continue to hold it passively, or will you actively examine it?
?
The passage forces you to see doubt as an assent—a decision to believe you are uncertain. What if your doubt is not a neutral state but a choice? What would it mean to choose certainty instead?
