Defining Sahw, Nisyan, and Types of Ignorance
Defines sahw, nisyan, jahl basit, jahl murakkab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sahw (inattention), nisyan (forgetfulness), jahl basit (simple ignorance), and jahl murakkab (compound ignorance) as defined by the author.

Core Idea
Sahw and nisyan are degrees of inattention; jahl basit is absence of knowledge.

After clarifying the nature of doubt, the author defines other epistemic states. He explains that inattention from a perceptual image, if it leads to complete loss requiring re-acquisition, is forgetfulness (nisyan); otherwise it is simple oversight (sahw). He then defines simple ignorance as the absence of the faculty of knowledge and compound ignorance as its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines sahw, nisyan, jahl basit, and jahl murakkab.
Notice the conditional structure: 'إن انتهى... فنسيان وإلا فسهو'.
2
Compare sahw and nisyan
The key difference is whether the inattention leads to permanent loss of the mental image requiring re-acquisition (nisyan) or not (sahw).
3
Understand jahl basit and jahl murakkab
Jahl basit is simply the absence of knowledge (a privation). Jahl murakkab is its opposite because it involves a false belief that contradicts knowledge.
The phrase 'لصدق الحد' refers to the definition of opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What distinguishes nisyan from sahw?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

And does that change how you should respond to your Lord? Commit to one concrete action to guard against nisyan in your worship.

Mu'tazili Objection and Response on Knowledge and Ignorance
Responds to Mu'tazili objection about similarity of knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that knowledge and ignorance are essentially similar and the author's counterargument using the concept of necessary accident (arad lazim).

Core Idea
Knowledge and ignorance differ by essential accidents, not merely by correspondence.

The Mu'tazila argue that knowledge and ignorance are fundamentally the same mental state, differing only by an accidental property of correspondence with reality. They claim that a single belief can change from knowledge to ignorance without changing its essence. The author responds by asserting that some accidents are necessary (lazim), and thus a difference in accident can indicate a difference in essence. This debate clarifies the ontological status of ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili argument
The Mu'tazila claim that knowledge and ignorance are 'mumathil' (similar) because the only difference is the accident of correspondence. They give an example of a belief that changes status while the mental content remains the same.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author replies that accidents can be necessary (lazim), in which case they determine the essence. The very claim that the essence is the same in both cases is disputed.
The phrase 'اتحاد الذات في الحالين نفس المتنازع' is key.
3
Apply the distinction to sahw and nisyan
The passage concludes by applying this logic to sahw and nisyan: they are both absences of the faculty of knowledge but differ by the condition of occurrence (qayd al-turyan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المراد بالشك الحكم بتساوى الطرفين عند العقل .
قلنا : هذا تصديق بكون أحد الأقسام الأربعة بمنزلة قولك : أنا شاك في الفرق بين السهو والنسيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge and ignorance are essentially the same mental state differing only by correspondence with reality.
Correction: The author argues that the accident of correspondence may be necessary (lazim), so the essence differs; the claim of essential unity is the very point in dispute.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the Mu'tazili argument that knowledge and ignorance are similar?
A) He agrees that they are similar.
B) He argues that accidents can be necessary, so essence differs.
C) He denies that knowledge can turn into ignorance.
D) He changes the subject.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili claim that knowledge and ignorance share an essence is refuted by noting that some accidents (like correspondence) are necessary and thus define the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discover that a belief you held confidently was actually false, do you feel that your very self has changed, or only your relation to reality? How does the author's distinction between essential and accidental change challenge the way you view your own intellectual errors?
?
The Mu'tazilites treat knowledge and ignorance as essentially the same, differing only by correspondence. Are there areas of your religious knowledge where you treat true and false beliefs as having the same root, simply because they feel similar? What would it mean for your faith if some differences are not mere accidents but define your very state of being?
?
When you miss a prayer or forget an important verse, do you treat it as a simple oversight (sahw) or as a deeper negligence (nisyan)? How does the author's distinction between these two types of absence of knowledge challenge your self-accountability before Allah?

The Three Stages of Knowledge
Al-Taftazani enumerates three stages of knowledge and draws an implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three stages of knowledge for a created being: pure potentiality, summary knowledge, and detailed knowledge, and how knowledge of an essence summarily implies knowledge of its parts.

Core Idea
Knowledge progresses from mere potentiality to summary grasp and finally to detailed awareness.

Al-Taftazani begins by laying out a fundamental epistemological framework for Islamic theology. He distinguishes three distinct levels of knowledge that a created being can have regarding any originated thing. Understanding these stages is essential for grasping later discussions about divine attributes and human cognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three stages
Start by reading the Arabic passage from 'علم المخلوق' to 'لكن إجمالاً لا تفصيلا' along with the translation. Notice how each stage is defined and exemplified.
Pay attention to the example of the page and letters.
2
Identify and compare the three stages
Identify each of the three stages: pure potentiality, summary knowledge, and detailed knowledge. Compare their definitions: potentiality is readiness, summary is an all-at-once holistic state, detailed is part-by-part awareness.
The first stage is not actually knowledge but preparedness.
3
Understand the clarification about knowledge of essence
The author concludes that knowledge of an essence necessitates knowledge of its parts, but only summarily. Reflect on why this is important for theology.
This resolves a potential objection about knowing God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم المخلوق ومراتب الحادث ثلاث :
الأولى : ما يكون بالقوة المحضة، وهو الاستعداد للعلم، وحصوله للضروريات يكون بالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة كما يستفاد من حس اللمس أن هذه النار حارة، فتستعد النفس للعلم بأن كل نار حارة، وعلى هذا القياس وللنظريات يكون بالضروريات بأن يرتب فيكتسب النظرى .
والثانية : العلم الإجمالي كمن علم مسألة فغفل عنها ثم سئل فإنه يحضر الجواب في ذهنه دفعة من غير تفصيل، وحقيقته حالة بسيطة إجمالية هي مبدأ تفاصيل المركب.
والثالثة : العلم التفصيلي : وهو حضور صورة المركب بحيث تعرف اجزاؤه متميزاً بعضها عن بعض ملاحظاً كل منها على الانفراد، وذلك كما إذا نظرنا إلى الصحيفة دفعة فلاشك أنا نجد حالة إجمالية من الإبصار، ثم إذا حدقنا النظر وأبصرنا كل حرف حرف على الانفراد حصلت لنا حالة أخرى مع أن الإبصار حاصل في الحالين.
فالأولى : بمنزلة العلم الإجمالي، والثانية بمنزلة العلم التفصيلي وبهذا يتبين معنى كلامهم أن العلم بالماهية يستلزم العلم بأجزائها، لكن إجمالاً لا تفصيلا.
واعترض الإمام بأن الحاصل في العلم الإجمالي إما أن يكون صورة واحدة فيلزم أن يكون للحقائق المختلفة صورة واحدة مطابقة لكل منها على أنها متساوية لها بل نفس ماهيتها، وما أن يكون صوراً متعددة لتلك المختلفات فيكون العلم التفصيلي بها حاصلاً وغاية التفرقة أن يقال إن حصول الصورة إن كان دفعة واحدة فعلم إجمالي، وإن كان على ترتيب زماني بأن يحضر واحد بعد واحد فتفصيلي، لكن على هذا لا يكون الإجمالي مرتبة متوسطة بين القوة المحضة، والفعل المحض التفصيلي على ما زعموا ويمكن الجواب بأن الحاصل في الإجمالي صورة واحدة تطابق الكل من غير ملاحظة لتفاصيل الأجزاء، وفي التفصيلي صور متعددة يطابق كل منها واحداً من الأجزاء على الانفراد، وفهم بعضهم من العلم الإجمالي مجرد تميز الشيء عند العقل، ومن التفصيلي ذلك مع العلم بتميزه، وقد سبق الكلام في أن العلم بالشيء هل يستلزم العلم بالعلم به، وفي أنه على تقدير الاستلزام هل يلزم من العلم بشيء واحد علوم غير متناهية، بناء على تغاير العلم بالامتياز وبامتياز الامتياز ، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية .
The knowledge of the created and the stages of originated (thing) are three: The first: that which is in pure potentiality, and it is the preparedness for knowledge, and its attainment of necessary (knowledge) occurs through the external and internal senses, as is derived from the sense of touch that this fire is hot, so the soul becomes prepared for the knowledge that every fire is hot, and on this analogy, and for theoretical (matters) it occurs through necessary (knowledge) by arranging so that the theoretical is acquired. The second: summary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī), like one who knew a matter then became heedless of it, then was asked, and he brings the answer to his mind all at once without detail, and its reality is a simple, summary state that is the principle of the details of the composite. The third: detailed knowledge (al-ʿilm al-tafṣīlī): it is the presence of the form of the composite such that its parts are known distinguished from each other, each one being observed individually. For example, when we look at a page at once, there is no doubt that we find a summary state of vision; then when we focus our gaze and see each letter individually, another state occurs for us, while vision is present in both states. Thus the first is like summary knowledge, and the second is like detailed knowledge, and by this the meaning of their statement becomes clear: that knowledge of the essence necessitates knowledge of its parts, but summarily, not in detail. And the Imām objected that what is obtained in summary knowledge is either one form, so that different realities would have one form corresponding to each of them, being equal to them, indeed their very essence, or it would be multiple forms for those different things, so that detailed knowledge of them would be obtained, and the ultimate distinction is to say that if the occurrence of the form is all at once, it is summary knowledge, and if it is in temporal order, one after another, it is detailed. But on this, the summary would not be a middle stage between pure potentiality and pure detailed actuality, as they claimed. And it is possible to answer that what is obtained in the summary is one form that corresponds to the whole without observing the details of the parts, and in the detailed there are multiple forms, each corresponding to one of the parts individually. And some understood from summary knowledge mere distinction of the thing in the intellect, and from detailed that with knowledge of its distinction. And the discussion has already preceded concerning whether knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of the knowledge of it, and whether, assuming this necessitation, one knowledge of a single thing necessitates infinite knowledges, based on the diversity of knowledge of distinction and knowledge of the distinction of distinction, and so on to infinity.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological classification and defends it against an objection. The author first enumerates three stages of knowledge for a created being regarding originated things. The first stage is pure potentiality (al-quwwa al-mahḍa), which is readiness for knowledge. For necessary truths, it is actualized through the senses, preparing the mind for universal judgments. For theoretical truths, it is actualized by arranging necessary premises. The second stage is summary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī), exemplified by someone who knows an answer but becomes heedless, then recalls it all at once without detail. Its reality is a simple, summary state from which the details of the composite proceed. The third stage is detailed knowledge (al-ʿilm al-tafṣīlī), where the form of the composite is present with its parts distinguished and observed individually, like reading a page letter by letter after a holistic glance. The author then compares these stages: the first (potentiality) is like summary knowledge relative to detailed? Actually, he says: "Thus the first is like summary knowledge, and the second like detailed knowledge" – meaning that potentiality is to the actualization of summary knowledge as summary is to detailed. This clarifies the maxim that knowledge of a thing's essence necessarily implies knowledge of its parts, but only summarily, not in detail. 

Then an objection from 'the Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) is presented. The objection argues that in summary knowledge, what is obtained is either a single form or multiple forms. If a single form, then different realities would share one form, which would be identical to their essences—contradicting their diversity. If multiple forms, then detailed knowledge would already be present, eliminating the distinction. Moreover, if the difference is merely temporal (all at once vs. sequential), then summary would not be a middle stage between potentiality and detailed actuality, as the proponents claim. 

The author provides an answer: in summary knowledge, a single form corresponds to the whole without observing the details; in detailed knowledge, multiple forms each correspond to one part individually. Thus summary is genuinely intermediate: it has actuality (the form is present) but lacks detail. The passage then notes that some scholars interpret summary knowledge as mere mental distinction of the thing, and detailed as that plus awareness of the distinction. Finally, the author refers to a previous discussion about whether knowledge of a thing requires knowledge of that knowledge, and whether this leads to infinite regress—a separate but related epistemological issue.

Key Terms

العلم الإجمالي
العلم التفصيلي
الضروريات
النظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think summary knowledge is simply incomplete detailed knowledge.
Correction: Summary knowledge is a distinct state that is the principle of details, not merely a lesser degree of detail.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of summary knowledge?
A) It is the preparedness for knowledge.
B) It is a simple, summary state that is the principle of the details of the composite.
C) It is the presence of the form with parts distinguished individually.
D) It is the same as potentiality.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a thing's essence implies knowledge of its parts summarily, not in detail, which is crucial for understanding how we know God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know God's attributes, but after reading this passage, are you honest enough to admit your knowledge is mostly summary? What would it take for you to pursue detailed knowledge of even one attribute, and what might that cost you?
?
Think of a religious truth you hold—like 'Allah is just.' According to Taftazani, does your knowledge of that truth match the stage of pure potentiality, summary, or detailed? How does your answer expose a gap between what you profess and what you've actually worked to understand?
?
The passage teaches that knowing a thing's essence only requires summary knowledge of its parts. Where in your life have you settled for summary knowledge about your faith, using it as an excuse for not diving deeper? Commit today to pick one concept from this lesson and transform it from summary to detailed—what is your first step?

Logic and Technical Terms

7 min
Objection to Summary Knowledge and Its Defense
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes an objection against summary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by Imam al-Razi against the coherence of summary knowledge as a middle stage, and how al-Taftazani answers it.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge is a genuine intermediate stage, not reducible to potentiality or detailed knowledge.

After presenting the three stages, al-Taftazani addresses a serious objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The objection challenges whether summary knowledge can truly be a distinct intermediate stage between potentiality and detailed actuality. Understanding this defense is key to appreciating the sophistication of Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read from 'واعترض الإمام' to the end of the passage. Note the two horns of the dilemma: if summary knowledge is a single form, it contradicts diversity; if multiple forms, it becomes detailed.
The objection uses a logical dilemma.
2
Understand the temporal distinction objection
The Imam also argues that if the difference is only temporal (all at once vs. sequential), summary knowledge would not be a middle stage between potentiality and detailed actuality.
This targets the claim that summary is intermediate.
3
Analyze the author's answer
Al-Taftazani replies that in summary knowledge, a single form corresponds to the whole without observing details; in detailed knowledge, multiple forms correspond to parts individually. This preserves the middle status.
The answer avoids the dilemma by distinguishing the mode of correspondence.
4
Consider alternative interpretation
The passage mentions that some understand summary as mere distinction, and detailed as distinction plus awareness. Reflect on how this aligns with the main answer.
This is an additional note, not the primary answer.

علم المخلوق ومراتب الحادث ثلاث :
الأولى : ما يكون بالقوة المحضة، وهو الاستعداد للعلم، وحصوله للضروريات يكون بالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة كما يستفاد من حس اللمس أن هذه النار حارة، فتستعد النفس للعلم بأن كل نار حارة، وعلى هذا القياس وللنظريات يكون بالضروريات بأن يرتب فيكتسب النظرى .

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological classification and defends it against an objection. The author first enumerates three stages of knowledge for a created being regarding originated things. The third stage is detailed knowledge (al-ʿilm al-tafṣīlī), where the form of the composite is present with its parts distinguished and observed individually, like reading a page letter by letter after a holistic glance. This clarifies the maxim that knowledge of a thing's essence necessarily implies knowledge of its parts, but only summarily, not in detail. 

Then an objection from 'the Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) is presented. The objection argues that in summary knowledge, what is obtained is either a single form or multiple forms. If a single form, then different realities would share one form, which would be identical to their essences—contradicting their diversity. If multiple forms, then detailed knowledge would already be present, eliminating the distinction. Moreover, if the difference is merely temporal (all at once vs. sequential), then summary would not be a middle stage between potentiality and detailed actuality, as the proponents claim. 

The author provides an answer: in summary knowledge, a single form corresponds to the whole without observing the details; in detailed knowledge, multiple forms each correspond to one part individually. Thus summary is genuinely intermediate: it has actuality (the form is present) but lacks detail. The passage then notes that some scholars interpret summary knowledge as mere mental distinction of the thing, and detailed as that plus awareness of the distinction. Finally, the author refers to a previous discussion about whether knowledge of a thing requires knowledge of that knowledge, and whether this leads to infinite regress—a separate but related epistemological issue.

Comprehensive and Detailed Knowledge
Author defines comprehensive and detailed knowledge and addresses an infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between comprehensive and detailed knowledge, including temporal and non-temporal definitions, and how the author addresses the objection about the intermediate status of comprehensive knowledge.

Core Idea
Comprehensive knowledge is a single form of the whole; detailed knowledge is multiple forms for each part.

The author begins by clarifying two types of knowledge: comprehensive (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī). He presents a non‑temporal and a temporal distinction, then answers an objection that comprehensive knowledge would not be an intermediate stage between pure potentiality and pure actuality. The passage also references a prior discussion on infinite regress in knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms 'ilm ijmālī and 'ilm tafsīlī.
The passage contains a brief reference to a previous discussion on infinite regress.
2
Identify the two definitions of comprehensive knowledge
The author gives two ways to distinguish: first by whether the mental form is single or multiple, second by whether the forms are obtained all at once or sequentially. Understand both.
3
Study the objection and answer
The objection is that on the temporal definition, comprehensive knowledge would not be an intermediate stage. The answer states that comprehensive knowledge involves one form matching the whole without details, while detailed knowledge involves multiple forms each matching a part.
This answer preserves comprehensive knowledge as an intermediate stage.
4
Note the infinite regress reference
The author refers to a previous discussion about whether knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of that knowledge, leading to an infinite chain. Understand that this is a related issue but not fully expanded here.
This is a separate problem that arises if one assumes entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكل منها على أنها (۱) متساوية لها بل نفس ماهيتها، وما أن يكون صوراً متعددة لتلك المختلفات فيكون العلم التفصيلي بها حاصلاً وغاية التفرقة أن يقال إن حصول الصورة إن كان دفعة واحدة فعلم ،إجمالي، وإن كان على ترتيب زماني بأن يحضر واحد بعد واحد فتفصيلي، لكن على هذا لا يكون الإجمالي مرتبة متوسطة بين القوة المحضة، والفعل المحض التفصيلي على ما زعموا ويمكن الجواب بأن الحاصل في الإجمالي صورة واحدة تطابق الكل من غير ملاحظة لتفاصيل الأجزاء، وفي التفصيلي صور متعددة يطابق كل منها واحداً من الأجزاء على الانفراد، وفهم بعضهم من العلم الإجمالي مجرد تميز الشيء عند العقل، ومن التفصيلي ذلك مع العلم بتميزه، وقد سبق الكلام في أن العلم بالشيء هل يستلزم العلم بالعلم به، وفي أنه على تقدير الاستلزام هل يلزم من العلم بشيء واحد علوم غير متناهية، بناء على تغاير العلم بالامتياز وبامتياز الامتياز ، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية .
(۱) سقط من (ب) حرف الجر (على).
For each of them [that they are] identical to it, but rather the same essence, and that they are multiple forms for those different things, so detailed knowledge of them is attained. And the ultimate distinction is to say: if the attainment of the form is all at once, it is comprehensive knowledge; and if it is in a temporal order, such that one is presented after another, then it is detailed. But according to this, comprehensive knowledge would not be an intermediate stage between pure potentiality and pure actual detailed knowledge, as they claimed. And the answer is possible: that what is attained in comprehensive knowledge is a single form that matches the whole without observing the details of the parts, while in detailed knowledge there are multiple forms each matching one of the parts individually. And some understood comprehensive knowledge as mere distinction of a thing by the intellect, and detailed knowledge as that along with knowledge of its distinction. And the discussion has preceded concerning whether knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of the knowledge of it, and whether, on the assumption of entailment, knowledge of one thing necessitates infinite sciences, based on the difference between the knowledge of the distinction and the distinction of the distinction, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses comprehensive (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili) knowledge. It begins by noting that if the mental image is single and matches all, then the image is the same for all; but if there are multiple images for different things, then detailed knowledge is achieved. The distinction can also be made based on time: if the image is obtained all at once, it is comprehensive; if sequentially, it is detailed. However, this leads to the problem that comprehensive knowledge would not be an intermediate stage between pure potentiality and pure actuality. The author offers an answer: in comprehensive knowledge, there is one image matching the whole without noting details; in detailed knowledge, multiple images each matching a part. Another understanding is mentioned: comprehensive knowledge is mere mental distinction of a thing, while detailed knowledge is that plus awareness of the distinction. The passage then references a previous discussion about whether knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of that knowledge, and whether that leads to infinite regress (ilal ghayr al-nihaya).

Key Terms
علم إجمالي
علم تفصيلي
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that comprehensive knowledge is merely incomplete or less detailed knowledge, but it is actually a distinct kind involving a single form that matches the whole without details.
Correction: The author clarifies that comprehensive knowledge is not simply incomplete detailed knowledge; it is a separate category with its own definition: a single mental form for the whole, while detailed knowledge requires multiple forms for parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes comprehensive knowledge?
A) Knowledge obtained through sequential reasoning.
B) A single mental form that matches the whole without observing details.
C) Multiple mental forms each matching a part.
D) Knowledge of the distinction and awareness of that distinction.

Key Takeaway
Comprehensive knowledge is a single mental form representing the whole without details, while detailed knowledge involves multiple forms each representing a part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that comprehensive knowledge is a single form representing the whole without details. In your own religious learning, do you often stop at that comprehensive stage, content with a single, undifferentiated understanding of a truth? Where in your life are you settling for a fuzzy mental form when Allah commands you to know Him with detailed knowledge? What is one belief about Allah that you only know comprehensively, and what is a specific step you will take today to gain detailed knowledge of it?
?
The passage mentions that some define comprehensive knowledge as mere distinction (tamyiz) and detailed knowledge as that distinction plus knowledge of that distinction. When you learn a new Islamic ruling, do you only know it in a way that you can distinguish it from other rulings, or do you also know that you know it—that is, are you consciously aware of your own act of knowing? If not, how might that lack of self-awareness lead to blind following or forgetfulness? Commit now to reviewing one recently learned ruling until you can explain both the ruling and the reasoning behind your knowledge of it.
?
In your pursuit of Islamic knowledge, are you more focused on acquiring many detailed facts (multiple forms) or on grasping the comprehensive single form that unifies them? Which approach has Allah commanded more? The Prophet ﷺ said, 'Whoever Allah wishes good for, He gives him understanding of the religion.' Understanding (fiqh) often means detailed knowledge applied. Are you willing to trade the comfort of a single, all-encompassing idea for the hard work of mastering its parts? Write down one topic where you have only a comprehensive knowledge and commit to breaking it down into three detailed points before next week.

The Debate on Knowledge Transformation: Theoretical vs. Necessary
Author enumerates four positions on knowledge transformation with supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions among theologians on whether theoretical knowledge can become necessary (and vice versa), and the arguments behind each view.

Core Idea
Knowledge types may transform by divine act, but the majority deny necessary becoming theoretical.

This passage presents a key methodological debate in Islamic theology: can one type of knowledge change into another? The discussion involves the possibility of theoretical knowledge becoming necessary through God's creation, and the reverse transformation. Several scholars offer differing opinions based on principles like homogeneity of sciences and essential attributes. Let's explore the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the debate.
Notice the multiple views presented in quick succession.
2
Identify the different positions
List each opinion mentioned: the unanimous view on theoretical becoming necessary, al-Baqillani's permissibility of the reverse, the majority's prevention, and al-Imam's specific objection.
Also note al-Amidi's restatement of the unanimous view.
3
Understand the arguments
For each position, note the supporting argument: homogeneity for al-Baqillani, impossibility of being free from necessary knowledge for the majority, essential attributes for al-Imam.
Focus on the reasoning behind al-Imam's essentialist argument.
4
Connect to broader epistemology
Reflect on how this debate impacts the foundations of knowledge in kalam. The essential nature of primary intuitions is a key point.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
انقلاب العلم النظري إلى ضروري
قال : المبحث الرابع :
قيل : لا خلاف في جواز انقلاب النظري ضرورياً بأن يخلق الله تعالى، وجوز القاضي (1) عكسه لتجانس العلوم بناء على كون التعلقات والتشخصات من العوارض التي ليست مقتضى الذات فيجوز على كل ما يجوز على الآخر، كما يجوز على الإنسانية التى فى زيد ما يجوز على التي في عمرو ومن قال لو سلم التجانس فلا شك فى اختلاف الأنواع ذهل عن معنى التجانس ومنعه الجمهور مطلقا لاستحالة الخلو عن الضروري مع التوجه، وبعضهم فيما هو شرط للنظر للدور).
قال الإمام : لا يجوز انقلاب العلم البديهي كسبياً وبالعكس لأن كون تصور الموضوع والمحمول كافياً في جزم الذهن بالنسبة بينهما، أو مفتقراً إلى النظر أمر ذاتى له والذاتي لا يزول، وهذا مع ظهور المنع على مقدمته الأولى مختص بالأوليات .
(۲)
وذكر الآمدي (٢) وغيره أن انقلاب النظري ضرورياً جائزاً اتفاقاً بأن يخلق الله
The fourth investigation: The transformation of theoretical knowledge into necessary knowledge. He said: The fourth investigation: It was said: There is no dispute regarding the permissibility of theoretical [knowledge] becoming necessary by God's creation. And al-Qadi al-Baqillani permitted its reverse due to the homogeneity of the sciences, based on the fact that relations and individuations are among the accidents that are not required by the essence, so whatever is permissible for one is permissible for the other, just as whatever is permissible for the humanity in Zayd is permissible for that in Amr. And whoever said, 'If homogeneity is granted, there is no doubt about the difference of types,' is heedless of the meaning of homogeneity. And the majority prevented it absolutely, due to the impossibility of being free from necessary [knowledge] when focusing [attention]. And some [restricted it] to what is a condition for theoretical reasoning, due to circularity. Al-Imam said: It is not permissible for intuitive knowledge to become acquired and vice versa, because the sufficiency of the conception of the subject and predicate for the mind's certainty regarding the relation between them, or the need for theoretical reasoning, is an essential [attribute] for it, and the essential does not disappear. And this, along with the obvious prohibition on its first premise, is specific to primary intuitions. And al-Amidi and others mentioned that the transformation of theoretical [knowledge] into necessary [knowledge] is unanimously permissible by God's creation.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is the fourth investigation (al-mabhat al-rabi') on whether theoretical (nazari) knowledge can become necessary (daruri). It states that there is no dispute about the permissibility of theoretical knowledge becoming necessary by God's creation. Then it mentions that al-Qadi al-Baqillani permitted the reverse (necessary becoming theoretical) due to the homogeneity of all sciences, since the specific relations and individuations are accidental and not essential, so what is possible for one is possible for another. However, the majority (al-jumhur) prevented the reverse absolutely, because it is impossible to be free from necessary knowledge when focusing attention (al-tawajjuh). Some restricted it to what is a condition for theoretical reasoning due to circularity (al-dawr). Al-Imam (likely al-Ghazali or al-Razi) argued that intuitive knowledge cannot become acquired and vice versa because the sufficiency of the conception of subject and predicate for judgment is essential (dhati), and the essential does not change. This is specific to primary intuitions (awwaliyat). Then al-Amidi and others mentioned that the transformation of theoretical to necessary is unanimously permissible by God's creation.

Key Terms
علم نظري
علم ضروري
انقلاب
تجانس
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all types of knowledge can freely change from one to another, either by divine act or naturally.
Correction: The passage shows that while theoretical can become necessary by God's creation, the reverse (necessary to theoretical) is strongly contested, and al-Imam argues that primary intuitions cannot change due to essential attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Imam, why can't intuitive knowledge become acquired?
A) Because it is against the sunna.
B) Because it is essential to intuitive knowledge that the conception alone suffices for judgment.
C) Because God does not allow it.
D) Because it leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The transformation of knowledge types is debated; theoretical can become necessary by divine act, but the reverse is mostly rejected due to essential attributes of intuitive knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that some knowledge is so essential that it cannot change. What knowledge do you hold that you would consider 'essential'—so certain that you would not allow it to be transformed? Have you ever experienced a shift in certainty about a core belief? What does that suggest about the nature of your faith?
?
Al-Imam insists that intuitive knowledge (like the certainty of self-evident truths) cannot become acquired or reasoned. Are there beliefs you once accepted on authority (as necessary) but later felt the need to rationalize? Does your reliance on rational proofs strengthen or weaken your certitude?
?
The passage notes that the transformation of theoretical to necessary is possible only by a divine act. If God were to give you direct certainty about a truth you currently struggle to accept through reason, would you welcome it? Or would you resist because it bypasses your intellect? Examine your heart: do you truly trust God to grant certainty, or do you insist on reaching it yourself?

Initial Opinions on Multiplicity of Created Knowledge
First part of the fifth topic: early opinions on multiplicity of created knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early disagreements among scholars on whether created knowledge multiplies with the multiplicity of known objects.

Core Idea
The relation to the known defines whether knowledge multiplies.

The question of whether created knowledge multiplies when its objects multiply has divided scholars. Some argue that the relation (ta‘alluq) to the known is intrinsic to knowledge, so each known requires a distinct act of knowledge. Others counter that the relation is external, as in eternal knowledge, making multiplicity unnecessary. This lesson introduces the first set of opinions and their rationales.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, focusing on the first set of opinions (up to 'وهو ضعيف').
Notice the structure: question, then three opinions with their justifications and rebuttals.
2
Identify the main opinions
Identify the opinion of al-Shaykh and the Mu'tazila (yes, because relation is included), the counter-opinion (no, because relation is external), and the opinion about theoretical knowns.
Each opinion has a specific reasoning.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
Focus on the rebuttal to the third opinion: it is possible for two theoretical knowns to be known by one act of reasoning. The author calls this rebuttal weak.
This shows the ongoing debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: هل يتعدد العلم الحادث بتعدد المعلوم؟ قال الشيخ، وكثير من المعتزلة: نعم، لأن التعلق داخل فيه. وقيل: لا لكونه خارجاً كما في القديم فهو فرع الخلاف في تفسير العلم. وقيل: يتعدد إن كان المعلومان نظريين لئلا يلزم اجتماع النظرين في علم، ورد بجواز أن يحصلا بنظر كما علما بعلم وهو ضعيف. وقال القاضي والإمام: يتعدد إن كان المعلومان مما يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما، وإلا لزم جواز انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه، ورد بأنه قد يعلم تارة بعلم وتارة بعلمين. اتفق القائلون بالعلم القديم على أنه واحد يتعلق بمعلومات متعددة، واختلفوا فى الحادث فذهب الشيخ وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن الواحد منه يمتنع أن يتعلق بمعلومين. وهذا هو المعنى بقولنا بتعدد العلم بتعدد المعلوم. وذهب بعض الأصحاب إلى أنه يجوز. وجعل الإمام الرازي الخلاف مبنياً على الخلاف في تفسير العلم أنه إضافة فيكون التعلق بهذا عين التعلق بذاك، أو صفة ذات إضافة فيجوز أن يكون للواحد تعلقات بأمور متعددة كالعلم القديم.
The Fifth Topic: Does created knowledge multiply with the multiplicity of the known? Al-Shaykh and many of the Mu'tazila said: Yes, because the relation (التعلق) is included in it. And it was said: No, because it is external as in the eternal [knowledge], so it is a branch of the disagreement over the interpretation of knowledge. And it was said: It multiplies if the two knowns are theoretical, to avoid the occurrence of two theoretical sciences in one knowledge, and it was rebutted by the permissibility that they could be obtained by one theoretical reasoning as they were known by one knowledge, and this is weak. Al-Qadi and al-Imam said: It multiplies if the two knowns are such that the knowledge of them can be separated; otherwise, it would entail the permissibility of the separation of a thing from itself. And it was rebutted by [the fact] that it is sometimes known by one knowledge and sometimes by two knowledges. Those who affirm eternal knowledge agreed that it is one and relates to multiple knowns, but they differed concerning created knowledge: al-Shaykh and many of the Mu'tazila held that the one [created knowledge] cannot relate to two knowns. This is the meaning of our statement: 'knowledge multiplies with the multiplicity of the known.' Some of the associates (الأصحاب) held that it is permissible. Imam al-Razi made the disagreement based on the disagreement over the interpretation of knowledge: whether it is a relation (إضافة) so that the relation to this is identical to the relation to that, or a qualified attribute (صفة ذات إضافة) so that it is permissible for the one to have multiple relations to multiple things, like eternal knowledge.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth topic (المبحث الخامس) on whether created knowledge (العلم الحادث) multiplies with the multiplicity of known objects. The author begins by stating the question. Then he reports the opinion of al-Shaykh [Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari?] and many Mu'tazila: yes, because the relation (التعلق) to the known is part of the definition of knowledge. Then another opinion: no, because the relation is external to knowledge, as in eternal knowledge, so this dispute is a branch of the disagreement over the definition of knowledge. Another opinion: it multiplies only if the two knowns are theoretical (نظريان) to avoid combining two theoretical reasonings (النظرين) in one knowledge; this is rebutted by the permissibility that both knowns could be obtained by one theoretical reasoning, as they are known by one knowledge, and the author calls this rebuttal weak. Then al-Qadi and al-Imam [likely al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni] said: it multiplies if the two knowns are such that knowledge of them can be separated; otherwise, it would entail the absurdity that a thing can be separated from itself. This is rebutted by the observation that a thing may sometimes be known by one knowledge and sometimes by two knowledges. Next, the passage notes that those who affirm eternal knowledge (العلم القديم) agree it is one and relates to multiple knowns, but they differ concerning created knowledge: al-Shaykh and many Mu'tazila hold that one created knowledge cannot relate to two knowns. This is the meaning of the statement that knowledge multiplies with the multiplicity of the known. Some associates (الأصحاب) allow it. Finally, Imam al-Razi bases the entire disagreement on the interpretation of knowledge: if knowledge is a relation (إضافة), then the relation to one known is identical to the relation to another, so one knowledge cannot have two relations; if knowledge is a qualified attribute (صفة ذات إضافة), then one knowledge can have multiple relations to multiple things, like eternal knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم الحادث
التعلق
النظريان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third opinion is a universally accepted middle ground.
Correction: The third opinion is presented and immediately rebutted, though the rebuttal is considered weak, indicating it is not widely accepted.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for al-Shaykh's opinion that created knowledge multiplies with the known?
A) Because the relation to the known is external to knowledge.
B) Because the relation to the known is included in the definition of knowledge.
C) Because theoretical knowns cannot be known by one knowledge.
D) Because eternal knowledge is one and relates to many.

Key Takeaway
Early opinions on the multiplicity of created knowledge diverge based on whether the relation to the known is considered intrinsic or extrinsic to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something new, do you feel that your knowledge is a single thing or multiple separate things? How does your answer affect how you organize your beliefs and priorities?
?
This passage shows that scholars disagreed about the nature of knowledge—yet they all sought truth. Are you willing to sit with uncertainty and disagreement in your own understanding of deep matters, or do you demand a single answer to feel safe?

Al-Qadi, Al-Imam, and Al-Razi on Knowledge Multiplicity
Second part: opinions of al-Qadi, al-Imam, and al-Razi's synthesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the later opinions of al-Qadi and al-Imam, the consensus on eternal knowledge, and al-Razi's framework for the disagreement.

Core Idea
Knowledge as a relation vs. a qualified attribute determines multiplicity.

The discussion deepens as al-Qadi and al-Imam introduce a criterion of separability. Meanwhile, scholars agree that eternal knowledge is one yet relates to many, but they differ on created knowledge. Imam al-Razi offers a foundational explanation: the disagreement hinges on whether knowledge is a pure relation or a qualified attribute that can have multiple relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'وقال القاضي والإمام' to the end of the passage.
Pay attention to the arguments and the final synthesis by al-Razi.
2
Examine al-Qadi and al-Imam's opinion
They say knowledge multiplies if the knowns can be separated in knowledge; otherwise, absurdity follows. Note the rebuttal.
Consider the example of knowing something by one knowledge and later by two.
3
Understand the agreement on eternal knowledge
All who affirm eternal knowledge agree it is one and relates to many. The dispute is only about created knowledge.
This sets the stage for the next point.
4
Analyze al-Razi's basis
Al-Razi traces the disagreement to the definition of knowledge: is it a relation (idafa) or a qualified attribute (sifa dhat idafa)?
If relation, one knowledge cannot have two relations; if qualified attribute, it can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Objection on Separable Knowns and Partial Answer
Objection and partial answer on separable knowns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that separable knowns require separate knowledges and the incomplete refutation that only shows the need for two knowledges, not impossibility.

Core Idea
Separable knowns force separate knowledges, or a thing would separate from itself.

This passage presents a key objection raised by two major kalam authorities, al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni. They argue that if two known things can be known separately, then one knowledge cannot cover both, otherwise absurdity follows. The answer, though cut off, begins to indicate that the objection only shows the need for two knowledges, not the impossibility of one knowledge relating to both in some way.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analysis to grasp its flow. Note that the text ends abruptly.
The passage is incomplete in the source.
2
Identify the objection's structure
Find the premise (separability), the conditional (if one knowledge then absurd), and the conclusion (impossible).
3
Understand the absurdity
Why does the objection claim 'separation of a thing from itself'? Because if knowledge is identical for both, knowing one means knowing the other, so separation cannot happen.
4
Examine the partial answer
The answer states that it suffices for separability that they are known by two knowledges. What does this imply about the objection?
The cut-off prevents seeing the full rebuttal, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما قال القاضي وإمام الحرمين : إنه يمتنع إن كان المعلومات بحيث يجوز انفكاك العلم بأحدهما عن العلم الآخر، وإلا يلزم جواز انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه ضرورة أن العلم بهذا نفس العلم بذاك، والتقدير جواز انفكاكهما . وأجيب بأنه يكفي في جواز الانفكاك كونهما معلومين بعلمين في
And as al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn said: It is impossible if the known things are such that the separation of knowledge of one from knowledge of the other is possible; otherwise, the separation of a thing from itself would be necessary, since the knowledge of this is identical to the knowledge of that, and the supposition is the possibility of their separation. And it was answered that it suffices for the possibility of separation that they are known by two knowledges in
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī) and Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) and begins an answer that is cut off. The objection argues that if two known objects are such that knowledge of one can be separated from knowledge of the other (i.e., one can be known without the other), then it is impossible for one knowledge to cover both. Because if one knowledge did cover both, then the knowledge of one would be identical to the knowledge of the other, implying that if one can be known without the other, a thing could separate from itself, which is absurd. Therefore, such separable knowns must be known by two knowledges. The answer, though incomplete, states that for the possibility of separation, it is sufficient that they are known by two knowledges – implying that the objection's condition of separability does not force an impossibility if we accept two knowledges. The answer likely continues to argue that the objectors' premise that they could be known by one knowledge is not necessary. The author leaves the response truncated, but the direction of the refutation is that the objection confuses the possibility of two knowledges with the necessity of one knowledge.

Key Terms
القاضي
إمام الحرمين
انفكاك العلم
العلم بهذا نفس العلم بذاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection proves one knowledge cannot relate to separable knowns at all.
Correction: The objection only shows that if two knowns are separable, they cannot be known by a single knowledge; but they can be known by two knowledges, so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn's objection?
A) That knowledge of separable knowns must be identical.
B) That if knowns can be known separately, one knowledge would make a thing separate from itself.
C) That all knowns are inseparable.
D) That two knowledges are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objection of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn shows that separable knowns cannot be known by a single knowledge without absurdity, but the incomplete answer indicates that two knowledges resolve the issue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that if knowledge of two things can be separated, they cannot be truly united in one knowledge. In your own life, do you ever claim to know something (e.g., that you love someone) while your actions show separation from that knowledge (e.g., you treat them poorly)? How can you ensure your knowledge is integrated into your actions?
?
The answer in the passage is incomplete—it ends abruptly. Are you comfortable with not having all the answers in your faith? Where do you demand certainty that Allah has not granted?
?
The objection relies on the identity of knowledge: if one knowledge covers two objects, then knowledge of one is identical to knowledge of the other. Can you think of a time when you assumed that understanding one aspect of a person gave you full knowledge of them, only to be proven wrong? How does this lesson warn against such intellectual pride?

Two Knowledges: Classification by Object and Locus
Classification of two knowledges by object and locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two knowledges are classified based on whether their objects and loci are the same or different, and the permissibility of two knowledges having the same object.

Core Idea
Two knowledges are classified by their objects and loci, not by time in the first instance.

In this passage, al-Sanusi begins by outlining the fundamental classification of two knowledges. He explains that when the objects differ, the knowledges are different; when the objects are the same, the knowledges may be similar. He also introduces the role of the locus (the knower) and discusses the permissibility of two knowledges relating to one object.

Guided Path
1
Read the General Rule
Read from the beginning until 'وهل هما متماثلان هو فيه خلاف' to grasp the initial classification and the conditional about time.
Notice the qualification 'وقيل إن اتحد وقته وإلا اختلفا'—time appears early.
2
Understand the Detail on Locus and Object
Read the detailed breakdown from 'وتفصيل ذلك' to the end of the selected passage. Pay attention to the concepts of 'محل' and 'معلوم'.
The author explains the case of multiple loci and multiple objects separately.
3
Analyze the Case of Multiple Objects
Focus on 'وإذا تعدد متعلقهما... إذ لو كانا مثلين لم يجتمعا في محل واحد'. Why are the knowledges different even if the objects are similar?
The key point is that similarity of objects does not imply similarity of knowledges.
4
Reflect on Permissibility of Two Knowledges on One Object
Note the statement 'لاخفاء في جواز تعلق العلمين بمعلوم واحد'. The dispute is only about their similarity.
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم عند التعدد فالعلمان المتعلقان بمعلومين مختلفان وإن تماثل المعلومان وبمعلوم واحد متماثلان . وقيل إن اتحد وقته وإلا اختلفا ضرورة اختلاف المعلوم باختلاف الوقت. لاخفاء في جواز تعلق العلمين بمعلوم واحد. وهل هما متماثلان هو فيه خلاف. وتفصيل ذلك : أن للعلم محلا هو : العالم، ومتعلقاً المعلوم. فإذا تعدد المحل كعلم زيد وعمرو بأن الصانع قديم مختلفان إن جعلنا اختصاص كل منهما بمحله لذاته وإلا فمثلان. وإذا تعدد متعلقهما فالعلمان مختلفان سواء كان المعلومان متماثلين كالعلم ببياضين أو مختلفين كالعلم بالسواد والبياض، إذ لو كانا مثلين لم يجتمعا في محل واحد. فإذا اتحد متعلقهما فالجمهور على أنهما مثلان سواء اتحد وقت المعلوم أو اختلف . أما عند الاتحاد فظاهر ، وأما عند الاختلاف فلأن اختلاف الوقت لا يؤثر في اختلاف العلمين كما لا يؤثر اختلاف الوقت وتقدمه وتأخره في اختلاف الجوهرين .. واعترض الآمدى بأن الفرق ظاهر فإن الوقت هاهنا داخل في متعلق العلم كالعلم بقيام زيد الآن وقيامه غداً، ولاخفاء في اختلاف الكل باختلاف [؟]
He said: Then, when there is multiplicity, the two knowledges related to two objects are different even if the two objects are similar, and related to one object are similar. And it was said if their time is one, otherwise they differ necessarily due to the difference of the object with difference of time. There is no obscurity in the permissibility of two knowledges relating to one object. Whether they are similar is a matter of dispute. And the detail of that is: Knowledge has a locus, which is the knower, and an object, the known. So if the locus is multiple, like the knowledge of Zayd and 'Amr that the Creator is eternal, they are different if we consider the distinctiveness of each to its locus as essential, otherwise they are similar. And if their objects are multiple, then the two knowledges are different, whether the two objects are similar, like knowledge of two whitenesses, or different, like knowledge of blackness and whiteness, because if they were similar they would not coexist in a single locus. So if their object is one, the majority hold that they are similar whether the time of the object is one or different. As for when the time is one, it is obvious; as for when it differs, because difference of time does not affect difference of the two knowledges, just as difference of time, its precedence and posteriority, does not affect difference of two substances. And al-Amidi objected that the difference is apparent, for time here enters into the object of knowledge, like knowledge of Zayd's standing now and his standing tomorrow, and there is no obscurity in the difference of the whole with difference of [?].
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage classifies the relationship between two knowledges based on their objects and loci. It begins by stating a general rule: two knowledges related to two objects are different (even if the objects are similar), while two knowledges related to one object are similar. A qualification is added: if the time of the object is the same, they are similar; if time differs, they differ because the object itself differs with time. The permissibility of two knowledges relating to one object is affirmed, but whether they are similar is disputed. The author then provides a detailed breakdown: knowledge has a locus (the knower) and an object (the known). If the locus differs (e.g., Zayd and 'Amr both knowing that God is eternal), the knowledges are different if the distinctiveness to each locus is essential; otherwise they are similar. If the objects differ, the knowledges are always different, regardless of whether the objects are similar (like two instances of whiteness) or different (black and white), because if they were similar they could not coexist in one locus. If the object is one, the majority say the knowledges are similar, irrespective of time difference, because time does not affect the essence of knowledge. Al-Amidi objects that time is part of the object (e.g., knowing Zayd's standing now vs. tomorrow yields different objects), so the knowledges should differ when time differs. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the objection is clear.

Key Terms
علمان
معلوم
محل
تماثل
وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two knowledges cannot share the same object.
Correction: The passage explicitly states there is no obscurity in the permissibility of two knowledges relating to one object; the dispute is only about their similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on two knowledges related to two objects that are similar (e.g., knowledge of two instances of whiteness)?
A) They are similar
B) They are different
C) It depends on time
D) It depends on the knower

Key Takeaway
The classification of two knowledges depends primarily on their objects and loci, not on the similarity of the objects themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God is One. Another believer also knows this. According to this lesson, is your knowledge essentially the same as theirs or distinct? What determines that in your own heart? Are you willing to let go of the pride of having 'your own' knowledge and see it as a shared truth?
?
When you learn a new ruling from the Quran, do you treat it as a universal truth or as something attached to your own understanding? How does the distinction between locus and object challenge you to seek knowledge from others who may have a different locus but the same object?

One Object: Are Two Knowledges Similar? The Role of Time
Dispute on similarity of two knowledges with same object and differing time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that two knowledges on one object are similar regardless of time, and al-Amidi's objection that time affects the object.

Core Idea
Time does not change the essence of knowledge, but it can change the object, leading to a dispute.

This lesson continues the classification by examining the case where two knowledges have the same object. The majority hold that they are similar even if the time of the object differs, while al-Amidi argues that time is part of the object, so knowledge of the same object at different times is different.

Guided Path
1
Read the Majority View
Read from 'فإذا اتحد متعلقهما' to 'اختلاف الجوهرين' to understand the majority position and their justification.
Note the analogy with substances: time does not affect the essence.
2
Understand al-Amidi's Objection
Read the objection starting with 'واعترض الآمدى'. Why does he think time is part of the object?
The example: knowing Zayd's standing now vs. tomorrow—the objects differ.
3
Compare the Two Positions
Consider the implications: does time change the knowledge itself or only the object?
The majority sees time as external to the knowledge; al-Amidi sees it as part of the object.

Classification of Two Knowledges by Object and Subject
Classification of two knowledges by subject and object with majority view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two instances of knowledge are classified as similar or different based on their object (معلوم) and subject (محل), and why the majority holds that two knowledges of the same object are similar even if the time differs.

Core Idea
Two knowledges of the same object are similar; two knowledges of different objects are different.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a subtle issue in epistemology: when do two instances of knowledge count as the same or different? The author distinguishes between the locus of knowledge (the knower) and the object of knowledge (the known). He then presents the rule: if the objects differ, the knowledges differ; if the object is the same, the knowledges are considered similar according to the majority, even if the time differs. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms and the logical flow.
Pay attention to the words 'محل' and 'متعلق'.
2
Identify the two key distinctions
The passage first presents a general rule, then details by locus (subject) and object. Identify the cases where knowledges are different and where they are similar.
The rule for multiple objects is straightforward; the locus case involves an additional condition.
3
Examine the majority view on a single object
Focus on the statement 'الجمهور على أنهما مثلان سواء اتحد وقت المعلوم أو اختلف'. Why does the majority ignore time? The analogy with substances is crucial.
The author argues that time does not affect the similarity of two knowledges of the same object.
4
Consider the alternative opinion
The passage mentions an alternative view that considers time: if the time is one they are similar, otherwise different. Contrast this with the majority view.
This shows that the issue was debated.
5
Apply the concept to examples
Think of two people knowing that God is eternal – their knowledges are similar. Now imagine one person knowing whiteness today and another knowing whiteness tomorrow – according to the majority, those knowledges are also similar.
The key is that the object (whiteness) is the same universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم عند التعدد فالعلمان المتعلقان بمعلومين مختلفان وإن تماثل المعلومان وبمعلوم واحد متماثلان . وقيل إن اتحد وقته وإلا اختلفا ضرورة اختلاف المعلوم باختلاف الوقت). لا خفاء في جواز تعلق العلمين بمعلوم واحد. وهل هما متماثلان فيه خلاف. وتفصيل ذلك : أن للعلم محلا هو : العالم، ومتعلقاً المعلوم. فإذا تعدد المحل كعلم زيد وعمرو بأن الصانع قديم فالعلمان مختلفان ( إن جعلنا اختصاص كل منهما بمحله لذاته وإلا فمثلان). وإذا تعدد متعلقهما فالعلمان مختلفان سواء كان المعلومان متماثلين كالعلم ببياضين أو مختلفين كالعلم بالسواد والبياض، إذ لو كانا مثلين لم يجتمعا في محل واحد. فإذا اتحد متعلقهما فالجمهور على أنهما مثلان سواء اتحد وقت المعلوم أو اختلف . أما عند الاتحاد فظاهر ، وأما عند الاختلاف فلأن اختلاف الوقت لا يؤثر في اختلاف العلمين كما لا يؤثر اختلاف الوقت وتقدمه وتأخره في اختلاف الجوهرين.
He said: Then when there are two (knowledges), the two knowledges related to two objects are different even if the two objects are similar, and related to one object they are similar. And it was said: if its time is one, (they are similar) otherwise they differ due to the necessity of the object differing with difference of time. There is no concealment of the permissibility of two knowledges being attached to one object. And whether they are similar there is a dispute. And the detail of that is: knowledge has a locus which is the knower, and an object which is the known. So if the locus is multiple, like the knowledge of Zayd and Amr that the Maker is eternal, the two knowledges are different (if we make the specification of each to its locus essential, otherwise they are similar). And if their object is multiple, the two knowledges are different whether the two objects are similar like knowledge of two whitenesses or different like knowledge of blackness and whiteness, because if they were similar they would not coexist in one locus. So if their object is one, the majority holds that they are similar whether the time of the object is one or different. As for when it is one, it is obvious; as for when it is different, because difference of time does not affect the difference of the two knowledges just as difference of time and its precedence and delay do not affect the difference of two substances.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage classifies the relationship between two instances of knowledge (علمين) based on their object (معلوم) and subject (محل). The author begins by stating the general rule: two knowledges related to two different objects are different even if the objects are similar (e.g., two whitenesses); two knowledges related to one object are similar. He then notes an alternative opinion that considers the time of the object: if the time is one they are similar, otherwise they differ because the object differs with time. Next, he clarifies that it is permissible for two knowledges to relate to one object, but there is disagreement whether they are exactly similar (متماثلان). The detailed explanation follows: knowledge has a locus (المحل) which is the knower (العالم) and an object (المتعلق) which is the known (المعلوم). If the locus is multiple (e.g., Zayd's knowledge and Amr's knowledge of God's eternity), the two knowledges are different if we consider their association with their locus as essential; otherwise they are similar. If the object is multiple, the knowledges are always different, whether the objects are similar or different, because similar knowledges cannot coexist in one locus (i.e., they cannot be found together in the same mind as distinct). If the object is one, the majority (الجمهور) holds that the two knowledges are similar, regardless of whether the time of the object is the same or different. The case of same time is obvious. For different times, they argue that difference in time does not cause difference in the knowledges, just as time does not affect the identity of two substances (جوهرين) over different times.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
محل
مثل
مختلف
وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two knowledges of the same object must be different if the object's time differs.
Correction: The majority holds that time does not affect the similarity; two knowledges of the same object remain similar even if the time differs, just as a substance remains the same across time.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, if two knowledges have the same object but at different times, they are:
A) Different because time is part of the object
B) Similar because time does not affect the knowledges
C) Different because the knowledges are in different subjects
D) Similar only if the times are the same

Key Takeaway
The majority view is that two knowledges of the same object are similar, regardless of time, because time does not affect the essence of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge that Allah is Eternal—is that exact same knowledge present in the heart of every believer? If not, what does that mean about the unity of faith? Reflect on a time you felt your knowledge of a truth was unique, and whether that made you prideful or isolated you from others.
?
You are pursuing knowledge of Islam. Does the passing of time make your knowledge of a truth—like the oneness of Allah—different now than when you first learned it? If not, how should that certainty shape your actions today, especially when doubts arise?
?
The passage says two knowledges of the same object are similar regardless of time. If your knowledge of a sin’s harm is the same as a scholar’s, why do you still commit it? What is missing in your knowledge that leads to action?

Al-Amidi's Objection on Time as Part of the Object
Al-Amidi objects that time is part of the object; the author clarifies the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's objection that time is part of the object of knowledge, and the author's clarification that when the object's time differs, the object itself becomes multiple.

Core Idea
Time can be part of the object, making the object multiple when time changes.

This passage presents a nuanced objection by al-Amidi to the majority view on similarity of knowledges. He argues that when time is part of the object, different times make the objects different, so the knowledges are not similar. The author then clarifies the correct view, distinguishing cases where time is external versus internal to the object. Let's explore this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the objection and the author's response.
2
Identify al-Amidi's objection
Find how al-Amidi argues that time is included in the object. Consider his example of Zayd's standing now versus tomorrow.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author distinguishes between the object being multiple when time is part of it and the object being single when time is external. Grasp this key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض الآمدى بأن الفرق ظاهر فإن الوقت هاهنا داخل في متعلق العلم كالعلم بقيام زيد الآن وقيامه غداً، ولا خفاء في اختلاف الكل باختلاف الجزء بخلاف كون الجوهر في زمانين فإنه خارج عنه، وإنما نظير ذلك العلم بالشيء في وقتين لا العلم بمعلوم مقيد بوقتين، هذا والحق أن المعلوم إذا اختلف وقته كان متعدداً لا متحداً وأن اتحاده مع تعدد العلم إنما يتصور عند اختلاف وقت العلم، والظاهر أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو عند اختلاف محله وقد سبق الكلام في أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو مختلفان.
Al-Amidi objected that the difference is apparent, for time here is included in the object of knowledge, like knowledge of Zayd's standing now and his standing tomorrow, and there is no hiddenness in the difference of the whole by difference of the part, unlike the case of a substance being in two times, for it is outside it; rather the analogy for that is knowledge of a thing in two times, not knowledge of an object restricted by two times. This, and the truth is that when the time of the object differs, it is multiple not single, and its unity with multiplicity of knowledge is only conceivable when the time of the knowledge differs, and the apparent is that they are then similar, or when its locus differs, and there has preceded speech about whether they are then similar or different.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Amidi and the author's response. Al-Amidi objects to the majority view that two knowledges with the same object at different times are similar. He argues that the difference is clear because time here is part of the object of knowledge (متعلق العلم). For example, knowledge of 'Zayd standing now' and knowledge of 'Zayd standing tomorrow' have different objects because the time is part of the known state. Thus, the whole (the knowledge) differs when a part (time) differs. This is unlike the case of a substance (جوهر) existing in two times, where time is external to the substance. The proper analogy would be knowledge of a thing in two times (without time being part of the object) not knowledge of an object restricted by two times. The author then gives the correct view (الحق): when the time of the object differs, the object is multiple, not one. So the earlier case where the object is one is only when the time of the object is the same. Unity of the object with multiple knowledges is conceivable only when the time of the knowledge differs (i.e., two knowledges occur at different times of the same object) or when the subject (محل) differs. The author notes that the case of different subjects was discussed earlier, and it was debated whether the knowledges are similar or different.

Key Terms
اعتراض
الآمدى
وقت
متعلق
جزء
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that time difference always makes two knowledges different even when the object is the same.
Correction: The author clarifies that if time is part of the object, the object becomes multiple, so the knowledges are of different objects. But if time is external (as in a substance), the object can remain single.

Checkpoint
What is al-Amidi's main objection to the majority view?
A) Two knowledges cannot have the same object
B) Time is part of the object, so different times make the objects different
C) Majority view is based on a misunderstanding of substances
D) Objects cannot be restricted by time

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's objection shows that when time is part of the object, differing times make the object multiple, so the majority view stands only when time is not part of the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about a past sin and your current memory of it, do you treat the object (the sin) as the same or different? According to this lesson, the time difference may make the object multiple. How does this affect your understanding of repentance and God's forgiveness—does it challenge you to consider each instance of sin as a distinct object of confession?
?
In your daily arguments with others, how often do you assume you are talking about the same 'object' when time or context has actually changed? Can you recall a specific disagreement that might have been resolved by recognizing that time was part of the object? What will you do differently next time you sense such confusion?
?
This passage distinguishes between time as part of the object versus external. How does this distinction impact your belief about God's eternal knowledge? If God knows all temporal events in a single eternal act, does that mean time is external to His knowledge? Does that raise any doubt in your heart about the reality of your free choices? What step of faith do you need to take to trust that God's knowledge does not negate your responsibility?

The Known Object and Its Time: Why Change Means Multiplicity
The author argues that a known object whose time differs is multiple, not one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a known object must be considered multiple if its time differs, according to the author's argument.

Core Idea
A known object is tied to its time; change the time, and you change the object.

The author grapples with a subtle problem: can the same thing be known at two different times and still be one? Common sense might say yes, but philosophical rigor demands a closer look. This passage dismantles that intuition by distinguishing between the external existence of a substance and our knowledge of it, and argues that the known object itself becomes multiple when its time differs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'existence in two times' and 'knowledge in two times'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how the author distinguishes between the substance existing in two times and knowledge of a thing in two times. The part is unlike the substance's existence.
This sets the stage for the argument.
3
Locate the core thesis
Find the sentence stating: 'if the time of the known object differs, it becomes multiple, not one.' This is the central claim.
Identify where this sentence begins.
4
Consider the implications for multiple instances of knowledge
Understand that oneness of the object with multiple instances of knowledge is only possible if the times of knowledge differ. This clarifies the relationship between knowledge and its object.
The author discusses when two instances of knowledge are similar or different.
5
Connect to the previous discussion
Note the reference to an earlier discussion about similarity vs. difference of two instances of knowledge. This passage builds on that foundation.
This shows continuity in the author's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزء بخلاف كون الجوهر في زمانين فإنه خارج عنه، وإنما نظير ذلك العلم بالشيء في وقتين لا العلم بمعلوم مقيد بوقتين، هذا والحق أن المعلوم إذا اختلف وقته كان متعدداً لا متحداً وأن اتحاده مع تعدد العلم إنما يتصور عند اختلاف وقت العلم، والظاهر أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو عند اختلاف محله وقد سبق الكلام في أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو مختلفان.
The part is unlike the existence of the substance in two times, for it is outside of it. Rather, the analogy for that is knowledge of a thing in two times, not knowledge of a known object restricted to two times. This, and the truth is that if the time of the known object differs, it becomes multiple, not one, and that its oneness with multiplicity of knowledge is only conceivable when the time of knowledge differs. And the apparent is that they (i.e., the two instances of knowledge) are then either similar or [different] due to differing locus, and the discussion has preceded regarding whether they are then similar or different.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the relationship between the known object (al-ma'lum) and time. The author distinguishes between the external existence of a substance in two times and the case of knowledge. He argues that if the time of the known object changes, the object itself becomes multiple, not one. The oneness of an object with multiple instances of knowledge is only conceivable when the instants of knowledge themselves differ, not the object's time. The apparent conclusion is that two instances of knowledge of the same object at different times are either similar or different, depending on the locus (mahall) of knowledge. The author refers to a previous discussion on whether they are similar or different.

Misconception: One might think that the same object can be known identically at different times without change.
Correction: The author argues that differing time makes the known object multiple, not one, because time is integral to the object's identity.

Checkpoint

B) When the time of the known object differs

D) Never, the object is always one

Key Takeaway
The time of the known object is part of its identity; differing times entail distinct objects.
