Defining Qudra: Lexical and Theological Dimensions
Footnote defining qudra and presenting three theological positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical meaning of qudra, its distinction from quwwa, and the differing theological views on human power.

Core Idea
Qudra is power for rational beings; theologians disagree on its scope in humans.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid unpacks the term 'qudra' (power) in both language and theology. It clarifies the nuance between qudra and the broader quwwa, then surveys three key Islamic theological positions: the extreme denial by Jahm ibn Safwan, the Mu'tazilite affirmation of equal capacity for action and omission, and al-Razi's redefinition of qudra as mere force. Understanding these distinctions is essential for grasping debates on divine omnipotence and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Pay attention to the technical terms and how they are defined.
The passage has a clear structure: definition, distinction, technical term, then three views.
2
Identify the lexical distinction between qudra and quwwa
Notice that quwwa applies to both rational and non-rational beings, while qudra is restricted to rational beings. This is a crucial semantic difference.
Examples given: power of current (quwwa) vs. power of educator (qudra).
3
Survey the three theological positions
Read the views of Jahm, the Mu'tazila, and al-Razi. Each represents a different stance on human capability. Reflect on how these views relate to the core idea of human agency.
Jahm denies any human qudra; Mu'tazila affirm it as a quality enabling opposites; al-Razi reduces it to mere force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة هي القوة على الشيء، وهي مرادفة للاستطاعة والفرق بينها وبين القوة أن القوة تضاف إلى العاقل وغير العاقل فتكون طبيعية وعقلية كما في قولنا قوة التيار ووقوة الجسم وقوة الخيال على حين أن القدرة لا تضاف إلا إلى الكائنات العاقلة كما في قولنا قدرة المربي، وقدرة الحاكم وقدرة [؟] الإرادة والقدرة في الاصطلاح صفة الإرادة. وقد نفى جهم ابن صفوان كل قدرة عن الانسان وقال: لا قدرة له أصلاً وهذا غلو في الجبر. أما المعتزلة فيقررون وجود القدرة ويقولون : إنها صفة يتأتى منها الفعل بدلاً من الترك، والترك بدلاً من الفعل. وأما الرازي فإنه يطلق القدرة على مجرد القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال الحيوانية المختلفة، أو على القوة الجامعة لشرائط التأثير.
Al-qudra is the power (quwwa) over a thing, and it is synonymous with al-istita'a. The difference between it and al-quwwa is that al-quwwa is attributed to both rational and non-rational beings, thus it is natural and rational as in our saying 'the power of the current', 'the power of the body', and 'the power of imagination', whereas al-qudra is not attributed except to rational beings as in our saying 'the power (qudra) of the educator', 'the power (qudra) of the ruler', and 'the power (qudra) of will'. And al-qudra in terminology is a description (sifa) of will. Jahm ibn Safwan denied all qudra from man and said: he has no qudra at all – this is extremism in jabr. As for the Mu'tazila, they affirm the existence of qudra and say: it is a description from which action proceeds instead of omission, and omission instead of action. As for al-Razi, he applies al-qudra to mere al-quwwa which is the principle of different animal actions, or to the al-quwwa that combines the conditions of effect.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological analysis of the term 'qudra'. It begins by defining qudra as power over something, synonymous with istita'a. It distinguishes qudra from quwwa: quwwa can be attributed to both rational and non-rational beings (e.g., current, body, imagination), while qudra is only for rational beings (e.g., educator, ruler, will). In technical terminology, qudra is described as a property of will. The passage then surveys three theological positions: Jahm ibn Safwan denies human qudra entirely, labeling this as extremist jabr (compulsion). The Mu'tazila affirm qudra as a quality enabling both action and its opposite (omission). Al-Razi applies qudra either to the mere force that generates diverse animal acts or to force fulfilling the conditions for causation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qudra is often conflated with quwwa; many think they are identical.
Correction: Qudra is specifically for rational beings, while quwwa is broader, covering both rational and non-rational entities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group denies human qudra entirely?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) Jahm ibn Safwan
C) Al-Razi
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Qudra in Islamic theology is a power specific to rational beings, and scholars differ on its scope in humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are about to commit a sin, do you convince yourself that you have the power to stop, or do you feel helpless? How does the Mu'tazila's view that qudra enables both action and omission challenge your excuse for sinning? This week, catch yourself in a moment of disobedience and consciously say: 'I have qudra from Allah to choose His pleasure.'
?
If you truly believed that your every action—even a good deed—is only a gift of qudra from Allah, how would your pride after worship diminish? What would change in your daily salah if you realized that your ability to stand, bow, and prostrate is entirely dependent on His granting of qudra? Test this tomorrow by thanking Allah specifically for the power to worship Him.
